NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
APPRAISAL OF GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG) has requested Bank
assistance to finance an agricultural development project in the North Western
State of the Federation. The project was identified by a Bank mission in
November 1972, and was subsequently prepared by the Bank in collaboration
with the North Western State Authorities. This report is based on the find-
ings of an appraisal mission that visited Nigeria in S"ptember and October
1973.
ii.       Under the present economic circumstances and associated industrial
growth, industry and the urban population are becoming more dependent on the
rural population for assured food and industrial crop supplies, and at the
same time the gap in income earnings between urban and rural communities is
becoming ever wider. The northern States of Nigeria and their rural popula-
tions are the main producers of grain crops for human consumption and of
industrial crops for processing industries, and yet their per capita income
of about US$40 per annum is the lowest in the Federation.
iii.      Except for the extreme northern areas, mainly north of 120 latitude,
the soils and climate of the northern States are generally suitable for ex-
tensive annual crop production. Research workers and the more responsive
farmers using improved inputs have adequately shown the response of improved
seeds to economic applications of fertilizers, and the viability of insecti-
cides for pest control purposes. Due to the disruptions caused by the civil
war (1966-68) and its aftermath, the lack of planning resources, and until
recently considerable financial constraints, very little had been done to
improve the production of the small farmer. Even now experienced manpower
to plan and implement rational developments in agriculture is in extremely
short supply. The Federal and State Governments recognizing the need to
increase the production from the small farm sector, 70%_of_thepopulation,
now plan to accelerate the development of agriculture throughout Nigeria.
This project refers to one of three projects in northern Nigeria that the
Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG) would develop with the Bank's
assistance.
iv.       The project area (3,800 km 2) is centerd around Gusau in part of
the Sokoto Division of the North Western State. The area supports a popula-
tion of about 620,000 people (9% of the State's population) including some
68,000 families farming on average 3.5 ha each. The net annual family income
including the value of produce consumed on the farm is currently about US$266.



- ii -
The population is expanding, the soils are rapidly declining in fertility,
and the people are under constant pressure to increase output. As such,
conditions are ripe for project action and farmer response is expected to be
high.
v.        The project would cover physical infrastructure, support services
and farm inputs; including 1,000 km of low cost agricultural roads; 85 earth
dams and 160 ponds to provide water supplies to humans and livestock; a
project administrative center at Gusau, 4 development unit centers and 40
farm service centers; develop two seed multiplication farms to produce im-
proved high yielding seed; and would expand and create new training facili-
ties for project staff and farmers. The project would also provide better
support services in the form of a tenfold increase in extension staff, in-
troduce new credit and marketing services to the farmers; and the associated
staff houses, offices and transport facilities required to support these
services. The project would procure farm inputs to sell to farmers for cash
or credit. An evaluation unit would be established to evaluate the opera-
tions and benefits of the proposed investments.
vi.       The project would be managed by a special Management Unit respon-
sible to an Executive Committee, both to be established by a gazetted order
of the North Western State Executive Council. The committee would be chaired
by the Permanent Secretary of the State's Ministry of Agriculture. The Manage-
ment Unit would be suitably staffed through transfer of existing Government
staff or personnel presently under training. Deficiencies in specialized
technical skills and high level management staff, unavailable in Nigeria,
would be made up through international recruitment.
vii.      The proposed Bank loan of US$19 million would be made to the Federal
Government of Nigeria for a term of 20 years, including a five-year grace
period. The loan would be 51% of total project costs, estimated at US$37.4
million, and would cover all the foreign costs estimated at US$17.7 million
and 7% of local costs equivalent to US$1.3 million. The US$19 million Bank
loan would be onlent to the North Western State Government (NWS). NWS would
provide US$17.0 million or 45% of total project costs, and the remaining
project costs estimated at US$1.4 million, or 4%, would be contributed by
participating farmers in the form of cash payments for seasonal inputs and
down payments on farm equipment.
viii.     Procurement of vehicles, plant equipment, fertilizers, insecticides,
farm tractors and implements, having a value of more than US$25,000 would be
through international competitive bidding (ICB), and would have an estimated
value of US$11.4 million. Contracts for civil works such as houses, offices
and warehouses valued at US$5.4 million would not be suitable for ICB, due to
the dispersed locations and small size of individual contracts, and would be
awarded under locally advertised competitive bidding. Foreign firms and
suppliers who are well represented in Nigeria would not be precluded, but
bonafide domestic contractors would receive a 7-1/2% preference in bid evalua-
tion. Construction of roads, water supplies and soil conservation works esti-
mated at US$6.7 million would be carried out on force account due to the shortage



- iii -
of suitable medium-scale contractors who normally would undertake this work,
however, petty contractors would be employed to the maximum extent feasible.
A large part of project costs, an estimated US$7.6 million, would be for labor,
staff salaries and allowances, vehicle maintenance and operation, general ser-
vices and project evaluation, and as such would be unsuitable for competitive
bidding. Internationally recruited staff would be employed under terms and
conditions acceptable to the Bank.
ix.       Proceeds of the Bank loan would be disbursed to cover:  (a) 100%
Or the CIF costs of directly imported vehicles, tractors, heavy plant and
equipment, or 75% of the cost of these items if locally procured - US$2.0
million; (b) 100% of the CIF costs of directly imported farm tractors and
equipment, ox drawn equipment, spraying machines, fertilizers, insecticides,
battery sets and seeds, or 71% of these items if procured locally - US$3.5
million; (c) 100% of the cost of expatriate staff - US$1.6 million; (d) 60%
of the cost of buildings, houses, furnishings, and construction materials
and labor for roads, dams and soil conservation works - US$3.2 million; (e)
60% of local staff costs - US$2.8 million; (f) 60% of the cost of the field
evaluation unit - US$0.5 million; and (g) US$5.4 million to cover unallocated
expenses. Disbursements of the Bank loan would be against import documen-
tation, contracts and certified records of expenditure, the latter would be
scrutinized from time to time by the Bank during supervision missions.
x.        The principal direct benefits would, through the output of 41,000
project farmers, be from project year 5 the annual incremental production
of 14,000 tons of seed cotton (64,000 bales of lint), 9,000 tons of groundnuts,
11,000 tons of sorghum, 21,000 tons of maize, and 1,500 tons of cowpeas; the
project would indirectly benefit a further 27,000 farm families through pro-
vision of better communications, water supplies and markets. Including the
non-farming population, some 620,000 people would one way or other receive
some benefit from the project, not Ieast the project would provide additional
employment - equivalent to 8,000 manyears per annum. Intangible benefits
would include the provision of a basic infrastructure on which health and
other social services could be developed, expansion of local trade to the
non-farming population, and the strengthening of institutions including the
Local Authority, and the State Ministry of Agriculture. The project establisheci
evaluation unit would provide considerable information to support the future-
planninL!p_ Federal and State agricultural Rolicies.  The project would train
Nigerian staff-in-maniigement and technical aspects applicable to rural devel-
opment.  The economic rate of return is 16% a 10% reduction in commodity
prices would reduce the rate of return to 9% and the project would still be
viable (9% rate of return) if production targets were reduced to 70% of those
projected. The net fa-rm income for farmers directly affected by the project
would increase from YS$266\ to US$360 per annum at full development, and the -
net per capita income 6E the total farming population would rise from US$41 /
to US$55 inferring an annual income growth rate of 6% per annum, compared
to the present no growth situation, and is achievable provided that the
physical inputs and management needs are provided.
xii.      On the basis of the assurances that were obtained during negotiations,
and set out in Chapter IX, the project is suitable for a Bank loan of US$19
million.






I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      This report appraises a loan application made to the Bank by the
Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG). The purpose of the loan would
be to assist Nigeria's North Western State Government (NWS) to finance an agri-
cultural development program involving 68,000 small farmers in the Sokoto
Administrative Division. The project would involve the construction of vital
cro- extraction roads and water supplies, and would provide farmers with the
technical, marketing, and credit services, and inputs needed to insure sig-
nificant increases in production and farmer incomes. Currently, the latter
are low, about US$266 per family, or US$41 per capita.
1.02      The project is deliberately large; this is essential in order to
make a meaningful impact in such a large and populous country. On the other
hand, the risks that do exist are minimized, provided competent management is
available because the technical innovations proposed have been proven, the
farmers are hardworking, and the production induced by the project is in high
demand on domestic and export markets. The project concept could be applied
throughout most of northern Nigeria. One important role of the project would
provide a practical training ground for Nigerian staff in the technical and
administrative management of projects of this type.
1.03      The project is within the frame of policies outlined in Nigeria's
"Perspective Plan for Agricultural Development to 1985." In particular, it
follows FMG policies to increase production of both food and industrial crops
to supply Nigeria's rapidly increasing urban populations and industries in-
cluding intensive livestock production enterprises, particularly in the
southern States. The project would assist to develop sound agricultural
credit systems tailored to provide credit to large numbers of small farmers.
It would provide also a much needed base for the expansion and improvement
of health and other social 'services currently planned by the State Government;
the project road development program would be a vital first phase to facili-
tate the improvement of these social services.
1.04      This report refers to similar projects at Funtua (NC State) and
Gombe (NE State). These projects were appraised concurrently and are similar
in nature; while administratively totally separate, they have common production
policies, techniques and services. The three projects represent a first, but
very substantial, step by the Federal and State Governments to increase the
production and raise the living standards of large numbers of peoples in the
relatively less developed and environmentally harsh northern States.



-2-
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      The 12-State Federal Republic of Nigeria has a total area of about
914,000 sq km, and in 1971, a population estimated at 68 million. About 35%
of GDP estimated at US$9.1 billion in 1972/73, is generated in the agriculture
sector! which employs 70% of the labor force. In 1972/73, total domestic
exports amounted to US$2.1 billion, of which petroleum represented about 82%
(US$1.8 billion). The main agricultural exports are cocoa, groundnuts, and
groundnut oil and cake. Oil palm products used to be exported, but with in-
creasing domestic consumption and a general decline in the industry, palm
oil is no longer exported, and palm kernel exports are dwindling. Cotton,
once a major export, is now fully utilized by the local milling industry and
local supply cannot meet demand. By and large, Nigeria is self-sufficient
in food: food imports are estimated at US$2.6 per capita.
2.02      Nigeria can be divided into three major agricultural and ecological
zones (see map): a southern wet forest zone, south of the confluence of the
Benue and Niger Rivers, mainly growing perennial tree crops, including cocoa,
oil palm and rubber; a central dry forest zone known as the middle belt which
is underpopulated and tsetse infested, but has a high potential for annual
food crops and livestock; and a northern zone, covering about 50% of the
country. with a tree savannah ecology, and which supports Nigeria's cattle
population, and produces its main cereal crops, and groundnuts and cotton.
B. Agriculture in Northern Nigeria
2.03      North Western State is one of four States in northern Nigeria which
have very similar agricultural and ecological systems. The four have also
a closely associated history of institutional development and agricultural
policies, as prior to 1968, the whole region, including two other States, was
administered as one from a regional capital at Kaduna. Thus, a description
of the North Western State, its policies and problems, would apply closely
to the situation in North Central, North Eastern and Kano States.
2.04      North Western State, with a population of about 7.5 million, lies
between latitudes 8-14° north and 3-1/2 - 7-1/2° east, and comprises about
166,000 sq km. Within the State there is considerable climatic variation,
and rainfall varies from 1,250 mm in its south to less than 750 mm on the
border with Niger. Much of the State has an assured monomodal rainfall (4-5
months) of at least 750 mm per annum; although the distribution within the
total is sometimes erratic, leading to "drought" years such as occurred in
1973. Most of the State has a tree savannah ecology, and in those areas of
higher tree densities, the incidence of tsetse is high and trypanosomiasis is
a constraint to livestock production, as well as being a human health problem.



- 3 -
The proposed project which would be centered on Gusau covers about 2% of the
State and includes 8% of the population.
2.05      The major crops are annuals such as millet and sorghum, the prin-
cipal cereals, and groundnuts, cotton, and cowpeas. Minor crops include maize,
tomatoes, chilies, cassava and yams. Livestock plays an important role in
the economy and include cattle, sneep, goats and poultry. The Fulani people,
who are nomadically inclined, owns most of the livestock, and have a consider-
able impact on the arable farmer. Traditionally, cotton and groundnuts have
becn the north's main cash crops and are likely to remain so, but as the
savannah areas of Nigeria are generally well suited for cereal production,
they could play an important role in the future in producing cereals for the
expanding market created by intensive livestock industries and urban popula-
tions of the south.
C.  Agricultural Poliua
2.06      Agricultural production in the State is virtually stagnant, and
any increase that is occurring is due to population growth. Although signi-
ficant improvements in crop yields have been demonstrated to be technically
and economically feasible by the research institutions, very little has been
done to help the farmer to obtain and use the innovations necessary to improve
his production. Until very recently, NWS had no clear-cut policy for increas-
ing production, and, in the absence of specific plans to implement the policy,
the Ministry of Agriculture (MA) still tends to function on a basis of main-
taining the status quo. The extension service is weak and poorly staffed,
credit and marketing institutions barely exist, planning is poor, and the
expertise needed to implement successfully major agricultural and rural devel-
opment projects is in very short supply.
2.07      Nigeria's manufacturing industries that are based on farm products
such as cotton and groundnuts, and the rapidly expanding urban population
(estimated to be increasing at 5% annually) are totally dependent on the
small traditional farmer for their raw'materials and food supplies. These
farmers, as throughout Nigeria in general, are very poorly serviced, do not
use improved and drought resistant varieties and have very limited access to
fertilizers and agro-chemicals. Production is, therefore, irregular and is
governed by the vagaries of climate and other influences, such as pest attack,
that remain beyond the farmer's control. Given the foregoing, the small
farmer is unable to bank on producing an assured surplus.
2.08      FMG is becoming increasingly aware of the need to rectify the
current situation, and in its discussions with the Bank and NWS has agreed
that, in the short term, at least the quickest way of improving farm produc-
tion and incomes would be to provide a concentration of support services to
areas of reasonable potential and dense farm population. In such areas, farm
sizes cannot increase and farmers, with declining land fertility, are facing



-4-
the problem of maintaining, let alone increasing, production. Such farmers
are likely to respond well to any improvements that may be offered to them.
For the longer term, it will be important to develop programs to open up
areas of unused land, particularly in the now tsetse-infested middle belt,
for settlement by farmers and graziers.
2.09      The project appraised in this report would provide a package of
services to existing farming communities with the objective of increasing
production of cash and food crops; it would train agricultural, credit,
marketing, administrative and engineering staff, and would develop NWS
agricultural planning capacity. The increase in farm incomes, generated by
project actions, would be significant. Further background information is
available in Report 416(a) titled "Current Economic Position and Prospects
of Niger,ia" dated August 5, 1974 with special reference to Vol III on
"Problems'.and Prospects of Food Production."
III. PROJECT AREA
A. General
3.01      The project area covers 3,800 km  or about 2% of the total area
of the North Western State. It comprises part of the Sokoto Administrative
Division. Map shows area location within Nigeria.
3.02      The annual rainfall is about 900 mm and is confined entirely to a
5-month period from May through September. Under reasonable management and
utilization of improved seeds and fertilizers, good yields can be achieved
under the prevailing climatic regime. Due to the seasonality of rainfall and
the underlying granitic basement complex, groundwater is severely restricted,
and with increasing land pressures stream flow has markedly diminished and
water supplies for human and livestock resources are a limiting factor to
development.
3.03      The project area is occupied by the people of the Hausa tribe, and
by mid-1974 is estimated to have some 68,000 farm families that support a
total project area population of 620,000. The social structure is significant
because of the relative strength and influence of the hierarchical structure
of the Local Authority. The project comes within the jurisdiction of the
Sultan of Sokoto whose hereditary authority is delegated through salaried
district and village heads. The latter are responsible for village level
administration of land tenure arrangements based on Islamic law and on cus-
tomary usufructory rights.
3.04      Information on farm size is scant but it is estimated that the
average size farm holdings is 3.5 ha. There are some larger farms (over 50



- 5 -
ha) within the project area, but these are relatively few. Cropping patterns
(see Annex 1) are dominated by sorghum and millet as food grains, and cotton
and groundnuts as the major cash crops. Numerous minor crops are grown.
3.05      The project area is well served by main roads, and has a railhead
at Gusau. However,crop extraction and other minor roads are virtually non-
existent, and communities are seasonally cut off from each other and the
outside world. Transportation of farmer produce and inputs is therefore
difficult and expensive. Further project area data are in Annex 2.
B. Institutions
3.06      State institutions that would be concerned with the project and
their relevant responsibilities are described in Annex 2. They include: the
1Ministry of Agriculture (MA) that would have the responsibility for carrying
out the project; the Ministry of Works (MOW), responsible for State-financed
public works programs; the Ministry of Health (MOH), responsible for all
aspects of human health in the State; the Ministry of Industry Trade, and
Cooperatives (MITC) responsible for cooperative development; and the Sokoto
Local Authority (SLA). The project area comes under the control of the SLA,
whose functions include the collection of tax in respect of people and cattle
(haraji and jangali, respectively) and the administration cf land allocation.
The Gusau and Kaura Namoda Development Boards are responsible for the provi-
sion of services such as feeder road construction and maintenance, dispens-
aries, and primary education as a complement to State Government activities
in these fields.
3.07      Another institution that would be concerned with or affected by
the project and which is described in Annex 2 would be the Institute of Agri-
cultural Research (IAR) which was created in the early 1960's from the Re-
search Division of the then Northern Nigeria Ministry of Agriculture, and
which now is a semi-autonomous body linked with Ahmadu Bello University (ABU;
(at Annex 3).
3.08      Institutions concerned with crop marketing and processing are the
Northern States Marketing Board (NSMB) which as purchasing agent of the Federal
Nigerian Produce Marketing Company (NPMC) is responsible for organizing the
domestic purchase, through its Licensed Buying Agents (LBA), of controlled
crops such as cotton and groundnuts at prices predetermined by FMG; and the
Cotton and Agricultural Processors Ltd. (CAP) which was formerly the British
Cotton Growers' Association (Nigeria) Ltd. and which has had a monopoly for
ginning Nigerian cotton since exports began over half a century ago. CAP
has ginneries throughout the cotton-growing zones of the northern states.
The present ginning agreement between CAP and NSMB expires in 1976 and for
this reason assurances were obtained from NWS during negotiations that any
future ginning arrangements would not adversely affect the production, market-
ing or ginning of cotton within the project area.  The Produce Inspection
Division (PID) of MA has important responsibilities for grading controlled
produce. Crop processing, marketing and grading arrangements are described
in Annex 2.



- 6 -
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Description
4.01      The project would be carried out over a five-year investment period
1975/76-1979/80, and would involve:
-    constructing about 1,000 km of low cost agricultural roads,
85 earth dams, 160 tapkis (ponds), and associated soil con-
servation structures;
-    constructing an administrative center in Gusau, 4 development
unit centers, and 40 farm service centers;
-    expanding Gusau seed multiplication farm and the new
development of an additional farm at Kaura Namoda;
-    expanding training facilities at Gusau and constructing
a new project training center at Kaura Namoda,
-    provision of adequate farmer extension, credit and market-
ing services, including staff, vehicles and equipment;
-    provision of seasonal and medium-term loans to farmers
for the purchase of crop inputs and farm equipment;
-    establishing a project evaluation unit; and
-    preparation of plans for the continued provision of project
services after the investment phase of the project is com-
pleted.
4.02      The project would be managed by the Gusau Project Management Unit
(GPMU) which would carry out the day-to-day management operations. GPMU
would be responsible to the Gusau Project Executive Committee (GPEC) for
budgetary and policy control.  A committee would be set UpDto coordinate
project activities at Federal level. Details at Chapter VT.
B. Detailed Features
4.03      A summary showing the expected scheduling of project activities
is at Annex 2, Table 1; it includes the construction program, the rate of
village involvement, and the number of farmers affected by the project
annually.



- 7 -
4.04      Agricultural Road Development.  The project would provide a network
of 1,000 km of agricultural roads, of a design adequate to allow light traffic
during most of the wet season, and heavy trucks during the dry season. The
roads would permit the efficient evacuation of farm produce, the timely supply
of inputs, the effective movement of project service staff, and much improved
general access to farms and hamlets. This basic infrastructure would allow
other Government programs including health and education to expand into areas
which currently are inaccessible. Annex 4 gives details of construction
methods, specifications, and plant and equipment requirements. In view of an
acute shortage of local contracting capacity, and the probable lack of interest
of foreign contractors in work of this sort, construction would be undertaken
by force account through a project operated and managed field construction
unit which would make some use of petty contractors and would thereby assist
in their development. Maintenance of roads and dams would be the responsi-
bility of the Gusau and Kaura Namoda Development Boards. In order to build
up their operational effectiveness, the Boards' road maintenance staff would
be seconded to the project for training and operational control. The project
would provide, during the investment period, funds for purchase and operating
required equipment.
4.05      Water Development.  The project would set up and operate a unit to
construct some 85 small and medium size earth dams, each of a minimum capacity
of 100,000 m3, equipped with simple systems to reticulate the water away from
the reservoir. The reservoirs would supply water for human and livestock needs
and would be located in strategic areas. They would also be used to supply
water for compaction purposes during road construction. In addition, some
160 tapkis (ponds) would be developed to provide a better distribution of water
for livestock for all but the last 3 months of the dry season. In a very few
instances, the dams and tapkis, where land and water availability allows,
would be used for the irrigation of village vegetable production. Abundant
dam sites are available. Annex 5 provides detail on design and specifications.
4.06      Soil Conservation.  In association with the water development
program, soil conservations measures would be undertaken within the catchmen..
areas of the dams (about 5 km2 each). These would include the construction
of cutoff ditches, artificial waterways and contour ridging and be aimed to
reduce siltation of the dams and control erosion. Further soil conservation
measures would be taken in providing the farmers with marker ridges to assist
in contour realignment of crop ridges. During the project investment period,
some 2.700 km2 or 70% of the project area would be so protected; these programs
should be continued beyond the investment period. Annex 5 provides the de-
tails of this project component.
4.07      Building Development.  The project would include construction of
houses, offices and stores needed for project services. It would include the
construction of 210 houses of various sizes, a project office, 4 development
center offices, a railhead store and other facilities. In addition, 40 farm
service centers, each including a 500-ton capacity warehouse would be con-
structed, and improvements made to two market depots associated with existing



-8-
cotton ginneries. Those houses and buildings not required by MA after the
project investment period would be taken over by other Government depart-
ments operating within the area.
4.08      Seed Multiplication Farms.  The project would make improvements to
the existing seed multiplication farm at Gusau; and would develop a new seed
farm at Kaura Namoda. Investments would include farm buildings, water supplies,
farm machinery and equipment. These seed farms would, in association with
larger farmers, provide project and other State farmers with improved seeds,
act as demonstration and research centers, and provide practical training in
farm management. Details are at Annex 3, Schedule 1. During loan negotia-
tions, assurances were obtained from NWS that not later than one month from
loan effective date management responsibilities for Gusau seed farm, together
with its assets and staff, would be under the care of GPMU; and that additional
suitable land for the expansion of Gusau seed farm and land for the Kaura
Namoda farm would be made available to the project.
4.09      Training Facilities.  The project would expand boarding accommoda-
tion at the Gusau training center from 20-40 trainees and construct a new
center at Kaura Namoda, also catering for 40 residential trainees. Initially,
the centers would be used for project staff training, but progressively em-
phasis would be on farmer training.
4.10      Farmer Support Services.  The project would expand and intensify
support services for farmers. It would increase the farmer extension person-
nel ratio from the current 1:2,430 to 1:290. This would involve an increase
i in extension agents from 28 to 230.  The extension personnel would include,
besides basic extension agents, personnel specialized in livestock husbandry,
seed multiplication and farm management, farmer training, and farm equipment.
The tractor hire service now operated by MA at a loss would be phased out,
and gradually replaced by project sponsored and assisted private operators.
A credit service would be established to operate a project controlled credit
revolving fund and the contingent services to farmers (para 6.20). A market-
ing service would also be established to improve market liaison, quality
control, price information, produce weighing and to control traders. The
credit and marketing services would employ some 220 staff of various grades;
of these only about 10, mainly produce examiners are now employed by MA.
Consequently, the majority of personnel employed in these services would
receive in-service training from the project (details are at Annex 6).
4.11      Other MA services such as veterinary, fisheries and forestry would
continue to work within the project area, and would coordinate their activi-
ties with GPMU.
4.12      Farm Inputs.  Under the project, seasonal inputs would be made avail-
able to farmers for cash and credit. Such inputs and equipment are estimated
to amount, during the project investment period, to 25,000 tons of fertilizer,
3,000 tons improved seeds, 650 tons insecticides, 30,000 ultra low volume
insecticide sprayers, 5,000 ox carts, 5,000 ox plows and 50 tractors and



- 9 -
associated implements. Inputs and equipment would be sold for cash or credit
at the farm service centers. Details are at Annexes 7 and 8.,,
4.13      Project Evaluation.  A field evaluation unit would be established
to collect relevant data necessary for the evaluation of project actions and
resultant social and economic benefits. Much of the data would be processed
by the evaluation section of the Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU)
to be established by the Federal Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources
(-MANR) and financed under Funtua Agricultural Development Project loan.
The field evaluation unit would receive technical direction from PNEU, but
would be administered by the project (Annex 9 for details).
4.14      Provision would be made for the necessary support items such as
motor vehicles, equipment, etc. to ensure that the objectives of the project
are fulfilled. Details at Annex 10.
4.15      Post Project Development.  During the project investment period,
MA and GPMU would draw up detailed plans and arrange for the allocation of
staff to allow project services to continue undisrupted to farmers within the
project area after the end of the investment period. Presupposing no radical
changes in NWS organization extension and development services would be handled
by MA's regular provincial organization suitably strengthened; the field evalua-
tion unit would be absorbed by M4A Planning Unit; NSMB would take over market
control and liaison, and the Gusau and Kaura Namoda Development Boardsor any
other appropriate agency would maintain roads and water supplies provided under
the project. Assurances that the the roads and dams would be maintained were
obtained during negotiation.
4.16      During the project the evaluation section of PMEU would consider
various alternatives for the future use and operations of the revolving credit
fund and the supply of farm inputs. It is important that by 1980 credit and
farm supplies should be commercially orientated, and that the institutions
involved should be relatively independent of Government with control in the
hands of participating farmers. Prior to negotiations the intention was tc
set up a Farmer Finance Company to control and operate the revolving credit
fund. During negotiations FIG indicated that it would prefer to postpone
the formation of such a company to a time when (a) further evaluations of
farmer credit institutions and requirements had been made by PMEU during the
first three years of the project; and (b) evidence, if any, of improvements
in the performance of the existing cooperative system under new Federal policy
and guidance. The exclusion of the proposed Farmer Finance Company should have
no adverse effect on the project during the development period, provided that
firm plans are made for the proper transfer of the fund to the appropriate
institution prior to the project's completion. Thus, PMEU would play an
important role in evaluating the problems and requirements for future com-
mercial services to project farmers.

or 47% of total costs.

5.03      Project costs include a 5% physical contingency, and a variable price
contingency 11 over the proposed five-year loan disbursement period. Contingen-
cies amount to N 6.1 million (US$9.3 million) or 25% of total project costs.
Costs are based on current quotations for vehicles, plant, equipment, materials
and farm inputs; on current contract rates for building construction; and on
up-to-date salary and wage scales inclusive of allowances. Costs of expatriate
staff are based on current international salaries, and include standard benefits
and allowances. In the case of farm inputs, only incremental requirements are
*sted. Participating farmers would, as now, receive a 50% subsidy on tihe cost
of phosphatic and nitrogenous fertilizers and the costs of this subsidy is in-
cluded in project costs. Project costs include import duties and taxes (inter-
nationally recruited staff salaries are estimated net of tax). Total taxes
amount to N 1.2 million (US$1.8 million). Analysis of the costs, Annex 10,
Table 1, shows that 8% (N 1.9 million) is for project administration; 25%
(N 6.0 million) is for extension, credit and market services; 19% (N 4.4 million
is for road, soil and water conservation; 23% (N 5.7 million) for incremental
farm inputs and fertilizer subsidies; 2% (N 0.5 million) for project evalulation;
and 25% (N 6.1 million) for contingencies. Internationally recruited staff
costs are estimated at 4% of total costs, about one third of which would be
for specialized technicians currently unavailable or in critically short sup-
ply in Nigeria.
B. Proposed Financing
5.04      It is proposed that IBRD make a loan of USQ19 million to FMC,
repayable at 8% over 20 years including a five-year grace period for principal
during which interest and commitment ciharges on the loan would be paid.
Equivalent to 51% of total project costs the loan would cover all foreign
costs, estimated at US$17.7 million (N 11.6 million), and 7% of local costs
amounting to US$1.3 million (N 0.9 million).  The US$19 million would be
onlent to the NWS and during negotiations it was agreed that the subsidiarv loan
agreement between FM4C and NWS would have been approved by the Bank.
5.05      NWS would provide N 11.2 million (US$17 million) or 45% of total
project cost as its contribution to project investmenit and operating costs.
NWSG Tne remainder of project costs estiLated at N 0.9 million (US$1.4 million)
or 4% would be contributed by participating farmers in the form of cash pur-
chases of seasonal inputs and down payments on farm equipment.  Details of
the project financing plan are shown at Annex 11, Table 1, and are summar-

5.06      It was agreed that as from the date of loan negotiations retroactive
financing of up to US$150,000 would be made for the employment of key personnel
(para 6.09).
C. Procurement
5.07      Procurement of vehicles, plant, equipment, fertilizers, insecticides,
farm tractors and implements, where orders or contracts had a value of more
than US$25,000, would be through international competitive bidding (ICB) in
accordance with IBRD guidelines. Such procurement is estimated to have a
value of about N 7.5 million (US$11.4 million). Domestically manufactured
goods would be allowed a 15% preference when comparing domestic bids with
those of foreign manufacturers. Contracts for the construction of buildings,
houses, and the purchase of construction materials and furnishings valued



- 13 -
at N 3.6 million (US$5.4 million) would not be attractive to foreign con-
tractors, due to the dispersed location of the work and the small size of
individual contracts and orders, and would be awarded under locally advertized
competitive bidding. In the latter case, foreign firms and suppliers would
not be precluded from bidding as such work would be advertized in at least
one local newspaper, but bonafide domestic contractors would receive a 7-1/2%
preference in bid evaluation. Construction of roads, water supplies and soil
conservation works estimated at N 4.4 million (US$6.7 million) would be carried
out on force account due to the shortage of suitable medium scale contractors
who normally would undertake this work, however petty contractors would be
em} oyed to the maximum extent feasible.  A large part of project costs, an
estimated N 7.6 million (US$11.6 million), would be for labor, staff salaries
and allowances, vehicle maintenance and operation, general services, and
project evaluation, and as such generally be unsuitable for competitive bid-
ding. Those items not included specifically under procedures for ICB or
local competitive bidding would be purchased through local commercial channels.
The remaining N 6.1 million (US$9.3 million) of project costs would be contin-
gencies. Nigeria has no preferential import tariff arrangements and competi-
tion between foreign manufacturers on the domestic market is keen.
D. Disbursement
5.08      The proceeds of the Bank loan (US$19 million) would be disbursed
over five years (1975/76-1979/80) to cover 51% of total project costs and
would be expended against the following categories:
100% of the cif cost of directly imported vehicles,
heavy plant, and equipment (Annex 10, Table 2) or 76%
of the cost of these items if locally procured totalling
US$2.0 million (N 1.3 million);
100% of the cif cost of directly imported farm tractors
and equipment, ox drawn equipment, spraying machines,
fertilizers, insecticides, battery sets and seeds (Annex
7, Tables 2-5) or 71% of the cost of these items if pro-
cured locally totalling US$3.5 million (N 2.3 million);
100% of the cost of internationally recruited staff
(Annex 10, Table 4) totalling US$1.6 million (N 1.0 million);
60% of the cost of buildings, houses, furnishings, and
construction material and labor for roads, dams and
soil conservation works (Annex 10, Table 3) totalling
US$3.2 million (N 2.2 million);
60% of local staff costs (Annex 10, Table 4) totalling
US$2.8 million (N 1.8 million);



- 14 -
60% of the cost of the field evaluation unit
(Annex 9, Table 1) US$0.5 million (N 0.3 million);
and
an unallocated amount totalling US$5.4 million (X 3.6
million) to cover physical and price contingencies.
5.09      Disbursements of the Bank loan would be against import documentation,
contracts and certified records of expenditures. For disbursements made against
certified records of expenditures, documentation would not be submitted for
review as a matter of course, but would be retained by the project management
unit for scrutiny by Bank supervision missions. Any surplus loan funds after
the completion of the project would be used to further develop the project
area providing expanded services to farmers.
E. Budgetary Control Funding Procedures Accounts and Audit
5.10      Budgets.  Annual budgets would be prepared by GPMU and approved
by GPEC for inclusion in the MA development budget. The initial budget
would use as its base the cost estimates set out in this report, but would
be amended where necessary to tie in with changes in costs and project poli-
cies and development schedules. Major changes in policies and procedures
would have to be approved by GPEC and the Bank. GPMU would, thereafter,
amend its monthly cash flow statement indicating costs, revenues and working
capital requirements. These monthly statements would be submitted to GPEC
for monitoring but GPMU would be authorized to operate on such monthly state-
ments unless officially informed to the contrary by the Chairman of GPEC.
Assurances that the above arrangements would be employed were obtained from
NWS during negotiations.
5.11      Funding Procedures.  On the basis of the annual budget approved by
GPEC, NWS would make the necessary allocations in its annual development
budget to cover its contribution to project costs as well as about 25% of
the remaining annual costs of the project to meet working capital require-
ments, the latter amounting to about N 0.6 million. In order to insure an
adequate flow of funds to the Project, assurances were obtained during nego-
tiations that when necessary adequate guaranteed draw down procedures would
be made by NWS with a bank of its choice to allow GIMU full access to funds
within the limitation of the approved annual budget. As a condition of loan
effectiveness, NWS would furnish the Bank with satisfactory evidence that a
project bank account, with adequate funds, had been established.
5.12      Accounts and Audit.  Accounts for GPMU would be kept under acceptable
commercial accounting procedures. However, such accounts would be set up to
facilitate the retrieval of cost accounting and project evaluation data, as
well as for audit control. All the accounts would be audited annually by in-
dependent auditors, mutually acceptable to NWS, FMG and the Bank. Audited
annual accounts, balance sheets and operating statements for GPMU would be



- 15 -
submitted simultaneously to NWS, FMG and the Bank within four months of the
closing of the financial year. During negotiations, assurances to these
effects were obtained.
VI. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Oranization
6.01      The project being the first of its kind in the State would have to
be carefully controlled. Because of its size and reponsibilities for various
development aspects not normally associated with any one Government Ministry,
it would need a degree of autonomy not normally Riven to Ministerial Depart-
ments.- Thus, the project would be implemented by the Gusau Project ge-
ment Unit (GPMU), headed by a Project Manager who would be responsible to the
Gusau Project Executive              PEG); the chairman of which would be the
Permanenfety of the Ministry of Agriculture oE NWS.  GPMU would have                :2
its headquarters at Gusau, the main center of the project area, GPMJ would   .
coordinate its activities with other similar projects at a Federal level
through a Federal CoordinatinRommittee. As a condition of loan effectiveness,
the North Western State Executive Council would gazette an order, acceptajble
to FMG and the Bank, establishing GPMT, setting out its terms of reference
and responsibilities.  Assurances to this effect were obtaTLned during nego-
tiations (a draft proposal for the order is shown at Annex 6, Schedule 2).
6.02      Gusau Project Executive Committee would, inter alia, determine
policy, coordinate project activities with those of associated State Ministries
and Departments, ezrcise budgPt-ry n   audit-e*utrol, approve the appointment
of professional grade staff, and review and set salary scales. GPEC would meet
at least once a quarter and would comprise:  the Permanent SretJariMA_as
Chairman, and representatives of_the Ministries of Finance, Local Government,
Works and EcQn     1auL  4gX NWS Water Board, a representative of the Federal   (f
Department of Agriculture, Chief Agricultural Officer for the North Western
State, and two members residing in the project area to be appointed by the
Military Governor. The Project Manager would be Secretary of GPEC.
6.03      A Federal Coordinating Committee would be established to review the
progress of the Gusau and similar projects in the northern States, It would
provide coordination between other Bank-financed agricultural projects oLf
similar nature in northern Nigeria, and would insure a full exchange of ideas
and results. In its review of project progress, it would be aided by the
evaluation section of the Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PIEU) set
up under the Bank loan for the Funtua Agricultural Development Project.  The
committee would meet at least twice a year and would comprise: The Director
of the Federal                                   as            ChaCief- Agricultural
Planning OfficerJFDA)_-as Secretary; a representative from the respective
project executive committees;,Project Managers of Gusau, Gombe and Funtua.-
Director of the Institute of Agricultural Research, the Head of PMEU evalua-
tion section; representatives of NAB and _N21liC; and representatives of the
Federal Ministry of Finance. During negotiations assurances were obtained



- 16 -
that a Federal Coordinating Committee would be established, with terms of
reference and composition satisfactory to the Bank, within 3 months of loan
effectiveness.
6.04      Project Administration would fall under the direct responsibility
of the Project Manager for all operational procedures and actions. He would
be responsible to GPEC. The GPEC, in consultation with the Project Manager,
would appoint one of the senior project staff as Deputy Project Manager. The
Project Manager would be assisted by: Chief Administrative Officer, Chief
Accountant, Chief Technical Officer, Chief Engineer, Chief Marketing Officer,
and Credit Manager (Annex 6, Schedule 3 provides details of the tasks of key
personnel). A summary showing the functions of the project's six operational
sections follows:
(a) Administration Section would be responsible for all project
personnel matters, staff transportation, registry, security and
public relations.
(b) Finance Section would be responsible for project accounts,
procurement, stores, internal audit, budget control and
would set up systems to provide accurate cost accounts for
evaluation and management purposes.
(c) Technical Services Section would be responsible for farm
extension advice, staff and farmer training, seed multipli-
cation, farm management advice to larger farmers, and tractor
hire services. The main development effort would be through
the extension service and structurally it would consist of
four development unit centers based on the towns of Chafe,
Kotorkoshi, Bungudu and Kaura Namoda, each headed by a Devel-
opment officer, 1/ who would supervise agricultural assistants
(AA) posted to service centers in each of the 40 villages of
the project area. Each AA would supervise an average of four
field supervisors (FS) who would live in the hamlets
associated with each village and who would work with the
hamlet farmers and their committees. In this way, the ex-
tension system would be tailored to the traditional village
structure.
(d) Credit Services Section. Credit services would work parallel
with the extension services. Credit Officers would be posted
to Development Unit Centers, and two Credit Assistants to each
farm service center.
1/   Normally the minimum grade of the officer filling this post would be
that of agricultural officer. However, in the early stages of the pro-
ject, it is likely that most of the posts would be filled by agricultural
superintendents.



- 17 -
(e)  Market Services Section would monitor the existing marketing
system, liaise and provide market information for buyers and
farmers, control the activities of Licensed Buying Agents (LBA),
and supervise the work of produce examiners (PE). A Marketing
Supervisor would be assisted by two Marketing Assistants posted
to each Development Unit Center and a Produce Examiner would be
posted to each market. Market Services would be responsible
for the logistics involved in procurement of farm inputs and
for their distribution and handling. As such, the produce
examiner would carry out the functions of store keeper when the
markets are closed.
(f)  Engineering Services Section would be a specialized service
responsible for land use planning, road construction, water
development, soil conservation, building supervision and work-
shop management. It would prepare tender documentation for
procurement of engineering equipment and supplies, supervise
building contractors, and implement all construction aspects
of the project not undertaken by contractors. It would main-
tain project vehicles and equipment.
6.05      Field evaluation would be directed by the Evaluation Section of
PNEU. The field enumeration staff would be administratively responsible to
the Project Manager. Details of the field unit are at Annex 9.
B. Staffin'
6.06      Both Federal and State Governments recognize the need for qualified
and experienced staff to administer the project and assure high technical
standards in its field implementation. The northern States are generally short
of qualified personnel to fill key project management and senior technical
posts, and the demand for personnel with such expertise is high. 7NWS is fully
aware of the problem of staffing the project adequately and is prepared to rn-
cruit staff internationally where qualified Nigerian personnel are not avail
Medium level technical and administrative staff needed for the project are
erally available in Nigeria, though not necessarily in the North Western StaLe.
Most lower level staff needs would be met from within the State: existing
civil servants; or the output of existing training schemes; or staff recruited
and trained directly by the project.
6.07      Management Staff.  International recruitment is expected to be
necessary for the Project Manager, Chief Accountant, Credit Manager, Chief
Engineer, and Chief Marketing Officer.  The Chief Administrative Officer and
Chief Technical Officer would be Nigerian appointments. During negotiations
assurances were obtained that the qualifications, experience and terms and
conditions of employment of appointees to the above posts would be mutually
acceptable to the Bank and to NWS. A condition of effectiveness would be that
the Project Manager, Chief Administrative Officer, Chief Accountant, Chief
Engineer and Chief Technical Officer had been appointed.



- 18 -
6.08      High Level Technical Staff.  Nigeria is critically short of expe-
rienced technical personnel. Given the shortage of experienced and available
technical staff it is likely that a farm manager, a seeds officer, a land use
planner. a road engineer, a mechanical engineer, a water and soil conservation
engineer and a senior evaluation officer would have to be recruited inter-
nationally. During negotiations assurances were obtained that their quali-
fications and terms and conditions of employrment would be acceptable to the
Bank and NWS.
6.09      Medium Level Staff.  Professional officer (e.g., Agricultural Offi-
cer) or senior technical officer (e.g., Agricultural Superintendent) grades
would be recruited from within the State or from other States in Nigeria.
All field technical, credit and marketing service staff would be required to
be Hausa speaking. Medium and lower level staff exist, although many are
assigned currently to nonproductive posts. During negotiations assurances
were obtained from NWS that a minimum of 2 Agricultural Officers, 12 agri-
cultural superintendents and 41 agricultural assistants or experienced
agricultural instructors would be made available to the project during the
first two years.
6.10      Where possible project staff, particularly agricultural staff, would
be appointed to the project as Government employees on full pensionable service.
Expatriate staff and other Nigerian staff such as engineers, credit and market-
ing personnel would be appointed on three year contracts renewable on negotia-
tion. Salaries would generally follow acceptable Government scales. Every
effort would be made to avoid transfer of staff away from the project, except
on the grounds of unsuitability, and assurances were obtained during negotia-
tions that transfers of senior staff into and from the project organization
would be undertaken only after consultation with GPEC.
6.11      Project staff of technical grades (or equivalent) and above would be
provided with project housing both at Gusau and in the field. Nontechnical
grades such as field supervisors (FS) and produce examiners (PE), as well as
clerks, drivers, messengers etc.would be expected to find their own accommoda-
tion in the towns and villages. At the discretion of project management,
staff would be provided with project transport. Project staff would qualify
for normal Government allowances. Annex 10, Tables 3-4 gives details. Due
to expected delays in construction of houses, costs are included for renting
private accommodation (Annex 10, Table 6).
C. Staff Training
6.12      GPMU would undertake the training of lower level staff through
special courses at project training centers (para 4.09). The training of
higher level staff would be undertaken on an inservice basis. GPMU would pay
particular attention to training selected staff to fill managerial positions
as these are vacated by internationally recruited personnel (see Annex 6,



- 19 -
Tables 1-5 for possible schedule) and would undertake as requested to train
staff to be employed for other NWS development projects. Such staff would
receive practical management training on the project and would have the
opportunity of decision making at various levels within the project, but
would retain executive responsibility for their own section of the project.
NWS and the Bank would ensure that adequate training of potential management
staff was undertaken, and GPM4U management would report regularly on the pro-
gress of project training.
D. Farm Inputs - Procurement and Distribution and Credit Procedures
6.13      The project would be responsible for the procurement, distribution
and sale, for cash or credit, of fertilizers, seeds, insecticides, sprayers,
oxdrawn equipment, tractors, and other necessary farm supplies; and would
decide on the suitability and composition of any package of inputs offered
to farmers. It is expected that 75% of seasonal inputs and 100% of farm
equipment would be sold on credit. The value of inputs sold during the
project life is estimated at N 4.9 million for fertilizer, N 1.7 million for
insecticides, seeds, and batteries; and N 1.6 million for farm equipment, a
total of N 8.2 million. The net cash requirement for inputs would be N 5.7
million of which N 2.4 million would be the NWS subsidy on fertilizers.
Annex 7 describes farm inputs and their procurement.
6.14      Project marketing services would be responsible, on advice of the
technical staff, for the procurement of farm inputs and for the logistics
involved in their handling within the project area.
6.15      Currently NWS subsidises nitrogenous and phosphatic fertilizers
by between 30 and 50% of their cost delivered MA store. The actual rate
depends on the efficiency and cost of NWS handling. Under the project
farmers would receive nitrogenous and phosphatic fertilizer at 50% of cost
cif farm service center. Such a subsidy is justified (a) as an incentive
for farmers to use fertilizer, 1/ and (b) to compensate for generally low
marketing board prices for statutory marketed crops. During loan negotiatio
assurances were obtained that the present subsidy on nitrogenous and phos-
phatic fertilizers would be maintained. Currently insecticides receive a
50% subsidy. However as usage is insignificant at present and the benefits
of their use clearly discernible and profitable insecticides would be sold
to farmers at their full cost.
Farm Credit
6.16      The organization of the delivery and recovery of farm credit is
described in Annex 8. The net incremental credit requirement and gross credit
disbursed during the five year project period is estimated at N 2.6 million,
and N 4.4 million respectively.
1/   The gross cost of fertilizer is high in northern Nigeria, and to induce
the farmer to use more fertilizer subsidies are an added incentive.
Fertilizer subsidies are general throughout Nigeria.



- 20 -
6.17      GPMU would set up a revolving credit fund to be operated by the
Credit Services Section of the project. Prior to the beginning of each
planting season, a schedule of proposed lending terms would be furnished by
GPMU to GPEC, which after consultation with appropriate Nigerian authorities
and the Bank would apply the lending terms for the said season. Interest
rates for sub-loans would be between 5-9-1/2%, and would be determined on a
basis of the category of farmers or type of inputs to be provided. Assurances
to these effects were obtained during negotiations.
6.18      As a basis for preparing crop budgets shown at Annex 1, it is as-
sumed that GPMU would lend at a flat service rate of 10%, a charge that can
be afforded by the farmers and would include sufficient margin to cover bad
debts etc. Repayments would be made for seasonal loans within 12 months;
sprayers over 2 years; ox drawn equipment over 3 years; and tractors and
implements over 4 years. Except in cases of crop failure, when a moratorium
would be declared by GPEC, a borrower not repaying his loan by the due date
would be charged a penalty of 1% per month. Funds have not been specifically
provided under the loan for cash credits to cover the employment of farm
labor, as it is considered that any additional labor requirement will be
met from within the family unit, particularly as the project is directed
towards the small family operatee farm holding. However, in the light of
current changes in Nigeria, specifically the advent of compulsory free
education, the need for hired labor may increase. During the project life
GPMU in association with PIMEU would establish the parameters, if any, for
cash credits, and the logistics involved in their disbursement and collec-
tion. If the findings are positive, the Government and the Bank would agree
on the utilization of local funds or disbursements from undisbursed portions
of the Bank loan.
6.19      The revolving credit fund is an intimate part of the project, and
of necessity has to evolve into a more permanent institution so that farmers
can utilize its funds after the project is completed. Such an institution
might take the form of cooperatives, farmer finance companies, or branches
of state or national credit banks. So as to ensure a proper transfer of
responsibilities the evaluation section of PMEU would, as one of its duties,
determine the future institutional form of the fund, taking into account the
performance of existing lending institutions and the needs of the farmers
(see para 4.16).
6.20      Operating procedures, which are described in Annex 8 would be
subject to modification as necessary, would be devised by the project credit
service but would include the following essential components: lending would
be limited to input items carried by the project at its farm service center
stores, and be in amounts specified on a simple farm management plan drawn
up for each borrower by extension service staff. Sale of items would be
recorded on a bookkeeping machine. As the farm service centers develop into
the main markets for cash crops, farmers would be encouraged to repay their
debts to GPMU at time of sale. All borrowers would nave a laminated identity
card containing their photograph, serial number, and signature or mark to
facilitate identification and record keeping.



- 21 -
6.21      The creditworthiness of each loan applicant would be certified by
his hamlet or village head, or a project approved farmers association; this
would be part of the mutual guarantee policy that GPMU would pursue. Under
this, once a year GPMU would review all hamlet accounts, and where defaults
were found the village, hamlet or farmer association authorities, whichever
the smallest unit, would be informed of their defaulters and urged to collect
from defaulters, unless due cause is presented for default. If defaults
were not made good, GPMU would withhold credit to all farmers in the default-
ing unit. It is believed that this technique would be a powerful influence in
avoiding default, under northern Nigerian circumstances. Provision would be
lade to extend credit to groups or to village farmer associations if farmers
wish to pool the input requirements and assume joint liability for the credit.
VII. PRODUCTION, MARKETS_ FAMEMRS' BENEFITS AND
IN~dANCIAL IMPLICATION TO GOVERNMENT
A. Yields and Outputs
7.01      The complexity of cropping mixtures and patterns in the project
area make yield variations between farms inevitable and substantial. It has
been assumed, however, that by project maturity in 1980 project area farmers
would fall into one of two categories for each of the five crops that would be
influenced by the project. The categories would be determined by the extent to
which the farmer had taken up improved practices. Details are given in Annex 1.
Yield improvements and increases in gross production due to project actions
are summarized for each of the five crops:
-       Before Development---     ----After Development----
Area  Av. Yield  Total Prod.    Area  Av. Yield  Total Prod.
(ha)   (kg/ha)  ('000 mi tons)   (ha)   (kg/ha)  ('000 m tons)
Seed Cotton   41,500      230            9       49,500      460           23
Sorghum        75,000     600           45       69,000      820           56
Maize i         1,000      700            1      12,500   1,740            22
Groundnuts    37,000      430           16       38,750      640           25
Cowpea          2,500     200            0.5      5,000      410            2
7.02      Yield improvements are conservative and fall well within experimental
and field results obtained in northern Nigeria and other similar ecological
zones elsewhere in West Africa.   The proposed crop inputs to achieve these
expected increase have been well tested, and are economic. Further details
are at Annexes 1 and 3.



- 22 -
B. Markets and Prices
7.03      Currently, food crops grown in the project area are marketed via
rural markets. Marketing is not efficient since although local competition
may be strong, the market open to the farmers is very small and only a
fraction of the national market. The main defects spring from the fragmented
nature of the market and farmers' lack of both accurate information and
bargaining power; the project would seek to rectify this position (details
are given in Annex 12, paras 38 and 39).
7.04      Following an FMG announcement in early 1973, statutory marketing
of cotton and groundnuts together with other cash crops in Nigeria is under-
going a series of organizational changes which include: (a) federal respon-
sibility for producer price determination and support price policies; this
could lead eventually to subsidization of primary and secondary marketing
operations by FMG; (b) abolition of produces taxes; (c) closer relationship
between world and producer prices and thus an end to indirect taxation through
accumulation of surpluses by the Marketing Boards; and (d) the Northern
States MIarketing Board (NSIB) becoming an agent for the Nigerian Produce
Marketing Company (NPMC), the latter wholly owned by FGN now being the price
controlling and setting organization within the statutory system. The
reforms are being effected, and the 1974 producer prices reflect the actual
world market situation and should provide considerable production incentives.
7.05      As there is need for considerable improvement in primary marketing,
the project would take various measures to improve marketing in general.
More specifically, the project would provide improved weighing and measuring
devices and village storage facilities, and would relocate markets to improve
the ease of crop marketing. For the statutory crops a number of marketing
reforms would be necessary, such reforms initially to be confined to the
project areas include: (a) timely announcement of producer prices (b) pro-
vision of adequate funds to LBA for produce purchase; (c) establishment and
enforcement of quality standards; and (d) disciplining of inefficient or
dishonest LBA and produce inspection staff.
7.06      During negotiations assurances were obtained that:
(i) NWS would cause NSMB to pay particular attention to
efficient produce buying within the project area; and
(ii) where necessary encourage groups of farmers to
operate as Licensed Buying Agents within the
Project Area.
7.07      Producer prices for industrial and export crops, such as cotton
and groundnuts, are expected under the new FMG policies to improve during the
early years of the project, but would then be expected to decline by 1980 in
line with prices projected by the Bank's Economic Analysis and Projections
Department. Food crop prices are expected to decline somewhat from present



- 23 -
levels in consequence of more intensive cereal production in the project area
but are expected to remain substantially above world (export parity) prices.

projected 1980 producer prices, expressed in 1974 constant Naira, as shown in
the following table:

C. Farmer Benefits
7.08      Farmers participating in project activities would obtain substantial
direct income benefits through utilizing project services and inputs. Addi-
tional indirect benefits due to improved roads, water supplies, soil conser-
vation and marketing would accrue to all farmers within the project area.
7.09      Estimated direct benefits have been calculated on a per ha basis
for the five principal crops.  The summarized crop budgets below indicate
the overall benefits of improved technology over traditional practices, and
in particular show the possibilities of maize as a major cash crop within the
area.

7.10 The report has made no attempt to produce a typical farm budget
since farm sizes and production combinations vary considerably throughout
the project area, and any such budget would not be realistic. Instead,
expected farmer benefits have been derived from a series of crop budgets
for 1 ha units of production. Project actions would result in measurable
additions to net family incomes totalling N 4.2 million per annum in 1979.
Studies undertaken in areas similar to the project show net farm incomes
including income from livestock and the value of produce consumed to be in
the order of N 145 (US$220) per family or about N 27 (US$41) per capita.
The net income for the five project crops accounts for about N 143 (US$217)
per family. The additional net income derived from the improved output of
tne five crops would be N 62 (US$94) per family, an increase of 43%. The
average per capita income would increase from US$41 to US$55. Such an im-
provement infers a growth in net farm income of 6% per annum and would appear
feasible given efficient project management.
D. Financial Implication for Government
7.11      The cash flow for NWS is summarized in Annex 11, Table 2.  The aver-
age cost to NWS inclusive of loan servicing, would be about N 2.5 million
annually until 1995. Tnereafter the cost would be reduced to N 1.0 million
annually. Considerable reduction, in Government support finance, could be
made if and when the fertilizer subsidy is reduced or totally removed; such
savings would amount to N 1.1 million annually. NWS could further recoup
up to N 2.6 million when the revolving credit fund is institutionalized,
thereby allowing NWS to retire part or all of its capital. The recent aboli-
tion of produces taxes on statutdry crops throughout Nigeria, including cotton
and groundnuts, would result in NWS receiving no tangible financial benefits
from the project.



- 25 -
7.12      The Federal Government would, through secondary or tertiary mul.
tiplier benefits, conservatively estimated for Nigeria at between 8 to 20t,
collect indirect taxation revenues of about N 0.7 million annually.
VIII. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
8.01      The project's principal direct benefit would be the incremental
increase in farm production that it would generate, and which would result
dire. .:ly in higher incomes for some 41,000 farm families and better living
standards for a further 27,000 who would benefit through the improved
infrastructure and marketing arrangements within the project area. If the
non-farming population, traders, laborers, etc., are included, then about
620,000 people would in one way or other receive primary or secondary benefits
through project activities. The economic rate of return from investment in
the project is estimated at 16%. Important assumptions used in the calcula-
tion at Annex 13 are:
(a)  a shadow wage rate of N 0.40 per 6-hr manday, as against the
official rate of N 0.60;
(b)  the exclusion of identifiable taxes from economic costs;
(c)  a shadow foreign exchange rate of US$1.27 per N 1.00 as
against the official rate of US$1.52 per N 1.00;
(d)  economic farmgate prices for 1980 in 1974 constant Naira
as projected by the Bank's Economic Analysis and Projections
Division (para 7.07) based on export parity for groundnuts
and cotton and import substitution for maize, sorghum and
cowpeas; and
(e)  the exclusion of water and associated soil conservation
development costs and the cost of the field evaluation unit
since the benefits attributed to them are intangible.
8.02      If no adjustment is made for t' shadow rate of foreign exchange,
the economic rate of return would be reduced to 8%. It is possible that
the project might not meet the proposed production targets for a number of
reasons, including poor management, lack of farmer response, and the possible
failure of delivery of farm inputs due to uncontrollable circumstances; and
as such it is important to test the economic rate of return against various
levels of reduced production and the associated reduction in farm inputs,
allowing all other factors to remain constant. This analysis is set out
below:



- 26 -
Production Levels as Percentage of Estimated Project Output
100%    90%    80%    70%    60%    50%
Economic rate of
return (%)               16     14      12      9       6      2
8.03      Such a sensitivity analysis indicates that the project would remain
viable even when production targets fall short by as much as 30%.  Production
at levels lower than 70% of target would infer the need for restructuring of
overhead investments, including reductions in staff and associated invest-
ments. A comprehensive sensitivity analysis is shown at Annex 13.
8.04      The project would have important secondary benefits that would
accrue both to the people of the project area, and for the State and
Federation as a whole. The more important ones would be for:
(a)  the people of the project area would benefit from (i) a general
improvement in communications and public utilities, due to
project actions initiating change and providing a basis for
extending, by other Government institutions, social improvements
including health and education; (ii) an expected increase in
local trade that would offer better opportunities of employment
for the non-farming population; and (iii) strengthening of
local institutions including the local authorities and local
departments of Government;
(b)  the North Western State would benefit principally through part
of its population being stabilized and becoming more productive.
Increased net employment accounting for 8,000 man-years per
annum would result from projected actions. The State would be
able to use the project to test new development ideas that could,
if successful, be easily transposed together with trained staff
to other areas. The Ministry of Agriculture would be streng-
thened, and would derive from the project new credit and marketing
techniques that could be easily applied to other State farmers.
The project would set up a seed multiplication center that
would provide seed to other farmers elsewhere in the State.
Finally, the project would produce additional raw material
and would demand additional input resources to support the
growth of the State's industrial expansion;
(c)  the Federal Republic of Nigeria would benefit from annual net
incremental foreign exchange earnings amounting at full
production to N 4.2 million (US$5.7 million).   Project
evaluation through the project-established evaluation unit
would provide a wealth of information that could assist in
determining Federal agricultural policy, and resource and
financial allocations throughout Nigeria.



- 27 -
8.05      Even though the economic rate of return appears favorable, in the
long run indirect tangible benefits and intangible benefits may prove far
more valuable than direct tangible benefits. Improved farm management
practices and profits to farming would far outlive the project. A step would
be taken in the right direction to reduce income disparity and to ensure a
more even food supply for Nigeria's urban populations and expanding commercial
livestock industries. Perhaps most importantly the people of the project
area would have the peace of mind that would come from more assured water
supplies in the dry season, the added confidence in an improved marketing
sys, m, the satisfaction of a little more money to spend, and the hope that
they and their children would live a little better in the future.
IX. RECOMMENDATIONS
9.01      During negotiations an assurance was obtained from FMG that the
Federal Coordinating Committee would be established with terms of reference
and composition satisfactory to the Bank within 3 months of loan effectiveness
(para 6.03).
9.02      During negotiations assurances were obtained from NWS that:
(a)  any future ginning agreement would not adversely affect
present arrangements as to the production, marketing or
ginning of cotton within the project area (para 3.08);
(b)  management responsibilities for Gusau seed  farm together
with its assets and staff would come under the care of GPMU;
and that suitable land would be made available for the ex-
pansion for Gusau seed farm and for the development of
Kaura Namoda seed farm (para 4.08);
(c)  Gusau and Kaura Namoda Development Boards or any other
appropriate agency would maintain roads and water supplies
provided under the project (para 4.15);
(d)  GPEC would be responsible for approval of GPMU annual budgets
and monthly cash flow statements, and GPMU would be authorized
to operate on such monthly statements unless officially in-
formed to the contrary by the Chairman of GPEC (para 5.10);
(e)  when necessary adequate guaranteed draw-down procedures would
be made with a bank of its choice to allow GPMU full access
to funds within the limitation of the approved annual budget
(para 5.11);
(f)  audited annual accounts for GPMU would be prepared by inde-
pendent auditors, mutually acceptable to NWS and the Bank,
and would be submitted simultaneously to the Bank, NWS and FMG
within 4 months of the closing of the financial year (para 5.12);



- 28 -
(g) the qualifications, experience and terms and conditions of
employment of the Project Manager, Chief Accountant, Credit
Manager, Chief Engineer, Chief Marketing Officer, Chief
Technical Officer and Chief Administrative Officer would be
acceptable to the Bank and NWS (para 6.07);
(h) a farm manager, a seeds officer, a farm management advisor,
a land use planner, a road engineer, a mechanical engineer,
a water and soil conservation engineer and a senior evaluation
officer would be appointed with qualifications and terms and
conditions of employment acceptable to the Bank and NWS
(para 6.08);
(i) a minimum of 2 agricultural officers, 12 agricultural super-
intendents and 41 agricultural assistants or experienced
agricultural instructors would be made available to the
project during the first two years (para 6.09);
(j) transfer of senior staff into and from the project would be
undertaken only after consultation with GPEC (para 6.10);
(k) the present subsidy on nitrogenous and phosphatic fertilizers
would be maintained (para 6.15);
(1) interest rates for subloans would be between 5-9 1/2%, and
prior to the beginning of each planting season a schedule of
