which in consultation with appropriate Nigerian Authorities
and the Bank would apply the lending terms for the said
season (para 6.17); and
(m)  (i) NWS would cause the NSMB to pay particular attention
to its efficient produce buying within the project area,
and (ii) where necessary encourage groups of farmers to

(para 7.06).
9.03      Retroactive financing would be available for the employment of key
personnel (para 6.07) appointed as from date of loan negotiations amounting
to no more than US$150,000 (para 5.06).
9.04      Conditions of effectiveness of loan would be that:
(a) NWS would furnish the Bank with satisfactory evidence that

ANNEX 1
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
Gusau Agricultural Development Project
Farm and Crop Development
A. General
1.        The major crops in the project area are sorghum and millet, cotton,
groundnuts and cowpeas. Minor crops include maize, svgarcane, chilies and
okra. Production data for cotton and groundnuts are compiled from purchases
by the Northern States Marketing Board (NSMB) (Tables 1 and 2). No pro-
duction data are available for other crops; nor is there other than qualita-
tive information on farmers' yields, farm sizes or cropping patterns.
However, a detailed economic survey of three villages in a similaj,social
and ecological area has been carried out and published recently. -   For this
reason, the survey has been used as a basis for the formulation of assump-
tions on present and projected farming activities.
2.        Cattle, sheep and goats are kept throughout the project area by
farmers, and in substantially larger numbers by semi-nomadic pastoralists
(Fulani). The latter utilize fallow land (a rapidly diminishing resource),
forest and grazing reserves, and crop residues for their livestock. They
exist in an increasingly uneasy symbiosis with settled cultivators as
pressure on land increases with rising populations. Livestock management
systems are generally poor and productivity is low. There are no reliable
data on stock numbers.
3.        Cultivations are carried out mainly by hand hoe and cutlass.  How-
ever, a small, and unquantified number of farmers are known to own work
oxen and equipment. The MA tractor hiring service plows and ridges land
for farmers at subsidized rates but its overall impact is negligible.
B. The Influence of Climate on Cropping
4.        From May to October, the widest range between mean monthly maximum
and minimum temperatures is 1000 to 70° F (380 C to 210 C). At project area
altitudes of 600 to 900 m cold spells do not cause any problems and rainfall
is the major factor for limiting crop growth. Monthly and annual data for
1/   An Economic Survey of three villages in Zaria Province, Samaru
Miscellaneous Paper 37, 1972: D. W. Norman



ANNEX 1
Page 2
Cusau Agricultural Station are given at Table 3. Instead of presenting
these in the traditional manner, they have been arranged in rank order to
provide an estimate of probability. Thus the modal value (half above and
half below) provides a demonstrable 50% probability not normally associated
with arithmetic average. However, for a basically subsistence agriculture
a more stringent requirement is appropriate and an 80% probability has been
chosen. At this probability rainfall is still quite adequate for crop
growth though the farmer can expect no rain in April and October, and
therefore his cropping season, compared to other more favorable areas is
reduced from 6 to 5 months. Rainfall distribution is critical to favorable
production, and the bumper crop years have always been associated with
seasons with extended rainfall at the beginning and end of season. Basically
the farmer is a pretty busy fellow who has limited resources for carrying
out his land preparation and subsequent cultivation. Thus eight years out
of ten he can expect sufficient rain in I-lay to allow timely cultivation and
subsequent planting operations (see Table 4). If the sequence is broken
then late planting particularly of cotton and sorghum occurs, which result
in low yields. Thus provision of oxdrawm equipment should allow for more
timely land preparation. In addition to the ever present risk of shortfall
of rain, the farmer in the Gusau area must also expect heavy rainfall
especially in August. Therefore if the farmer has not been able to sow
early enough in May he would run the risk, with poor plant cover, of suffering
from severe soil erosion and run-off. Attention should be paid to improve-
ment in soil and water conservation practices.  As a final comment, the
distribution pattern of the actual past few years is worthy of attention.
Against the modal annual value of 35.7 in (907 mm) the last 5 years have
all been lower but not markedly so. In fact, one of the most productive
agricultural seasons, 1969-70, was from the quite modest annual total of
33 in (838 mm). But what has happened during the past 2 years is that
September rainfall in 1972 was the lowest for over 40 years, that is a
probability occurrence of only 2 1/2 times in 100. As if this was not
enough, the same probability applied to the May 1973 rainfall, rainfall
amounted to only 0.2 in for a month for which half of the year's exceeded
4 in. But examination of the actual yearly rainfall does support the view
that the area has been singularly unfortunate without, however, supporting
the view that the area is becoming drier.
C. Farming Systems
Farm Size
5.        Although no detailed surveys have been carried out, field observa-
tions tend to verify estimates based on population and proportion of cropped
land derived from study of aerial photography mosaics of the area. These
point to a mean farm size of 3.5 ha.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
Size of Farm Family
6.        The Norman study -/ reports an average farm family of 8.5.  This
is not considered to be typical of the project area, where local opinion
and published statistics point to an average farm family approximating 6.5.
Composition is assumed to be as follows:
Small      Large       Female       Male          Total
Child      Child       Adult        Adult       Residents
1.7        1.0         2.2          1.6           6.5
Labor Availability
7.        Labor availabilitT,in mandays per day, on the basis of manday
equivalents used by Norman - would be as follows:
Small     Large      Female      Male
Child     Child      Adult       Adult       Total
Farm family                  1.7       1.0        2.2         1.6         6.5
Manday equivalents            -        0.5        0.75        1.0
Available mandays             -        0.5        1.65        1.6         3.75
In the Zaria study -  male adult labor accounted for 80 percent
of the total family labor input; female adult labor was insignificant.
Thus, assuming 50 percent availability of labor from the large child in an
average farm family, daily labor availability would approximate 2.0 mandays.
8.        Labor requirements assessed by Norman 1/ on traditional methods
of cultivation, which imply mixed cropping and low yields, appear to be low
relative to data on similar crops grown under comparable conditions in other
areas. Substantially higher labor requirements have been assumed for pro-
posed project crops as shown in the imodel crop budgets (Tables 5 through 9).
9.        Adequate labor profiles are not available and, while it appears
that total labor availability (at 2.0 manday equivalents per day) is adequate
for the average small farm there may be seasonal labor shortages, particularly
at planting and weeding periods in some seasons. However, the project would
appeal primarily to the more vigorous and efficient farmers and for this
reason, labor availability is not regarded as a serious constraint to
development.
1/   op cit.



ANNEX 1
Page 4
Cropping Patterns
10.       Intercropping is commonly practiced in a wide variety of combin-
ations. The cropping pattern of a "typical" farm, on the basis of adjusted
areas of sole crops might be as follows (ha):
Sorghum & other Cereals              1.50
Cotton                               0.70
Groundnuts & Cowpeas                 0.70
Others & Fallow                      0.60
Total                          3.50
11.       This pattern may be expected to change as a result of the project
which would promote sole cropping and the introduction of improved produc-
tion practices for cotton, sorghum, maize, groundnuts and cowpeas, and would
result in changes in areas planted to different crops. Overall farm size
would not be affected by the project, however, the amount of land in fallow
each year is likely to decrease. This decrease in fallow is currently
resulting in marked decline in soil fertility, particularly in areas furthest
from the homesteads which receive no farm yard manure. Some of these defi-
ciencies would be made up in the short term by supply of fertilizers, and
in the long term through the introduction of pasture. A summary of changes
in cropping patterns follows:

12.       The anticipated effect of the project on the five crops selected
for development, viz cotton, sorghum, maize, groundnuts and cowpeas is
considered in subsequent paragraphs. Proposed improved practices are
defined, and their likely impact analyzed in terms of area, numbers of
growers and production on a crop by crop basis.
D. Cotton
Background
13.       Cotton of the improved Allen type (Gossypium hirsutum) has been
grown in the project area for the past half century. Production fluctuates
widely due to the effects of climate and producer price. The mean level in



ANNEX 1
Page 6
recent years approximates 9,000 tons of seed cotton, some 6 percent of mean
total production from the northern States (Table 1), and 33% of the Sokoto
Province production.
14.       Seed of improved varieties produced at the Institute of Agricultural
Research (IAR) at Samaru is bulked up at the Daudawa seed multiplication
farm (see map) before distribution to farmers throughout the northern States,
although some seed is now bulked at the Gusau seed multiplication farm.
15.       Seed supplies are provided free by the NSMB at a series of dumps
throughout the project area. Seed for planting is treated at the ginnery
against Bacterial Blight (Xanthomonanas malvacearum) which is endemic to
the area. Any one of three dressings: mercurial (3 percent) at 1:180;
copper (45 percent) at 1:180; or Bronopal (12 percent) at 1:170, are effec-
tive and may be used.
Variety
16.       The present variety, S71 has been selected to yield highly and
to meet the needs of the textile industry. Under farmers' conditions, yields
average no more that 210 kg of seed cotton per ha, about 10% of the variety's
yield potential. Its main characteristics are shown below:
Ginning percentage                        34 to 36
Fibre length (32nd of an inch)            31 to 33
Fibre strength (g per ton)                20.9
Yarn strength (40's) CSP /1               2386
/1   Count Strength Product.
Recommended Production Practices
17.       Research carried out by IAR at Samaru and at outstations has given
rise to a set of recommended practices for the crop. These presuppose a
low level of technical assistance to farmers and do not reflect optimal
production conditions. More advanced techniques including the use of ultra
low volume (ULV) spraying for insect control are being tested under farmers'
conditions at several sites in the northern States. They are likely to be
fully approved for use on a field scale under the conditions proposed for
the project in the near future. Given adherence to the full package of
improved practices outlined below, average yields of seed cotton for farmers
using the package are predicted to approximate 630 kg/ha.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
18.       Land should be ridged on the contour at 0.91 m (3 ft) centers, and
20-25 cm (8-10 in) high. Ridges should be completed to allow for planting
in mid June. 6-8 seeds should be sown 2.4 cm (1 in) deep at 45 cm (18 in)
along the top of each ridge. 100 kg of boronated single superphosphate
should be applied broadcast before ridging (0.7 kg/ha of boron). At the
relative low yield of 630 kg/ha projected for project farmers, it would be
uneconomic to apply nitrogenous fertilizer, as under the prevailing climatic
conditions the yield response to nitrogen is limited. However, some of the
better farmers, who plant early, might apply 100 kg/ha of calcium ammonium
nitrate, as a top dressing, three weeks after planting. Plants should be
thinned to two per stand about three weeks after planting and preferably
afte- a good rain, to minimize the effects of root disturbance. Weeding and
ridge maintenance should be undertaken before weed competition is serious.
Normally three weedings are required.
19.       The cotton crop is attacked by a range of pests at different
stages in the growing period.
(a) Period between germination and flowering:
No major pests, Aphis gossypii may occur but seldom
build up to serious proportions. Empoasca spp occur
but as the cotton is bred for jassid resistance no
serious damage is caused.
(b) Period of flowering and boll growth:
Pests of vegetation include the mirid Campylomma which
can be serious, Lygus which is less serious, Sylepta
derogata (F) (the cotton leaf roller), various grass-
hoppers, leaf hoppers, and leaf eating beetles occur in
August and September and are of varying seriousness
depending on the season. Pests of the flowers and bolls
include stainers, mainly Dysdercus superstitiousus (F)
but also D. Fasciatus, and the red bollworm Diparopsis
watersi (Roths) which are usually more serious than the
American bollworm Heliothis armigera (Hb), the spiny
bollworm Earias insulana (Boisd) and Earias biplaga (Wlk),
the False codling moth Cryptophlebia leucotreta (Meyr) and
the pink bollworm Pectinophora gossypiella (Sound).
(c) Pests of bolls after the rains:
Bollworms (particularly the red) and adult stainers
increase until the bolls have matured.
20.       Ideally insecticidal applications should be timed according to
pest identification in the field. Since spotting of eggs demands considerable
skill and where pest identification may not occur, a precautionary spray



ANNEX 1
'Page 8
starting at 6 weeks after germination would ensure the protection of the
earlier bolls. Thereafter a succession of sprays would be undertaken and
it is likely that the advanced farmers would repeat the sprayings about
6 - 8 times.
21.       Harvesting should begin in October and be continued at regular
intervals to prevent boll spoilage. Picking should aim to avoid dirt, trash
and stained or damaged lint in order to qualify seed cotton for purchase as
Grade 1. All plants have to be uprooted and burnt by March 1, and no cotton
can be planted until June 1.
Project Proposals
22.       Increased production is expected to derive partly from increased
area planted but mainly from the adoption of new techniques.
23.       Area expansion over the project period is expected to be from
42,000 ha in 1975 to 50,000 ha in 1979, an increase of 4 percent annually.
This would come mainly from natural population growth but there would be
some area redistribution. It is estimated that the average farmer currently
grows 0.7 ha of cotton. At the end of the project period those using
"advanced" techniques are expected to handle about an average of 1.5 ha,
and those continuing to use traditional methods 0.5 ha, providing an overall
average of 0.83 ha per farmer.
24.       Adoption of new techniques would be at one level.  The use of sole
cropping, fertilizer and ULV spraying for insect control is described as
"improved". Such measures would result in yields averaging 630 kg seed
cotton per ha. Since the rainfall distribution is more critical than some
other areas in northern Nigeria, it is unlikely that many farmers will ever
plant early, as food crops have priority and are planted first. Currently
little or no spraying is carried out due to lack of supply of inputs and
very limited and sparsely located supplies of water required for the
conventional high volume water based insecticides. During the last three
seasons ultra low volume sprayers (ULV) have been tested within the project
area and have proven successful. They are also being used in an IDA finan-
ced project in Malawi, as well as in other East African countries with
success. In East Africa the option for the use of high volume sprayers
exist; in the project area, due to difficulties in locating good groundwater
any large expansion of production using high volume spraying techniques is
out of the question. During the 1971/72 season in Mozambique 11,000 ha
of ULV sprayed cotton averaged 666 Kg/ha as against 36,000 ha of unsprayed
cotton averaging 262 Kg/ha. The insecticide to be used would be endosulphan,
and would be applied at a rate of 3 liter/ha per spray.
25.       Nlajority of farmers plant late and as such even under ideal
conditions of management, the cotton crop would not yield at optimum levels
since the top bolls would not set. However, in order to assure the setting



ANNEX 1
Page 9
of the bottom bolls it is essential that the farmer commences spraying
before the flower buds appear. As a crop insurance this should be at 5 weeks
after germination and continue at 10 day intervals for as much as 6 weeks,
but depending on the likelihood of continued crop production confined almost
entirely to the setting of the bottom bolls. The following table sets out
the proposed levels of technology:
Level of Technology       Traditional        Improved
Variety                 Improved           Improved S 71
Pl-it population/ha    18,000              36,000
Fertilizer
application/ha          nil              50 kg boronated single
superphosphate

Tables 5 and 10 provide per hectare budget, and phasing of uptake of
improved practices.
E. Sorghum
Background
26.       Sharing with bulrush millet, 1/ the role of the traditional grain
staple of the Hausa people, sorghum is grown widely throughout the project
area. White to yellowish grained, open headed Guinea race types, known
locally as Farafara, predominate. The grain is normally ground to a coarse
flour and eaten as porridge. The stalks, which are seldom less than four
meters in height, are used for a variety of purposes of which building and
fencing are among the most important.
1/        Research work to date has yielded little information on methods for
improving yields of millet, and existing varieties do not show
economic responses to fertilizer, therefore the project would not
at this stage take action to improve the production of millet.



ANNEX 1
Page 10
27.       Under the traditional system of cultivation, mean yields are said
to approximate 600 kg of threshed grain per ha, but they vary widely in
response to intensity of intercropping, soil and climatic effects. No data
are available on sorghum production in the project area. Nevertheless, it
is safe to conclude that the crop is grown on all holdings, alone or in a
variety of mixtures.
28.       Norman's studiesl/ indicate that sorghum occupied approximately
30% of total area planted and, whilst most commonly grown in a two crop
combination with bulrush millet, was also planted alone or with up to four
additional crops. Using this as a basis, it can be calculated that some
75,000 ha of sorghum are planted in the project area which, at an average
yield of 600 kg/ha, would produce some 45,000 tons or about 7% of estimated
production from North Western State.
29.       Although its effects have not been quantified, the parasitic weed
Striga hermontheca is probably the most significant pest of sorghum. Others
include a range of stalk borers, but particularly Busseola fusca, and Sorghum
Midge Contarinia sorghicola. Major diseases include Downy Mildew, Sclerospora
sorghi, a variety of leaf diseases causing spotting and striping, and a
number of smut diseases, mainly of the genus Sphacelotheca. The latter are
to a considerable extent controlled by seed dressing with copper fungicides
which are readily available commercially.
30.       As befits a staple grain, sorghum is normally planted on the main
farm in close proximity to the homestead. Prior to planting land is plowed
and ridged by hand hoe or occasionally mechanically, at approximately 0.9
meters between rows and varying distances within rows according to inter-
cropping practices. Sorghum is planted in the project area in May/June,
after such crops as early millet. Two or three weedings and earthings-up
are normally carried out. The crop flowers at the onset of the dry season,
in late September/early October, and matures, utilizing residual soil
moisture, by late November/December.
31.       The stalks are cut near ground level and stacked in the field after
removal of the heads, which are bundled and stored in mud plastered granaries
(rumbus) in the farm compound. Heads are removed from the granaries as
required for domestic consumption or for sale following threshing. Storage
losses are variable, one estimate is that on average 20% of the crop is lost
in store, and though control methods involving granary sanitation and the
use of insecticides have been developed, they are not commonly used.
Recommended Practices
32.       Research on sorghum has been carried out in the northern States of
Nigeria for the past 20 years. Higher yielding varieties have been selected
from local types. A number of dwarf varieties and hybrids have also been
developed. This work has been accompanied by investigations including time
_/   op. cit.



ANNEX 1
Page 11
of planting, spacing, and fertilizer responses. Improved local varieties
grown under improved agronomic practices showed yield gains over tradi-
tional types of six to 25 percent (averaging 18.6% or about 240 kg/ha) in
over 90 trials planted at different sites in main sorghum production areas
up to 1970. Such increases are of little interest to farmers. The main
thrust of sorghum research is now directed towards production of high yielding
dwarf varieties and hybrids. Material now available has a yield potential
under field conditions of around 2,500 kg/ha and 4,000 kg/ha from dwarf
varieties and hybrids respectively. The grain from these types is not yet
widely acceptable for human food in rural areas but it can be sold readily
in urban areas for human and livestock food.
Project Proposals
33.       Total sorghum production in Nigeria has been estimated to approx-
imate four million tons per year, of which between five and ten percent is
marketed. Prices have risen overall in the past three years. However, any
major production increase could be expected to result in a marked fall in
price. This situation is unlikely to arise in the near future - in the
light of the ineffectiveness of existing extension, input supply and credit
services in the northern States as a whole. Within the proposed project
area, however, there are good opportunities for promoting some increase in
sorghum production, utilizing available technology and proposed project
services, despite the fact that many farmers are likely to continue to
grow at least some local sorghum under traditional cultivation methods. In
addition to the switch from traditional to improved varieties of sorghum
that is envisaged, it is also believed that a substantial amount of land
which in the past has been used to grow sorghum will, under the project, be
used for maize, see para 38.
34.       One level of improved technology has been assumed based upon
results from field trials and research recommendations.

F. Maize
Background
36.       Although well known, maize is presently a minor crop in the project
area. Cultivated mainly in crop mixtures on swampy areas (fadamas) primarily
as a vegetable for eating green - boiled or baked on the cob - it is unlikely
that total area exceeds 1,000 ha.
37.       High yielding, short season maize varieties and hybrids have been
developed recently by IAR and other researchers in the northern States.
Their promising performance under Northern Guinea zone conditions have
created marked interest in maize as a grain crop for the area. Trials at
Gusau under different levels of plant population and fertilizer rates
using variety (096) Bulk 3 gave yields ranging from 1,500 to over 5,600 kg/ha
of threshed grain depending on the level of treatments applied. Assuming
a discount from research findings to farmer field of 50%, this suggests an
obtainable farmer yield between 750 and 2,800 kg/ha.
Project Proposals
38.       These facts and the observation that the yellow grained varieties
produce meal which is an acceptable (some say a preferred) substitute for
sorghum meal in traditional porridge in North Western State, indicate that
the probability that the maize area and production could be increased
markedly under the project. Recent trends in maize prices, the burgeoning
urban population in the southern States, and the increasing demand for
livestock feeds and for industrial maize products provide further justifica-
tion for maize promotion under the project. Probable area increases are
expected to be at the expense of sorghum and millet, and would occur mainly
on the farms of more progressive farmers. The expected change in maize area
is shown in Table 12. This indicates an expected increase in maize area
from 1,000 to 12,500 ha of which 10,000 ha would be grown at improved levels
on plots averaging 1 ha, and 2,500 ha (20 percent) would be grown under
traditional systems on 0.10 ha plots. About 22,500 farmers are expected to
grow maize within the project (Table 7 shows per hectare budget).
39.       Levels of technology selected for application in the project area
have been based on field trials and practices advocated by workers at the
IAR. They are summarized in the following table.

Page 13

G. Groundnuts
Background
40.       Groundnuts (Arachie hypogea) are grown throughout the project area
but are of greatest commercial importance in drier, northern locations which
are climatically less well suited to cotton production. Production levels
may be inferred from purchases by the NSMB which have averaged 16,000 tons
annually in recent years (Table 2).
41.       The Nigerian groundnut crop is grown primarily for oil and average
kernels contain about 47% oil with a Free Fatty Acid (FFA) content of 2 to
3 percent. Yields of groundnuts grown under traditional systems, which
include multiple intercropping, wide spacing and late planting, vary con-
siderably but are probably of the order of 425 kg/ha of shelled nuts. At
this yield level and accepting the above production assumption, area plante,
on a sole crop basis is about 37,000 ha. The high labor requirement and
the present comparatively unattractive return from the crop probably limit
groundnut plot sizes to about 0.6-0.7 ha on farms which grow the crop for
sale to the NSMB.
42.       A long series of agronomic trials and a number of selection and
breeding programs by staff of the IAR have resulted in the formulation of
a package of recommended practices for groundnut production. These regularly
lead to a doubling of kernel yields over traditional practices. More



ANNEX 1
Page 14
advanced technology, including the use of fungicidal sprays to control
cercospora leaf spot disease is still under investigation and may be
available for dissemination in the near future. However, a number of
questions still attach to the technical and economic viability of certain
advanced techniques.
43.       For these reasons, only one level of improved technology has been
suggested. This approximates to the practices recommended by ERLS. The
practices proposed, which are outlined below, would be fostered by the pro-
vision of a physical package of inputs including seed, seed dressing,
fertilizer and advice. This would follow the lines of a successful groundnut
seed production project in neighboring Kano State.
44.       The proposed level of improved technology would incorporate timely
planting, i.e. shortly after the first rains, on land which has been prepared
as for cotton (para 18). Fertilizer, single superphosphate at 75 kg per ha,
should be applied as a band in the old furrows before splitting to make new
ridges. Seed would be of the variety S61 which should be shelled shortly
before planting and dressed with Aldran T or Fernasan D against seed and soil
borne diseases. Seed should be planted at two every 23 cm (9 in) on the
top of the ridges i.e., a seed rate of 40 kg/ha. By so doing, the foliar
cover achieved would be invaluable in preventing run off during the heavy
rainfall in August. Furthermore, after the early weeding and earthing, such
ground cover would reduce the frequency of weeding which must cease once
pegging 1/ starts. Harvesting should begin immediately the crop reaches
maturity.  Pod damage should be avoided by careful lifting.  The crop should
be dried by inverting plants on the ridge i.e. with pods uppermost for about
six days. Thereafter the pods should be handpicked and transported to an
appropriate site for decortication. Care is needed, particularly in lifting,
drying and picking to avoid the creation of conditions favoring the develop-
ment of the fungus Aspergillus flavus, responsible for the carcingogen,
aflatoxin, which has been transmitted on mouldy groundnuts.
Project Proposals
45.       The improved groundnut technology proposed for the project is
already well established. Its rate of spread has been limited largely by
the inadequacy of technical assistance and input supply services. For these
reasons, the rate of uptake under the project is expected to be rapid. By
Year 5 (1979), some 20,000 farmers (44 percent) are expected to be usilng
the improved technology.
1/   Shoots which eventually bear the groundnut turn down into the soil
prior to pod formation.

H. Cowpeas
Background
46.       Cowpeas (vigna unguiculata) occupy an important place in tradi-
tional Nigerian diets both in the North and South. They provide valuable
vegetable protein for subsistence farmers and some cash income when surplus
production is sold. The crop is seldom planted sole and normally forms one
part of a number of crop mixtures of varying complexity. Under these
circumstances yields vary widely. Further, because of the range of insect
pests to which it is susceptible both before and after flowering, yields
rarely average more than 200 kg/ha of shelled peas. Local varieties have
both upright and spreading characteristics; those producing a pale (whitish)
colored seed are preferred.
47.       Research on the crop has isolated major insect pests and established
the importance of their control for increased production. Major pests
include Ootheca mutalilis, a leaf eating beetle, Alcidodes leucogrammus, a
weevil which, in both the adult and larval forms, causes stem girdling,
Maruca testulalis, the larva of which bore into and feed on young flowers
causing them to drop, Rezotrachelus varium, a weevil which lays its eggs
in green pods wherein the larvae feed and pupate within the seeds. Further,
a number of bugs in the order Hemiptera feed on green pods causing premature
drying and shrivelling.
48.       Improved practices, including sole cropping, the use of improved
upright varieties, phosphatic fertilizer and insecticidal spraying result
in ten fold yield increases over traditional yields. The latter treatment
on sole cropped cowpeas is the most significant. Present recommendations



ANNEX 1
Page 16
are for six weekly sprays containing 20 percent DDT and two percent gamma
BHC at the rate of 5.5 liters of concentrate mixed in 109 liters of water
per ha. This recommendation is under review following investigation of
ULV teclniques.
Project Proposals
49.       No data are available on the area presently planted with cowpeas
but it is unlikely that the crop is grown widely in sole stand. The project
would introduce cowpeas as a sole crop to those farmers who wished to grow
cotton using improved and advanced techniques since such farmers would
already own ULV sprayers and be competent in their use. It is not expected
that large plots would be planted, and a mean size of 0.25 ha has been
assumed.
50.       One level of technology is proposed.  Its main element is summarized
below and is compared with the traditional system.
Traditional       Improved
Cropping                     Mixed            Sole
Variety                      Local            Local
Type                      Upright or          Upright
spreading

recommended by IAR

shelled peas (kg/ha)
51.       It is expected that uptake of improved technology would closely
follow cotton development since materials, equipment and techniques for
spraying cowpeas approximate those used for cotton, and the benefits are
considerable. For these reasons it is expected that most farmers with
one season's experience of spraying cotton would also grow cowpeas with
project advice and assistance. Furthermore, a small proportion could be
expected to introduce improved technology to their cotton and cowpea crops
simultaneously (for detailed budget and crop projections see Tables 9 and
14).

ANNEX 2
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Area, Institutions and Exipting Processing and Marketing
A.   Project Area
1.        Occupying a rectangular block of land on a South East/North West
axis between latitudes 11040' and 12040' N and longitudes 6020' and 7010' E,
the project area covers some 3,800 km2or about two percent of the total area
of the State. It comprises about half of the Gusau and Kaura Namoda Develop-
ment Areas within the Sokota Administrative District in the north eastern
part of North Western State. The project area comprises the whole of Kaura
Namoda, Chafe and Kotorkoshi districts, and those parts of Gusau and Bungudu
districts north of the Sokoto river (see map).
2.        Climate.  Data over the past 40 years at Gusau show that for half
of the years (50 percent probability), rainfall exceeds 37.5 in k(952 mm).
Considering that this is confined to the period April to October, it would
seem adequate to grow a range of short-term annual crops. However, the
within season distribution imposes some constraints which are considered in
Annex 1.
3.        Since the project area cannot be sure of receiving adequate rain-
fall in April and October, the entire crop cycle must be carried out in the
shorter season of May to September for which at 80 percent probability the
respective monthly totals are 2.0, 3.0, 5.4, 9.0 and 5.2 inches. With such
distribution it is evident that timely sowing of the crop followed by adequate
soil and water conservation practices are even more important than they are
at the projects in the North Central and North Eastern States.
4.        Water.  There are few perennial streams in the area, and ground-
water supplies are severely limited by the presence of the impervious under-
lying basement complex. With increasing pressure on land, the flow period
of streams has diminished markedly. Wells commonly fail during the dry
months, leading to prolonged water shortages which are becoming an increas-
ingly critical issue for both human and livestock populations. Fortunately,
topography is such that construction of small reservoirs appears a practicable
solution to the water problem.
5.        Topography and Soils.  The area is underlain by rocks of the base-
ment complex, which is predominantly granite. The landform is gently un-
dulating at an altitude of 600 to 900 m with occasional granite inselbergs.
Many small, ephemeral streams occur throughout the area.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
6.        Leached ferruginous tropical soils are common with well developed
sub-soil horizons containing some 30X clay. Drainage ranges from good to
rather poor. Surface capping is general throughout the area.
7.        Soils are weakly acid, with pH in the range 5.5 to 6.5 and are
deficient in nitrogen and available phosphate, particularly after prolonged
cropping. Attempts to maintain the fertility of farms close to settlements
is made by using animal manure at between two to three tons per ha. Recent
investigations have demonstrated an apparent boron deficiency and its solu-
tion, by the use of boronated superphosphate. Fertilizer responses by most
major crops have been established with some precision at low application
rates. Field trials involving higher levels of application, mainly for
cereals such as maize and sorghum, are presently underway in the project area.
8.        Vegetation.  Natural vegetation is savanna woodland typified by
Isoberlinia doka in association with grasses mainly of Andropogon and
Hyparrhenia species. Over much of the area the savanna has been replaced
following clearing and farming, by parkland dominated by such trees as
Parkia clappertoniana and Adansonia digitata (Baobab). These trees are pro-
tected by law and provide valuable dietary supplements from seeds and leaves
respectively. In wetter areas, which have been heavily farmed, spear grass
(Imperata cylindrica) is a troublesome weed. Additionally, the parasitic
weed Striga hemontheca causes significant yield reductions in sorghum.
9.        Population.  The project area is occupied by people of the Hausa
tribe, most of whom are devout moslems. Literacy in English, Nigeria's
official language, and Hausa, the vernacular, is low among adults in rural
areas, but is rising rapidly with the younger generation. Available data
are scant. The last census, from which data are available was carried out in
1963 and remains the best source of population data until the results of
the December 1973 census become available in detail later in 1974. Such
data will be of immeasurable value in detailed project planning and evaluation.
The only other possible source available at present, Local Authority returns
on numbers of tax payers, whilst collected annually vary in accuracy in
accordance with the diligence of the different officials charged with tax
collection responsibilities in the districts. Information from such sources
indicates only 60-80 percent of the adult male population that has been
imputed from census data. According to the census, population of the five
districts constituting the project area totalled 470,500 in 1963. Using a
growth rate of 2.5 percent annually, population of the project area currently
should be about 620,000.
10.       Data on numbers of persons per family in the project area are
also sparse. No demographic surveys have been carried out. However, indi-
cations from tax data point to an average of 4.72. Local opinion does not
confirm this, suggesting that family size approximates 6.5 individuals.
This figure in turn is lower than that obtained in a detailed survey of
three villages in Zaria Province, some 120 km south of the project area. The
latter arrived at a mean figure of 8.5 persons per family. This is sub-
stantially higher than the figure suggested by the Federal Office of



ANNEX 2
Page 3
Statistics for Zaria Province as a whole. Because of the spread between
family size derived from tax data on the one hand, and a microsample
in a neighboring province on the other, 6.5 was arbitrarily selected as
representing average family size. This was in accordance with local opinion
and falls between the extremes otherwise suggested. Thus, on the basis of
a family size of 6.5, and a population of 620,000, there aare now estimated
to be some 95,400 families in the project area.
11.       Numbers of farming families were calculated on the assumption that
about 28 percent would be engaged primarily in non-agricultural activities.
This figure was derived from census data and observations on non-agricultural
activities in the area. On this basis, it is currently estimated that there
are about 68,000 farming families in the project area.
12.       The population is divided between five districts of Sokoto Adminis-
trative Division and resides in 40 village areas. The estimated situation
in 1974 is as shown below:
District                No. Village Area            Population
Gusau                           6                     179,000
Kotorkoshi                      4                      40,500
Chafe                           6                     100,500
Bungudu                         8                      75,000
Kaura Namoda                   16                     225,000
TOTAL                   40                     6202000
13.       The social structure of the area is significant because of the
relative strength and influence of the hierarchical structure of the Local
Authority. The project comes within the jurisdiction of the Sultan of
Sokoto whose hereditary authority is delegated through salaried district
heads and village heads.
14.       Among their other functions, village heads are responsible for
village level administration of land tenure arrangements. The system is
based on Islamic law and on customary usufructory rights. In theory, it
provides for the allocation of such rights to land which is not yet so
allocated, to any native Nigerian who expresses the intention of settling
and using land within the village area. In practice, most land has been
allocated to families, the heads of which suballocate to family members in
accordance with assessed need and capability.
15.       As representatives of the Local Authority, village heads have con-
siderable political influence within their areas. Although this influence
is being eroded in urban areas, such as the wards of Gusau town, it is still
considerable in the villages. For this reason, every effort would be made to
obtain the support of village heads for the project by acquainting them with
proposed and ongoing developments within their areas. District heads would



ANNEX 2
Page 4
be similarly consulted and informed. Furthermore, as indicated in Chapter VI,
provision is made for Local Authority representation on the Project Committee.
16.       Farm Size.  Information on farm size is scant.  It is possible to
impute a possible mean farm size on the basis of assumed population, family
size and estimated area of cultivated land.  The latter figure is estimated
by NWS at 238,000 ha, 62% of total project area. This comparatively high
figure is based on a series of random traverses across photo mosaics of about
half of the project area, which yielded a somewhat more intensive (66 percent)
land use pattern: given 68,000 farm families (para. 11) then average size
of cultivated holding including fallow would be 3.5 ha.
17.       Cropping Patterns.  Cropping patterns are dominated by sorghum and
millet as food grains, and cotton and groundnuts as the major cash crops.
A diversity of minor crops are grown. Local knowledge and socio-economic
surveys in neighboring parts of the northern States indicate that sorghum
and millet typically occupy 45-55% of cultivated areas, cotton and groundnuts
about 16-20% each. Minor crops, usually grown in mixtures with the main
cereals and cash crops, include maize, chilies, kenaf, okra and a range of
vegetables.
Communications
18.       The project area is served by two main roads (see map).  The trunk
highway connecting the north west with Lagos passes through its southern
parts; a branch road to Kaura Namoda and Katsina starts at Gusau and serves
the northern and western pares, These are tarred and are maintained by the
Federal and State Governmenta. In addition, the Nigerian Railway Corporation
has a spur line from the main northern route, which branches at Zaria and
runs via Funtun and Gusat  to Kaura Namoda in the north west corner cf the
area.  This l4txe runs parallel with the road through the west of the project
area.  There io an airport n exrated by the Federal Government at Oisru but
no flights re currently scheduled. Telephone facilities exist at Gusau,
operated by     Posts and Telegraphs Department of the Federal Ministry of
Communications,
19.       Unfortunately, the basic communication network barely extends
beyond that described above.  Minor roads are non-existent or in poor repzsir
Communities are seasonally cut off from each other and the outside world.
Transportation of produce and inputs to rural areas is both difficult and
expensive. Attempts to improve the situation by self help activities and
by the Development Area Works Department are hampered by shortages of funds-
and competent executive capacity.
B. Institutions
20.       Institutions that would be concerned with the project and their
relevant responsibilities are described in the following paragraphs.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
21.       The Ministry of Agriculture (MA).  MA would have responsibility for
carrying out the project. The Ministry has representatives of its four technical
divisions within the project area, and it operates a cotton seed multiplication
farm at Gusau ksee map).  While the functions of its Field Services and Produce
Inspection Divisions within the area would be incorporated into the project
organization, the administration of the two other Divisions would remain un-
changed. Thus, animal health, organization of grazing facilities 1/ and
livestock improvement services, largely for the semi-nomadic Fulani pastoralists,
would continue to be provided by the Veterinary Division and the Forestry Divi-
sion would continue its work, which is concerned mainly with the regulation of
reserved land, and planting of woodlots. These functions would be largely
unaffected by the project.
22.       The Ministry of Works (MOW).  Responsible for State-financed public
works program, MOW has limited facilities and a small technical staff de-
ployed within the project area; MOW Is concerned mainly with road maintenance,
building construction, and the maintenance of State-financed structures.
Executive capacity relative to the State construction program is a major
limitant to its operations.
23.       The Ministry of Health (MOH).  Responsible for all aspects of human
health in the State, MOH is in process of developing its services and facil-
ities. Within the project area, a general hospital provides treatment ser-
vices for both in-and out-patients at Gusau. This and a number of planned
Rural Health Centers will provide the referral link between the sparse network
of district dispensaries, and the specialized hospital facilities operated
and planned by MOR at the State headquarters, Sokoto.  It is expected that
additional Rural Health Centers and a closer network of dispensaries providing
more comprehensive services are to be included in the forthcoming development
plan (1974-1978) of MOH.
24.       Ministry of Industry, Trade and Cooperatives (MITC) provides
services to industrial investors and is involved, for example, with the
de-eigepment of the new oil seed crushing mill being constructed at Gusau.
More relevantly, it is responsible for the administration of cooperative
societies within the State. In common with other northern States, cooperatives
in North Western have made little impact on the bulk of the rural population.
Success has been hampered by shortage of support and supervisory staff, and
by political interference in the early to mid-1960's leading to widespread
indebtedness.
25.       Primary producer and marketing societies organized in the project
area at this time formed the Zamfara Cooperative Union. Neither the primary
societies nor the union gained the full support of farmers. In common with
the cooperative movement elsewhere, these groups are to a greater or less
extent indebted to the State Government for preseason and marketing loans and
1/   This aspect would be affected by the Bank's proposed Livestock Project
appraisal report 127-UNI which includes provision for improvement of
facilities and services to pastoralists utilizing a grazing reserve in
the north west corner of the project area.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
are consequently inoperative. For these reasons, little emphasis is placed
on the cooperative movement in proposed credit and marketing arrangements
for the project.
26.       The Sokoto Local Authority (SLA).  The project area comes under
the control of the Sokoto Local Authority and the Gusau and Kaura Namoda
Development Boards. The main functions of the former include the collection
of tax in respect of people and cattle (haraji and jangali respectively);
the administration of farming land allocations; the latter is charged with
provision of services such as feeder road construction and maintenance, dis-
pensaries, and primary education as a complement to State Government activities
in these fields.
27.       One function of the SLA is of immediate and direct importance to the
project. It relates to the need for open support and encouragement of
project activities and the cooperation of SLA officials with project manage-
ment. Project concept and proposed activities were discussed 'at several
levels with the SLA (including a meeting with the Sultan of Sokoto). Re-
actions were favorable and full cooperation was promised. An important function
of the two Development Area Boards relates to the maintenance of the road
network which the project would construct. In the course of the develop-
ment period the road construction unit of the project would liaise closely
with the Works Department of the Development Area Boards in order to foster
a smooth handover of responsibility at full development.
28.       The Institute of Agricultural Research (IAR).  Created in the
early 1960's from the Research Division of the then Northern Nigeria Ministry
of Agriculture, IAR is a semi-autonomous body linked with Ahmadu Bello
University (ABU). Its governing body has representation from the Ministries
of Agriculture and Natural Resources of the six northern States. Its main
research station is located at Samaru, near Zaria, some 120 km from Gusau.
Further details on the organization and output of IAR are in Annex 3.
29.       IAR preserves contacts with headquarters and field staff of >A.
Field trials are carried out on an agency basis on MA sites, supervised
by ministry staff under the technical guidance of IAR researchers. Research
recommendations are conveyed to extension staff via the Extension Research
Liaison Service (ERLS) of IAR.
30.       While IAR has directed a major part of its resources to fundamental
plant breeding in the search for improved high yielding varieties, which
must continue, it must do more to find solutions to the practical everyday
problems of small farmers who struggle with seasonal limitations of labor
and severe shortages of capital and other resources. A practical program
of applied research aimed at identifying and resolving farmers' problems
would make IAR's program more immediately useful to the people it is intended
to serve.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
31.       Nigerian Agricultural Bank (NAB).  NAB is a Federal Government
insitution which was inaugurated in March 1973, with head offices in Kaduna.
UNDP has provided financial assistance to employ consultants to plan the
role of NAB and to manage it for three years; the Bank is executive agency.
Four expatriate senior management staff, including the General ianager,
have been employed; and Nigerian officers have been recruited.
32.       Northern States Marketing Board (NSMB).  NSMB is responsible for
organizing the domestic purchase of controlled crops, cotton and groundnuts
at prices predetermined by FMC. As from the 1973/74 season, NSMB became
purchasing agent of the Federal Nigerian Produce Marketing Company (NPMC),
under a new FMG program to improve crop purchasing. Crop purchases take
place at legally gazetted markets by Licensed Buying Agents (LBA) of NSMB.
In 1972, there were 34 cotton markets and 27 groundnut buying points with-
in the project area.
33.       Cotton and Agricultural Processors Ltd.  (CAP).  CAP (formerly the
British Cotton Growers' Association (Nigeria) Ltd.)) has had a monopoly for
ginning Nigerian cotton since exports began over half a century ago. CAP
has ginneries throughout the cotton growing zones of the northern States.
In addition to ginning and lint classification, CAP arranges for seed dressing
against Bacterial Blight, seed distribution to village dumps, and transporta-
tion of lint and seed as directed by NStMB. CAP is efficient and capable of
expanding its operations to meet the needs of the project. The present
ginning agreement expires in 1976, and CAP is exploring with State Govern-
ments the possibilities of greater local participation in the industry.
C. Processing and Marketing
34.       Cotton.  All cotton purchased for NSSMB in the project area is
ginned at CAP facilities located either at Gusau and Kaura Namoda (see mal
The Kaura Namoda ginnery also processes cotton produced outside the projec.
area. Some increase in effective capacity would probably be needed towards
the end of the project, and CAP has indicated its willingness to make thle
necessary arrangements, in consultation with MA and NSNB.
35.       The gazetted cotton markets usually are temporary structures made
from grass mats, and are located on the basis of anticipated purchases
(minimum 50 tons/year) and distance between markets (minimum 8 km). A
minimum of two LBA is mandatory for each market, which is controlled by a
Produce Examiner (PE) from the Produce Inspection Division (PID) of the MA.
The PE are responsible for the visual grading of seed cotton into one of two
grades, and should ensure that LBA pay correct amounts to farmers for their
produce. The PE are severely criticized as they seldom perform these
functions; apparently grading is virtually non-existent, and farmers
allege that many PE conspire with LBA to cheat farmers. The project would
seek to correct these deficiencies.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
36.       Cotton Seed.  Currently cotton seed is dressed against fungal
infection by CAP and is returned to the farmer. In the handling processes,
enormous losses occur which could be substantially reduced were an acid
delinting process employed. Through this, the seed germination rate would
be improved, thus requiring less seed to be returned to the farmer and re-
leasing a greater surplus of seed for the crushing industry. NSMB would
request CAP to install acid delinter equipment at their gin in Gusau; CAP
have agreed in principal to this suggestion.
37.       Groundnuts.  All groundnuts purchased for NSMB are inspected an
graded by PID staff. Normally, this takes place after the purchases from
farmers have been made by LBA at buying points licensed by SLA. Groundnut
buying points like cotton markets, are not properly supervised by PID and
there are frequent allegations that LBA manipulate weigh scales and prices
at the expense of farmers.
38.       Other Crops.  Control is normally not exercised on the marketing
or handling of other crops, such as sorghum, maize and cowpeas. However, SLA
has the power, if agreed by NWS, to restrict movement of foodcrops in times
of shortage. Such powers have been invoked in poor seasons from time to time.
39.       There is no national food crop market as such, but rather a series
of fairly highly organized, but disconnected, local centers. Consequently,
markets are thin and marked temporal and spatial price irregularities occur.
The project would aim to improve matters by the introduction of a market in-
telligence service, increasing farmers bargaining power through crop assembly,
and providing marketing advice and where appropriate helping farmers groups
to enter into contracts with large consumers such as livestock feed producers
and commercial starch factories.

Research and Seed Multiplication
A. Research Implementation
Institution
1.        Technical and rural economic research pertaining to the project
area is the responsibility of the semi-autonomous Institute of Agricultural
Research (IAR). The Institute was created in the early 1960's and is
linked to the Ahmadu Bello University (ABU) at Zaria. It has its main
station and administrative headquarters at Samaru, adjoining the ABU campus,
some 120 km south of proposed project headquarters at Gusau. IAR serves
the six northern States. Senior officials from their Ministries of Agri-
culture and Natural Resources (MANR) help to formulate IAR policy and
approve research programs.
2.        The Institute has a staff of over 150 professionals, and modern
and expanding facilities. It has established apparently good working
relations with other research institutions in Nigeria and the West African
region. Its standing in research circles is acknowledged to be high. Over
the years it has produced a wide range of research results relating to most
of the crops commonly grown in the northern States. Implementation of the
results has been hampered by lack of technical assistance and input delivery
services at the village and farmer levels; and a tendency for research to
be unrelated to the conditions encountered on farmers' farms. The latter
weakness has been high-lighted recently as a result of the activities of
the Agricultural Economics Department of IAR, whose staff are cooperating
with technical personnel in the formulation of research recommendations
more nearly related to farmers' needs and capabilities than has hitherto
been the case.
3.        Responsibility for transmission of research results to extension
staff is vested in the Extension Research Liaison Service (ERLS) of IAR.
Headed by a deputy Director of the Institute, ERLS produces a range of
audio and visual aids materials suitable for use by farmer contact extension
staff.  The Service is responsible for formulating draft recommended
practices for farmers. These are based on research results and an assess-
ment of the level of supervision and technical competence of field level



ANNEX 3
Page 2
extension staff employed by the State MANR's. This is generally low,
hence recommended practices tend to be simple and conservative in order to
avoid the hazard of loss of confidence by farmers in extension staff who
give wrong advice (due to lack of comprehension) or who are unable to
arrange for supplies of necessary inputs. ERLS has a staff of some 20
professionals and support personnel. Its chief would be willing to tailor
extension material to meet proposed project needs, and to assist with its
production.
4.        The use of IAR research results as a basis for recommendations
by ERLS to farmers has limitations. The farmer does not have the ideal
conditions for crop growth enjoyed by IAR on its stations. His resources
are limited, which necessitates a cropping pattern dictated by his limited
resources. What is now urgently required is a range of experimental work
to show what can be accomplished under the conditions of crop successions
which the environment and limited manpower resources impose on the farmer.
5.        Demonstrations, on both individual holdings and seed multiplica-
tion farms (see Schedule 1) using applicable IAR recommended inputs and
improved seed varieties, would play an important part in teaching new
techniques to farmers. These demonstrations must be of a very high standard
to have a real impact.
Research Programs and Recommendations
6.        Programs on the five crops proposed for the project have been
ongoing for many years. Thus, cotton has been the subject of continuing
research in the major disciplines over the past four decades. Programs
are staffed mainly by personnel of the Cotton Research Corporation (CRC).
They have resulted in the development of improved varieties. Thus S71,
which is recommended for the project area, has a yield potential under
Samaru conditions of well over 2,000 kg of seed cotton per ha. Its main
characteristics are listed in Annex 1. In addition, agronomic practices,
fertilizer responses, pest and disease problems of crop have been clearly
defined and, at least, partially solved, so that farm yields of about 1,100
kg can be expected over wide areas.
7.        The cotton research program has provided definite recommendations
on variety, cultural practices and fertilizer applications applicable to
the project area. Currently it is in the course of evaluating systems of
insect control using ultra-low-volume (ULV) spraying techniques on a field
scale at different sites in the proposed Funtua project area of the North
Central State. Advice on ULV techniques suitable for farmers is likely to
be forthcoming early in 1974. These would be similar to techniques used
by small scale cotton producers in other parts of Africa. Annex 1 contains
the essence of research recommendations for the cotton crop plus proposals
for ULV spraying based on experience elsewhere. Future work in the cotton
research program should include testing verieties that have a short growing



ANNEX 3
Page 3
period and can thus be planted late and still have a resonable yield expecta-
tion. Late planting of cotton is a practice forced on many farmers because
of limited manpower resources.
8.        Groundnuts have been the subject of close research interest and
substantial effort over the past two decades. High yielding varieties
suitable for the project area have been developed and a package of inputs
and practices capable of at least doubling farmer's yields has been available
for some years.
9.        Improved practices proposed for the project are based on current
recommendations by ERLS. They include the use of the seed of the variety
S61, seed dressing with Aldran T or Fernasan D, single superphosphate
fertilizer at 75 kg per ha, early planting and close spacing. It may be
that ongoing research on control of cercospora leaf spot will lead shortly
to the possibility of incorporating this treatment in practices proposed
for groundnuts in the project area. This should be kept in mind, along
with opportunities for production expansion through new varieties and/or
variations in fertilizer dressing, all of which are under investigation.
10.       Sorghum research has progressed from attempts to improve local
varieties into the development of dwarf varieties and hybrids suitable for
the differing ecological conditions encountered in the northern States.
Such material has a yield potential of at least five times greater than
farmers presently obtain. Selection and breeding programs have been underway
for the past 20 years with substantial assistance in the past decade from
the US through USAID programs involving USDA, USARS and several universities.
Cooperative programs with other West African sorghum producers have also been
operated through IAR.
11.       The outcome of this work, and related agronomic investigations,
has not yet reached the stage at which ERLS is able to issue a series of
recommended practices for general distribution. However, highly promising
results have been obtained from a number of trials, replicated at Daudawa
(in the North Central State) and a number of other sites in ecologically
comparable locations. These have formed the basis for the proposed level
of improved technology, outlined in Annex 1, involving the use of dwarf
sorghum varieties. Proposed levels of fertilizer application and the specific
types of sorghum most suitable for the project area should be reviewed after
1973 and 1974 cropping seasons in the light of results of ongoing and proposed
field trials scheduled for Gusau and other sites. Acceptance for human
consumption of new hybrid varieties on the basis of palatability should be
examined closely.
12.       Maize has the most exciting crop development potential in the
project. Recent research results in Nigeria, and experience in other
African countries having similar ecological conditions point to the pro-
bability of a substantial expansion of area and production in the short



ANNEX 3
Page 4
to medium term. Yields of over 7,000 kg/ha have been obtained under trial
conditions over several years at Samaru from synthetic varieties and hybrids.
Trials in the project area have yielded similarly, and promising new material
is presently being tested. Even if a reduction of 50 percent from research
to farmer fields is assumed yield levels of at least 2,800 kg can be readily
obtained.
13.       These developments are comparatively recent in the northern
States. They have been initiated by a number of cooperative and regional
programs, partly funded by the US. Table 2 indicates yield potential of
some of the material being developed. Agronomic practices established
elsewhere have been, and continue to be, tested under Nigerian conditions.
Some refinements are likely to be forthcoming, but basic production tech-
niques utilizing comparatively heavy fertilizer applications have been
proven in trials.
14.       Annex 1 sets out one level of recommended maize technology based
on Nigerian research results and experience in other African countries
having similar ecological conditions. These differ from current recommended
practices since they presuppose a much closer degree of technical assistance
to farmers plus the provision of the inputs necessary to obtain predicted
production levels. As for sorghum, proposed technology should be reviewed
in the light of 1973 and 1974 field trial results and amended as necessary
before incorporation in project activities.
15.       Cowpea is grown throughout the project area, and widely in
Nigeria. Research has centered mainly on control of insect pests, to
which it is highly susceptible both in the field and in store. However,
some improved varieties have been established and recommendations are
available on cultural practices. Responses to phospatic fertilizer (single
superphosphate) have been demonstrated.
16.       Control of insect pests is possible, provided the crop is sole
planted using high volume sprays and DDT/BHC mixtures. The probability
of insect control using ULV techniques is currently under investigation
at IAR. Results suitable for dissemination to farmers are expected to
be forthcoming in the near future. In anticipation of this, an improved
level of technology is proposed for the project, that involves ULV spraying
and fertilizer application to sole crops planted as recommended by ERLS.
Confirmation or possibly modification of the treatment should be sought
before their implementation, following results of ongoing and planned
investigations by IAR staff.
17.       The project management would undertake field trials in close coop-
eration with IAR at the Gusau and Kaura Namoda farms. Investigations
would form part of ongoing research programs in major commodities in the
project area. They would pay particular attention to the applicability of
results under project conditions.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
Seed Multiplication
18.       Seed multiplication is the responsibility of State Ministries of
Agriculture and Natural Resources from parent nucleus material developed
by IAR. At the present time there is no private sector seed industry.
With the exception of the cotton seed multiplication scheme at Daudawa,
Gusau and Gombe, efforts to pioduce and distribute improved planting
material are generally inadequate to meet the needs of the northern States
(for details of stage multiplication of cotton see Table 1).
Vq.       Project proposals for a substantial uptake of improved planting
mdaerial of four of the five crops considered dictate that adequate multipli-
cation facilities should be developed in the area. MIA already has a
60 ha farm at Gusau at which some of the cotton seed for the State is
bulked up. This farm would be extended to 400 ha, and a new farm of 200 ha
at Kaura Namoda would be developed to further expand seed multiplication.
20.       Seed production is a specialized business calling for good manage-
ment standards and a high level of technical competence. MIost MA staff
at farm manager level are weak in those qualities, and it is questionable
whether existing MA personnel could operate a seed production program
of the size proposed for the project. Quantities of seed would not be large
initially, but would build up quite quickly as shown in Schedule 1.
21.       Parts of both sorghum and maize seed production programs may be
of the new hybrids; exact amounts would depend on farmer uptake which itself
depends on the successful demonstration of significant yield differences
between the new hybrids and current composite strains. Groundnut and cotton
seed production would pass through two multiplication stages on the project
seed farms; final multiplication would be on private seed farms. The 600 ha
of the two project seed farms would be adequate to handle cotton and
groundnuts up to the third stage and perhaps the cereal production in the
early years. But the use of selected commercial farmers within the project
area must be envisaged for cereal quantities such as those projected from
year 3 onwards. Seed production development under this project could be
substantially expanded to serve as a major seed producing location for the
State.
22.       Schedule 1 of this Annex gives details on requirements, costs
and returns for Gusau and Kaura Namoda seed farms.

Project Seed Requirements
The project farms would produce the seed requirements of the pro-
ject farmers during the first two years of the project. Thereafter, any
excess requirement over and above production from the project operated
multiplication farms would be produced by large "commercial" farms within
the project area. Such farmers would be assisted by project staff. In the
unlikely event of a shortfall in seed, it is likely that surpluses gen-
erated by the Funtua agricultural development project would be available.
Due to the favorable conditions in Funtua considerable emphasis would be
placed there on seed production.
