NIGERIA
NORTH  WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Roads
General
1.        The lack of reasonable farm access constitutes a serious constraint
to development, the rectification of which is essential for the success of
the project. To enable most farmers to be within two kilometers of a reason-
able road requires development of 1,000 km of agricultural roads in the
Gusau area. These roads are needed so that vital agricultural extension
work can reach the farmer during the wet season when his crops are growing,
and for the delivery of farm inputs and evacuation of produce during the dry
season. In addition, social benefits by way of easier and more economical
haul of other supplies, better bus services, and easier travel by the
farmer's own modes of transport such as bicycles, donkey, ox cart and tractor,
would result.
2.        Traffic densities are difficult to predict with accuracy, but it
is certain that most of the heavy lorry traffic would occur in the dry
season when haul of farm inputs and produce is at its peak. Light traffic
would continue throughout the year, mostly consisting of local movement be-
tween villages. Because of this traffic pattern, where the heavier axle loads
occur in the dry months, it should be possible to use most of the roads through-
out the year, the main obstacle being those roads intersected by a major river
crossing which could cut off traffic for several months in the wet season.
Careful road location is necessary to reduce the frequency of such obstacles.
3.        During the five year development period, the project would construct
1,000 km of agricultural roads, of which, about 750 km would be unsurfaced
and 250 km would be surfaced. Roads would only be surfaced - with appropriate
thicknesses of natural occurring gravels - on main extraction routes to mar-
ket centers and where soil conditions make this essential (see map for
proposed road layout).
4.        Construction Standards. 1/ Roads would be constructed on the water-
sheds 2/ as such alignments are self draining and require minimal earthworks.
Construction would involve 1,500-2,500 YC/mile over a 32 ft formation, and
would be undertaken most effectively by tractors for site clearance, and
1/   Basic standards and data are calculated using English measurements.
2/   Lines of separation between drainage basins.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
motor graders for shaping the formation and providing turn-out drains from
longitudinal side drains; the use of motor graders would predominate.
Stripping (or topsoiling) over the formation width would seldom be required
on watersheds where weathering hlad taken place exposing material which is
capable, without surfacing, of adequately carrying the low volumes of traffic
likely to use such roads. Proposed typical road cross sections are attached
for (a) construction by grader and (b) low embankments that would be re-
quired where low-lying areas cannot be avoided (see Figure 1 of Annex 4).
5.        Construction Technique
(i) Selection of road center line would be carefully carried out by
stereoscopic examination of aerial photography (provided for under
the project, Annex 10 Table 2) and large-scale maps, and by site
inspection and levelling to locate the tops of ridges, which are
often very flat. The alignments would slavishly follow selected
watersheds.
(ii) Clearing of the formation would be carried out by tractor/dozer/
scraper combinations and debris moved outside the formation width.
(iii)  Topsoiling (or soil stripping) would only be carried out where
absolutely necessary, e.g. where topsoil or unsuitable material
exists and where the use of such soil in construction would reduce
road bearing strength.
(iv) Construction by grader. On watershed routes, the main method
of construction would be by motor grader. Material from grader-
blade cut side drains, depth 12" - 18", would be moved towards the
center of the formation width and shaped to a smooth camber.
This is essentially a lateral earth movement although some long-
itudinal regularization would occur along the road center line as
the grader shapes the formation. Scarifying the material in the
side drains may be necessary and occasionally a tractor-mounted
ripper might be needed to loosen material for easier movement by
the grader.
(v) Provision of turn-out (offshoot drains) from the longitudinal
side drains of the formation would be grader constructed to dis-
charge water from the formation and so prevent erosion in the side
drains being caused by long uninterrupted drainage flows.  Care
would be taken to ensure that turnout drains had the correct slope
to take water away from the formation.
(vi)  Compaction of formation after shaping is desirable.  The supply
of water for consumption by project staff and for road and dam
construction would be difficult. Rivers do not flow continually
throughout one year, very few dams exist, shallow wells generally
yield low, and frequently fail in the dry season; and boreholes
(tube wells) have not proved successful in the basement complex of
the project areas.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
The initial stage of the construction program would be confined for
the first dry season of the project to areas within a workable range of exist-
ing water supplies, say, 20 miles. Thereafter the dams constructed in the
first stage, after filling during the rains, would become the water source
for wider reaching road and dam construction activities.
(vii) Surfacing would only be carried out by tractor and scraper over
sections where the subgrade is found to have inadequate bearing
strength even for low traffic volumes, and where supplies of sur-
facing material within the practical working haul of tracked tractors.
Scrapers would require the support of a pusher during such opera-
tions. The principal function of the tractors would be clearing,
dozing and ripping and not surfacing (see para. 11). The self-
loading scrapers proposed for the dam construction unit would
assist with surfacing in the wet season. Local haulage constractors
would be used where long hauls of gravel are found necessary.
(viii) Minor embankments would be constructed by tractors and scrapers
on those sections where watershed construction is impossible. Side
borrow to fill would be used where practicable (see typical cross-
sections). In adopting predominantly watershed alignments, em-
bankments would be required in crossing from one watershed to
another and in doing so, at some sites, it would be possible to
construct dams and allow traffic to cross over a widened dam wall.
(ix) Drainage structures. Corrugated metal pipe culverts would be used
because of the ease and speed with which these can be transported,
assembled and constructed. Bitumen-coating of metal pipe would be
an effective method of protecting culverts from the slightly acid
(pH 5-6) soil water to which they would be exposed. Concrete
headwalls, tail walls and drop-pits using standardized form-work
would be constructed.
Method of Procurement
6.        Road Works - Three possibilities exist:
(i) By Contract. It is the opinion of Federal and State MOW officials
that major Nigerian (and foreign operations in Nigeria) civil
engineering contractors are fully (if not over) committed with
Federal and State highway development programs and that contracts
for low-standard agricultural roads would not interest them at the
present time. The project program is not of a type to interest
new foreign contractors because of their low unit cost of con-
struction, long construction period envisaged, and demand for a
modest amount of equipment and plant.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
(ii) By State Mlinistries and Agencies. The State Ministries of 1Works
and Agriculture and Natural Resources would be unable to undertake
the construction of project roads and dams without considerable
strengthening in staff and equipment. In any case MOW is already
over committed and is extremely short of adequately experienced
supervisory and technical personnel.
(iii) By Project Construction Units. The establishment of these units
would involve recruitment of senior managerial, technical and
mechanical staff, procurement of plant, training of operators,
setting up workshops, costing procedures and evaluation of physical
outputs. The recruitment of high calibre senior personnel with
experience in similar work is essential to the success of project
units. Hlowever, the main advantage of project construction by
force account would be lower cost of construction, flexible use
of equipment between roads and dams, and the training of personnel
to undertake similar work elsewhere in the State.
7.        Other integrated agricultural projects financed by the Bank have
in some instances involved the development of infrastructure through project
construction units. Construction has generally been efficient, at low cost,
and has provided an excellent training ground for young indigenous engineers.
In the light of such experiences, the inadequacy of existing Nigerian insti-
tutions, the lack of available contractors, and the need for flexibility, the
project would set up a special unit to undertake the construction of roads,
dams and soil conservation measures.
Construction Costs
8.        The average overall road construction cost is estimated at about
N 2,500 (US$3,800) per km. The cost includes engineering overheads, plant
capital and operating costs, workshop capital and operating costs, labor
and engineering supplies (see Tables 1-4).
Plant Requirement
9.        Plant requirements for agricultural road construction units have
been estimated by using plant output figures for similar work under similar
terrain and rainfall conditions and by estimating the total work quantities
required. Table 2 shows recommended major plant items. The predominant
method of construction would be by motor grader with one more powerful
grader for work in harder soil conditions. Other major equipment includes
two tracked tractors with dozers and scrapers for clearing and forming
shallow embankments, and two vibratory rollers for compaction or formation
and surfacing, where required.
Staff Requirements
10.       These lhave been related to the numbers and types of construction
equipment and to the tasks involved in alignment selection, grade construction,



ANNEX 4
Page 5
surfacing and drainage structures. A high degree of mobility is desirable;
caravans (dwelling trailers) would be provided for senior personnel and
portable accomnmodation for artisans, operators and laborers (Annex 6 Table 5).
Road Maintenance
11.       Although some road maintenance would be undertaken by the project
during the development period, the allocation of construction plant for
maintenance would not be allowed to interfere with output of new road; thus
once a new road had been built maintenance responsibility would be handed
over to the Gusau and Kaura Namoda Development Boards. Funds have been
included under Annex 10 Table 6 for the purchase and operation of maintenance
equipment, and under the project respective Development Board staff would,
during the project, be seconded to the project engineering service for purposes
of training and operating control.
Bridges
12.       Small streams would be crossed by constructing "Irish" crossings -
a concrete slab on a suitable rock foundation - or through the use of ARMCO
type culverts. No large bridges would be constructed, since most rivers are
extremely wide and run dry for 8 months of the year, at a time when crops are
being evacuated, and inputs are being delivered to service center stores. All
the roads would have year round access from at least one end so that during
the wet season access can be gained. In the dry season road maintenance
units would cut new approaches to the river beds, which would be sufficient
to allow ingress to traffic.
Compensation
13.       Compensation for loss of arable land due to road construction would
be made at established SLA rates and the amounts (provided for under Annex 10
Table 6) would be determined by SLA.

ANNEX 5

NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Water and Soil Conservation
1.        The project area suffers from extreme shortage of water for domestic
purposes during the dry season and part of the wet season until village wells
start to yield properly. The area is also prone to soil erosion principally
in the catchment areas of small streams and rivers. Under the ecological
and climatic conditions prevailing in the Project area, soil and water con-
servation are synonymous with each other, and where possible would be treated
together, particularly as proposed under the project, water development would
be through the construction of surface water dams.
A. Water Conservation and Rural Water Supplies
2.        Shortage of water supplies for domestic use during the dry season
and beginning of the wet season, January through to August, is acute in the
Project area. It is estimated that the existing wells, dams and tapkis
(ponds) can serve only 10-20% of total demand. Villagers depend for their
supplies on water from wells that on average can only be dug to 10-20 m,
most of which dry up during the dry season. An alternative source of water
are tapkis and small earth dams, but these are infrequent, and at times
poorly sited and constructed, resulting in poor holding capacity and at times
breakages. The final alternative is stream bed wells. These are seasonally
hand dug in water bearing sands of some, but not all, rivers and streams.
In many instances, these are last resort supplies resulting in carry distances
of up to 5 miles. Water collected from such sources is either head or donkey
carried, and during the crisis period (from February to June) is sold at a
standard rate of N 0.10 (US$0.15) per 20 liters. The lack of water is a
serious social and technical constraint, and unless efforts are made to
conserve and improve water supplies the project might only partially succeed.
3.        Existing plans by the State Government to improve water supplies
in rural areas has been given high budgetary priority. Plans are being
prepared to improve water supplied to larger towns, but for rural villages
efforts rarely go beyond providing assistance for well improvements. The
latter is not the correct policy since the ground water available in the
shallow aquifer is limited, and new wells themselves dry up and accelerate
the drying up of existing wells. In short it would seem that the draw off
of ground water is greater tharu its reservoir supply.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
4.        The possibilities of utilizing ground water from deep aquifers
have been reviewed. Unfortunately geological conditions in the area are
such that the Basement complex, which is an aquiclude, is of shallow for-
mation and precludes the possibilities of deep aquifers. Furthermore even
if utilization were possible some form of mechanical pumping would be
necessary raising problems of maintenance. Experience shows that most of
the simple pumps installed in the past have been broken within a year and
subsequently have been rarely repaired.
5.        For the reasons outlined above, heavy reliance on dugwells and/or
deep boreholes are impracticable. Fortunately it is possible to exploit
surface water supplies and to impound wet season (June-September) runoff
in small earth dams. The topography and stream pattern for the project area
indicate a promising potential for earth dam construction. This is further
substantiated by the 1:50,000 topographical maps which are available (with
50 and 25 foot contour intervals) and which indicate a large number of
potential locations for small dam sites.
6.        Hydrological data are generally poor, but enough for small dam
development purposes. Annual rainfall in the project area varies from
700 mm-1,200 mm, and a runoff yield varying from 0.080-0.150 m3/km2 can
be expected. Evaporation from the natural water surface is very high being
about 2,000 mm per year.
7.        A careful analysis of the topography, availability of local
construction materials and hydrology indicates that a network of small dams
of about 120,000 m3 in total capacity each should be built. Each would be
fed by runoff from a catchment of 3 to 4 km2 in area and would be capable
of meeting the year-round demands of 2,000 people and their livestock.
The dams would be equipped to provide a gravitational distribution system
for conveying water from the reservoir outlet to delivery points at separate
locations for people and livestock.
8.        A typical earthdam for the project area would have specifications
as detailed below:
(i) Features of average dam
-    Total maximum height                    6.70 m
-    Free board                               1.50 m
-    Depth at spillw.y level                 5.2  m
-    Total capacity                      120,000  m3
-    Dead storage or sedi-..ent space     20,000  m3
-    Useful storage capacity             100,000  m3
-     ..e spillway overflow with concrete sills with rip-rap in
becween (it ;--ay be necessary to peg down the rip-rap with
gabion wire in seciai cases) would be designed by the Soil
Conservation Engineer after he had surveyed the catchment,
studied its characterisi-es, rainfall intensity and worked
out the runoff.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
Dam - earth-fill with clay core if necessary, having 4 m
top width and 1:2 to 1:3 side slopes with rip-rap on
upstream face, containing about 7,000 m3 of fill.
Foundation - treated to impervious zone.  Core wall would
be installed to an impervious foundation.
-    Outlet work - outlet pipes of 7.5 cm in diameter laid in
concrete, with at least three concrete flanges, equipped
with a gate valve on the downstream.
-    Dam design - as portions of the project area are under
soils which contain a high percentage of clay and others
under deep soils containing high sand content, the design
of each dam would be based on the results of soil tests
the proposed borrow and foundation materials.
(ii) Distribution System
-    Main gravity pipeline - iron pipe of 7.5 cm in diameter
laid at 1:1,000 slope for a distance to clear valley
bottom, not exceeding 300 m.
-    Branch pipeline - iron of 5 cm in diameter conveying water
from the main pipe to the separate delivery points at a
distance of at least 50 m for villagers and 100 m for
livestock from the main pipe.
-    Villager delivery points - installed with an adequate
concrete platform and faucets. 1/
-    2 livestock delivery points - installed with an adequate
brick trough and faucets.
(iii)  Useful Life of Tank          20 years 2/
-    Design sediment space in tank:  1,200 x 20 x 0.85 - 20,000 m3
-    Total tank capacity:
-    Active or useful storage             100,000 m3
-    Space for sediment deposit            20,000 m3
-    Total capacity                       120,000 m3
1/   The possibility of using hydrograms, for pumping water from the larger
dams to nearby villages,should be considered as to cost and applicability.
2/   For this purpose, the first 20 years after completion of the tank are
considered as "productive life of the reservoir" and years beyond this
are considered as "falling" or "depleting" life.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
9.        The project could not in the first instance provide sufficient
dams to satisfy all project farmers. However, it would attempt to satisfy
demand in the more critical areas, and provide reserve capacity for most
village unit areas. 85 dams would be constructed over the 5 years of the
project; 5 would be built in year 1, followed by 20 in each subsequent year.
Thus on average, there would be one dam to 45 km2 which approximates to one
per 800 farming families. Ground water table should also improve, thereby
indirectly affecting a large number of the remaining 75%. If successful,
this dam construction program could be continued in another phase.
B. Soil Conservation Measures
10.       Soil erosion occurs through overstocking and concentration of
stock at sparsely located watering points, and through poor cultivation
methods including badly aligned planting ridges and late planted crops at
low plant densities.
11.       Virtually no soil conservation measures have been undertaken on
a large scale in northern Nigeria and there are no practical specifications
based on past experience that can be followed. Consequently, it would remain
for the project conservation engineer to modify the designs and layouts as
experience is gained during project implementation.
12.       Dam catchments would require special conservation measures to
prevent erosion and siltation. Conservation on other areas would be of a
less intensive nature but designed to provide a basis for any future more
intensive conservation measures that may be required. To avoid concentration
of livestock at the limited number of existing watering places and planned
project dams a number of tapkis (ponds) would be constructed.
13.       Dam Catchment Conservation Measures
(i) Master ridges would be marked with a plough on the contour
so that all cultivation would follow the contour, 3 lines
per 20 ha should be sufficient.
(ii) The Conservation Engineer would locate natural or artificial
water ways approximately 1,000 m apart. He would shape them
to the required size and if necessary have them grassed. In
the second year when the grass had established, "vee" ditches
or cut-off drains would be constructed.
Specifications. The diversion ditches (cut-off drains) would be
approximately 1,000 m long draining both ways into the grassed waterway.
The ditches would have a cross sectional area of 0.8 m2 (37 cm deep and
3.6 m wide) and have a grade of 1:400 and be 250 m apart.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
Equipment Required.  1 x 125 hp motor grader, 1 x 75 hp tractor
and plow.
14.       Conservation of Other Areas.  A conservation unit would be attached
to the road unit, and as the roads progressed master ridges would be consttucted
with a plow over an area extending approximately 2 km each side of the road.
3 master ridges per 20 ha would be sufficient. At the same time lines for
future access roads to villages or group of farms would be marked, the natural
drainage ways would also be demarcated so that they would be left out of
cultivation and allowed to revert to grass. In addition farmers should be
encouraged to practice tie ridges (boxed ridges). The ties would be 1 to 2 m
apart and half the height of ridges. This would give maximum absorption in
the early light rains. In the heavy rains the ties would break and allow
the excess water to drain off. Ties would be replaced towards the end of the
rains to assist in improving the infiltration of the late rains.
15.       Tapkis.  These would be constructed by a 140 hp angle dozer in
shallow depressions with a catchment area of 15 to 20 ha. Should the site
chosen have insufficient catchment, the catchment would be increased by the
use of diversion ditches.
Method. The dozer would scoop out the earth over an area approxi-
mately 40 m x 30 m with an average depth of 1.7 m. The embankment would be
made around the lower end giving a small increase to the holding capacity of
the tapki.  The diversion ditches would b! constructed by the motor grader
on a 1:500 grade with a capacity of 0.8 m  (37 cm deep and 3.6 m wide).
These would stop short of the tapki and be allowed to discharge towards a
bank 1 m high, 1 m thick and of sufficient length to allow the water to
circulate around the bank into the tapkis. This would reduce the velocity
and cause the silt to be deposited outside the tapki.
Equipment. No additional equipment would be required for tapki
construction as construction by the conservation unit would be done during
the crop growing season. About 160 tapkis would be constructed during the
life of the project.

NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Organization, Staff Requirements and Training
A.   Organization
1.        The project organization as described in Chapter VI of the main
report would assume responsibility for all existing functions of MA within
the project area, except for those aspects concerning animal health, fisheries,
forestry, and irrigation. The project organization would follow the pattern
as shown at Schedule 1 of this annex and would comprise a project executive
committee, primarily controlling policy, budgets and audits, and an opera-
tional project management unit responsible for the day-to-day operations
involved in implementation of project policies. The project executive
committee and management unit would.be established by a Government gazetted
order, a proposed draft for which is at Schedule 2.
2.        The Gusau Project Management Unit (GPMU) would operate through a
number of sections; the head of each would be responsible to the Project
Manager. The sections would be: Administration, Finance, Technical
Services, Credit Services, Market Services, and Engineering Services.
3.        The Administration Section would be responsible for personnel
administration, maintenance of records, organization of project transport,
office administration, project security and public relations.
4.        The Finance Section would be responsible for budgeting, budget
control, internal audit, procurement, stores, salary payments, accounting
procedures, cost accounting, etc. It would help in setting up financial
procedures for the revolving credit fund, and would conduct the internal
audit of the fund.
5.        The Technical Services Section would provide basic technical
support and services to all those project actions designed to increase
farmer productivity including:
(a) Development Services would provide the coordinating link
between marketing, credit and extension. The project would
have 4 development centers each serving a development area
and controlled by a Development Officer who would coordinate
all project activities within the area. Apart from coordi-
nation, the Development Officer would be directly responsible
for extension services. The latter would be organized on



ANNEX 6
Page 2
the basis of one Agricultural Assistant (AA) for each farm
service center. In turn each AA would supervise a number
of Field Supervisors (FS) who would work at the hamlet
level. The AA and FS would provide basic extension advice
to farmers and groups of farmers. Associated with the
extension service would be a small livestock advisory unit
that would (i) identify livestock problems and make
recommendations for livestock programs within the area;
and (ii) provide advice on practical animal husbandry
to those farmers engaged in livestock operations sponsored
by the project. In this connection close liaison would be
maintained with Government veterinary services.
(b) Seed Farms. Two seed farms at Gusau and Kaura Namoda would
be developed to provide part of project seed requirements
in conjunction with contract operations by some of the
larger farmers in nearby areas. The seed farms would also
be used for practical demonstration purposes, applied
research and training. Seed farm management would also
provide management advice to larger "commercial" farmers
within the project area. Tiese farmers would be directed
to NAB and/or commercial banks for farm credit and the
unit would assist them in this.
(c)  Tractor Hire Services, now supplied by MA, would be
continued under the project, but would be phased out
gradually in favor of private contractors sponsored by
the project.
(d) Training would be undertaken at the Gusau training center,
the capacity of which would be expanded from 20 to 40
students, and at a new center to be constructed at Kaura
Namoda. The two centers would be used initially for
training and retraining project staff in extension. credit,
marketing and other disciplines. As the project develops,
more of the available training capacity would be allocated
to project area farmers.
6.        The Credit Services Section would be developed in parallel to the
development services and would be structured around the Development Centers
at District level, and at the farm service centers at farmer level. The
service would provide farmers with inputs on credit or for cash, assist in
recovery of lepayments, and keep accounts. An area Credit Officer would
control credit activities within each development area, and each village
service center would have two Credit Assistants (Annex 9 gives details).



ANNEX 6
Page 3
7.        The Market Services Section would be responsible for produce
inspection at produce markets, for market liaison and information, and for
the control and monitoring of Licensed Buying Agents (LBA). It would also be
responsible for the development of group marketing systems, and where
necessary for linking producers directly with distant consumer, e.g.,
contracts for maize supply to southern feed mill operators. Each Development
Center would have a marketing supervisor assisted by two market assistants.
A produce examiner would be posted to each farm service center to service
its market. Marketing services would be responsible for the logistics
involved in handling and distributing farm inputs. In this respect the
produce examiners would act as storekeepers during the period when markets
are closed, and when the main handling and distribution of inputs would
occur.
8.        The Engineering Services Section would be primarily responsible
for implementation of the physical infrastructural program for the project.
It would determine design and make specifications for buildings, houses,
offices, etc., prepare tender documents, evaluate bids, and supervise
contractors. It would be responsible for the procurement of all plant,
vehicles and equipment, and for their operation and maintenance. It would
prepare designs for roads and associated structures, dams, and soil conser-
vation works, and would ensure their construction according to plan. It
would operate a project workshop. Finally it would have a special land use
section to provide support for other project units as well as to provide
information directly relevant to civil works construction. In all instances
it would work closely with Ministry of Works Engineers.
B.   Staff Requirements
9.        Details of the number of staff needed for the project, their
availability within the State and probable shortfalls are shown at Tables
1-5 of this Annex. Job descriptions and qualifications for the more senior
positions are at Schedule 3. In general existing staff in the State have
received inadequate training and experience to permit them to effectively
manage and operate the project and, therefore, internationally recruited
staff would be necessary for some of the key posts. Existing staff in th
project area would come under the project and additional staff would be
transferred to the project from other areas of the State. Priority during
the first two years would be given to the project for those Agricultural
Assistants completing ongoing training courges. On this basis sufficient
professional and technical staff would be available. Junior technical
staff, particularly field supervisors (FS), would be recruited and trained
by the project at its own training centers. Such training would initially
comprise of simple short courses on specific crops and associated problems.
The FS would receive further training after a period of field experience,
and thereafter regular refresher courses. Inexperienced credit and marketing
staff would be trained in a similar manner through a series of inservice
training courses.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
10.       The Project Executive Committee on the advice of the Project
Manager would be responsible for the appointment of all senior staff. Staff
appointments at lower levels would be the responsibility of the Project Manage-
ment Unit in consultation with relevant Government departments from which
particular transfers might be made. Staff salaries are compatible with
existing Government scales, and would from time to time be reviewed by the
Project Executive Committee.
C.   Staff Training
ll.       Professional training is provided at universities throughout
Nigeria. The Ahmadu Bello University at Zaria in the North Central State
caters especially for the six northern States, although northern students
attend both Federal and State financed universities elsewhere in Nigeria
and abroad. A degree in agriculture is given by ABU and the project would
expect to recruit some of its professional staff as they graduate from ABU.
12.       Diplomate (3-year course for holders of West African School
Certificate) and certificate (2-year) level training is the responsibility
of the Division of Agriculture and Livestock Service Training (DALST) of
ABU. Graduates of these courses are employed by the Ministries of Agricul-
ture and Natural Resources of the northern States.
13.       Four schools are operated by DALST:
(a) Samaru (North Central State)
Offering, at present, certificate courses in extension,
farm mechanics, home economics and a diploma course in
home economics. Expansion of this school, planned for
completion by 1975, would add diploma courses in farm
mechanics and extension to the syllabus.
(b) Bakura (North Western State)
Offering a certificate course in irrigation agronomy;
presently located at Samaru pending completion of the
new school at Bakura.
(c) Kaduna Livestock Service Training Center (North
Central State)
Offering certificate courses in livestock and range
management, and diploma courses in animal products
inspection, and range management.
(d) Kabba (Kwara State)
Offering diploma courses in agriculture at present,
and certificate courses in agriculture from 1974.

Page 5

that by 1975 Samaru would have a capacity for 600 students and the
other schools 300 students each.

/3   Special course to give existing Ministry technical staff the opportunity
to upgrade to AS (1972 only).
15.       The output of diplomates (Agricultural Superintendents) and certi-
ficate holders (Agricultural Assistants) bonded to work through the terms of
their scholarships for North Western State is forecast as follows: 1974 - 12;
1975 - 27; and 1976 - 36. These, together with intra State transfers, would
meet project requirements (see Table 2).
16.       The existing training of agricultural extension staff, graduates,
diplomates and certificate holders is sadly lacking in practical farm manage-
ment, and the ability of senior staff in the field to give advice to farmers
directed at increasing profit margins needs to be greatly improved. At
present extension staff tend to give blanket advice without considering
economic implications. Training establishments, for both senior and junior
staff, should improve the quality of their farm management training.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
17.       Farm training centers are operated by North Western State and
graduate Field Supervisors (FS), the lowest grade of extension worker. The
State also has farm institutes for training farmers and farmers' sons. Under
the project it is proposed to upgrade the existing Gusau Farm Institute to
a Farm Training Center and to build a new Farm Training Center at Kaura
Namoda. These centers would be used to train, in the initial years of the
project, project staff particularly FS and Credit Assistants. Later on the
emphasis would be more on farmer training.
18.       The recruitment of individuals for training as Field Supervisors,
Credit Assistants, etc. should not cause too many problems since the basic
primary level of education is relatively good throughout northern Nigeria,
and there is an increasing surplus of West African School Cerfiticate holders
on the labor market.
19.       Staff recruited by the project would be expected to have certain
minimum qualifications: Agricultural Officers - degree in agriculture;
Agricultural Superintendents - diploma or upgraded from lower level; Agricul-
tural Assistant - certificate; Field Supervisor - primary seven; Credit
Officer - degree or diploma, or certificate in accountancy preferably with
some commercial bank experience; Credit Assistants - West African School
Certificate; Market Supervisor - degree holder or relevant experience in
market organization; Marketing Assistant - West African School Certificate;
Produce Inspector - primary seven; Rural Engineers - engineering degree.
20.       The terms and conditions of the employment of project personnel
would conform with those established for all employees of the State Government
except that professional and technical grade staff would be provided housing
as an incentive to live and work in remote areas, and that new terms would
be made for both expatriate and Nigerian contract staff. For expatriate staff
contract terms would take into account the international market for such staff
and the benefits that normally accrue to such appointments. For Nigerian
staff contract terms would provide similar benefits as normal pensionable
Government staff but would also provide for a gratuity (to be determined by
GPEC) in lieu of a pension.
21.       Management Training.  In addition to technical staff, training
project management would play an important role in training staff to fill
managerial positions. In this respect internationally recruited staff would,
as one of their responsibilities, ensure the adequate training of their
subordinates and would be required to submit to GPEC, through the project
manager, quarterly reports on the training aspects of their section. The
project manager would have the overall responsibility for ensuring that an
adequate management training program is maintained and would report regularly
on its progress. There are sufficient funds available for the recruitment
of a training specialist if the project manager so requires.

NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Establishment of Project Management Unit
1.        As a condition of loan effectiveness, the North Western State
Executive Council would be required to publish an order in the State Government
Gazette establishing the Gusau Project Management Unit (GPMU) and the Gusau
Project Executive Committee (GPEC). The order would set out the objectives
and responsibilities of GPHU and GPEC and would include the following.
2.        The GPMU would be managed by a Project Manager and its objectives
should be:
(a) development of the project area with a view to increasing
production of agriculture and raising the living standards
of the rural population;
(b) construction of roads, water supplies and soil conservation
works associated with agricultural development;
(c) development and management of seed multiplication farms;
(d) training af project staff and farmers;
(e) provision of village service centers and stores;
(f) provision of extension, credit and marketing services;
(g) procurement of farm inputs; and
(h) development of supporting infrastructures such as houses,
buildings, offices, etc., for the above services.
3.        The GPMU would be controlled by the Gusau Project Executive
Committee (GPEC) which would be made up as follows:
(a)  Permanent Secretary   -   Ministry of Agriculture
(b)  Representative         -   Ministry of Finance
(c)  Representative         -   Mlinistry of Local Government



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 2
(d)  Representative        -   Ministry of Transport and Works
(e)  Representative        -   Ministry of Economic Planning
(f)  Representative        -   North Western State Water Board
(g)  Director of Federal Agricultural Department or representative
(h)  Chief Agricultural Officer for the North Western State
(i)  Two members from the project area to be appointed by the
Military Governor.
The Project Manager should be secretary of the GPEC.
4.        GPEC would be responsible to the Commissioner of Agriculture and
would have the following responsibilities:
(a)  Policy.  GPEC would determine agricultural and development
policy, and would consult with the Federal Government and
the World Bank when any major changes in policy may be
required;
(b) Finance
(i)  GPEC would approve the annual budget and would ensure
that sufficient Government funds are readily available
to the project;
(ii) GPEC would appoint independent auditors;
(iii) GPEC would submit audited project statements of accounts
to the Commissioner of Agriculture within four months
of the closure of each financial year;
(iv) GPEC would approve the opening of project bank accounts;
and
(v)  the Chairman of GPEC would approve the monthly cash
flow statements submitted by the manager of GPMU;
(vi) approve annually the terms of lending to project farmers
on the basis of reco mendations made by GPMU.
(c) Staff and Personnel Matters. In consultation with the Manager
of GPMU, GPEC would be responsible for the appointment of all
senior project staff and employees, their terms and conditions
of employment and annual review of salaries and allowances;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 3
(d) Award of Contracts. GPEC would, on the advice of the Manager
of GPMU, be responsible for approval of any single contract
valued at more than N 30,000;
(e) Inter-Departmental Coordination. The GPEC, through its members,
would be responsible for the coordination at Ministry level for
those issues that may affect the proper development of the
project.
Neetings of GPEC
5.   (a)  The Chairman would preside over all the meetings of the GPEC
which he attends, and in his absence the present members would
appoint one of their number to preside over such a meeting.
(b) The Chairman of the GPEC would invite the GPEC to meet at
such time and place as he may think suitable; provided that
no more than three months would elapse between any meeting
and the next. In absence of the Chairman, the Manager would
call the GPEC to meet whenever he deems this necessary.
(c) Six members of the GPEC would constitute a quorum of any
meeting of the GPEC. The GPEC would pass its decisions
by the Majority of the votes of its members present and
voting, and in case of equality of votes the Chairman would
have a casting vote.
(d) The GPEC may call any person to attend any of its meetings
without that person having a counted vote.
6.        GPMU would be responsible to GPEC for the day-to-day operations of
the project. In particular it would be responsible for the carrying out of
the objectives as stated in para 2 above. GPMU would be managed by a
Project Mlanager who would report directly to the Chairman of GPEC. The
main duties of GPMW would be:
(a) the implementation of an agricultural development program
in the Gusau project area following the policy guidelines
of the GPEC;
(b) the control and operation of the project revolving credit fund;
(c) the preparation of annual budgets and cash flows for approval
by GPEC. In particular GPMU would submit to the Chairman
of the GPEC monthly cash flow statements indicating any
changes in requirements for State Government funds due to
changes in project expenditures and inflow anticipated from
the Federal Government. Unless informed to the contrary
within 5 days of dispatch of such statements to the Chairman
of the GPEC, the manager would be authorized to make the
proposed expenditures;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 4
(d) the recruitment of project staff, in consultation, when
necessary, with other Government Departments, and for
senior staff with approval of GPEC;
(e) the advertising, negotiating, calling for tenders, and
after approval by GPEC, for concluding such contracts with
persons and organizations either inside or outside the
State for the erection of buildings, the construction of
civil works and the supply of goods, services and materials
as may seem to be requisite for the proper discharge of its
duties;
(f) the operation of project bank accounts;
(g) the preparation of reimbursement claims, against project
expenditure for submission to the Federal Military Government
and the World Bank.
7.        GPMU would administer a field evaluation unit that would be techni-
cally directed by a Federal Military Government-controlled Project Monitoring
and Evaluation Unit (PMEU).
8.        GPMU would prepare quarterly and annual reports for the simultaneous
submission to GPEC, North Western State Executive Council, Federal Military
Government of Nigeria and the World Bank.
9.        GPMW would be delegated any additional duties and powers by GPEC to
the attainment of the objectives as set out in this order.

NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Job Descriptions and Qualifications of Senior Staff
Project Manager
Experience would be the main criteria for the selection of a
Project Manager, although a formal qualification in agriculture would be
important.
At least 10 years' experience in successful agricultural development
work in the field, including development-oriented projects, preferably in
Africa, would be required. A high degree of administrative ability, an
experience in dealing with the highest level of Government, especially in
committee work, and previous involvement in extension in small farmer commu-
nities would all be necessary.
The responsibilities of the Project Manager would be the overall
and complete control of all project activities and the coordination of all
project activities with those of other Federal and State Government agencies
and local authorities.
His duties would include:
(a) control of all project funds with the assistance of the
Chief Accountant;
(b) establishment of a cost analysis and record keeping system
which would enable project progress and achievement to be
monitored and a meaningful evaluation of the project's
impact possible;
(c) responsibility for project personnel matters with the
assistance of the Chief Administrative Officer;
(d) coordination of all project activities;
(e) reporting project progress to Government through written
reports and through the various project committees; and
(f) training project staff and ensuring a proper management
training program is successfully implemented.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 2
The Project Manager -would be responsible to the Chairman of the
Gusau Project Executive Committee (the Chairman being the Permanent Secretary
of the Iinistry of Agriculture).
Chief Administrative Officer
The Chief Administrative Officer would be a very senior civil
servant; or have had equivalent experience in a major public corporation or
.private company.
In previous employment he would have undertaken senior administrative
responsibility and have gained a thorough understanding of government procedures.
His duties would include:
(a) responsibility for all project personnel;
(b) project transportation;
(c) maintenance of a project registry;
(d) the physical control and checking of project stores;
(e) the general project security;
(f) the public relations aspects of the project; and
(g) training administration staff.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
Chief Accountant
The Chief Accountant would be a professionally qualified accountant
with additional training in business management. he should have had at least
10 years' experience in a senior position in a large public corporation or
commercial company. If possible, he would have a knowledge of banking and
credit procedures. Previous employment involving working with high ranking
government officials would be an advantage.
His main responsibility would be the control of all project finances
on behalf of the Project Manager. His duties would include:
(a) preparation of budgets, cash flows and estimates of
expenditure for the proiect,
(b) ensuring correct applicaticn foro project funds;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 3
(c) assisting the Credit Manager in establishing an efficient
and meaningful system of recording agricultural credit
transactions;
(d) the responsibilities for project stores and requisites, with
the assistance of the section and department heads, and for
stores' control and audit procedures;
(e) internal auditing of project accounts; and
(f) training accounting staff.
Chief Technical Officer
The Chief Technical Officer would be a qualified agriculturalist.
He would have a thorough knowledge of tropical crop production and related
problems.
He would have had at least 5 years' experience in extension work
with small farmers where cotton, groundnuts and cereals were part of the
cropping system.
He would be responsible for all technical aspects of the project
and his duties specifically would include:
(a)  organizing research, and seed multiplication and distribution,
with the assistance of the Seeds Officer and Farm Managers;
(b) supervision of the training program for all extension staff;
(c) organizing and ensuring the effectiveness of extension
services in the project area;
(d) supervising the Livestock Advisor, the Farm Machinery
Section and the Farm Management Advisor; and
(e) liaising with and assisting the Market Section to ensure
it meets the project's requirements in marketing.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager and would
assist the Project Manager in his dealings with other government and local
authority agencies in matters related to the Technical Services.
Chief Engineer
The Chief Engineer should be a qualified civil engineer. Some
training in mechanical engineering would be an added advantage.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 4
He would have had at least 5 years' experience in the problems of
rural engineering in developing countries. A sound knowledge and experience
in road, water, and soil conservation engineering would be considered
essential. He would have had senior administrative responsibility in the
engineering field and would be accustomed to working with goverrnent agencies.
His duties would include:
(a) control of all project engineering activities;
(b) budgetary control;
(c) the preparation of all tenders, contracts, and quantity
surveys;
(d) supervision of land use planning, and the design of project
roads, buildings and dams; and
(e) for the efficient coordination of the different units of
the project engineering services, and close liaison with
those other Government departments, such as the Ministry
of Works and the Ministry of Local Government, who may be
associated with the project.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
Credit Manager
The Credit Manager would be a professionally qualified accountant
with a demonstrable sound working knowledge of the establishment and corpo-
ration of rural credit institutions. he would have at least 5 years of
experience in responsible positions in smallholder rural credit administration,
and have a record of sound administrative ability. Experience in the forma-
tion of farmers' credit groups or societies and working with these organi-
zations, and/or cooperatives, would be an advantage.
His prime responsibility would be the control of project farmer
credit operation, which would involve initially the disbursement of loans in
kind and their recovery.
His responsibilities would include:
(a) formation of a Revolving credit Fund as outlined in the
project appraisal report;
(b) establishing an efficient accounting and recording system
for farmers' loans;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 5
(c) requisitioning of funds and other required inputs for
timely disbursement and supply to farmers;
(d) liaison with the Evaluation Unit to provide data and
information needed in project evaluation; and
(e) training of credit staff.
The Credit Manager would be responsible to the Project Manager.
Chief Marketing Officer
Senior management responsibility in a large marketing organization
would be the principal qualification of the Chief Marketing Officer.
He would have experience in the production, primary marketing and
selling of tropical crops, particularly cotton, groundnuts and maize.
He would be responsible for the project's Marketing Services, and
his duties would include:
(a) monitoring and controlling the operations of all licensed
buying agents operating in the project area;
(b) establishing a market information service;
(c) liaison with the statutory marketing boards and other
crganizations private and public to ensure the effective
marketing and smooth flow of produce from the project area;
and
(d) coordinating with the Credit Manager to establish a system
of credit collection at time of crop marketing.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
Senior Farm Manager
The Senior Farm Manager would have some formal training in agricul-
ture, but more importantly would niave had at least 10 years' successful
experience in managing large -c   rzially oriented farms in the tropics.
A specific experience of cottor., groundnuts, sorghum and maize production,
or at least two o.- tb'~se cron- would be required. Previous experience in
growing these and other s'_nos "or seed production would be helpful.  A sound
knowledge of farm recordinr and accounting procedures would be essential.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 6
The Senior Farm Manager would manage one of the project farms and
would, through another manager, be responsible for the second project farm.
In total he would be responsible for the efficient operation of a 400 ha
unit growing cotton, groundnuts and cereals for seed.
His duties would include:
(a) budget preparation and cash flow control for the farm;
(b) preparation and implementation of a cropping plan for the
farm;
(c) establishment, maintenance and operation of a suitable
seed dressing and cleaning unit for the farm;
(d) procuring, maintaining, and operating appropriate farm
machinery;
(e) control of farm labor;
(f) assisting the Farm Management Advisor in providing advice
and assisting the large privately owned farm units of the
project area, especially those growing seed on contract;
(g) liaison with the Seeds Officer on technical seed production
matters; and
(h) training junior staff in practical large-scale farming.
He would be responsible to the Chief Technical Officer.
Seeds Officer
The Seeds Officer would have had formal training in agriculture or
agricultural botany. He would have had specific experience in commercial
large-scale seed production, preferably with at least two of the following:
cotton, groundnuts, maize, and sorghum. He would advise the Managers of the
Seed Multiplication Farms on seed production and would be responsible for
ensuring the distribution of improved seeds to farmers in the project area.
His duties would include:
(a)  advising and assisting the Farm Managers in procuring and
operating suitable seed cleaning and dressing units;
(b) sponsoring progressive farmers in seed production and
thereafter for advising them in seed production techniques;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 7
(c) the building up of a viable seed industry in the project
area; and
(d) the training of technical and professional project
personnel in seed production and distribution.
He would be responsible to the Chief Technical Officer.
Land Use Planning Officer
The Land Use Planning Officer would have had formal training in
agricultural, soil science or land resource use; land surveying and photo-
grammetry. Previous experience in Land Resource work and associated land
and agricultural development planning would be essential.
Duties of the Land Use Planning Officer would be to:
(a) prepare specifications for the production of aerial photographs
and uncontrolled mosaics of the project area;
(b) undertake a complete photo analysis of the project area
and prepare land use resource maps and inventory of
the area;
(c) plan, in conjunction with the Chief Technical Officer,
the location of roads and dams to be constructed under
the project;
(d) prepare all drawings, etc., of dam, soil conservation
and road designs that may be submitted by the engineering
sections of the project;
(e)  undertake any sucz^ o:her rmaaring and surveying considered
necessary by the Chief Engineer;
(f) undertake photoanalytical work that may be required by
the evaluation unit or Chief Technical Officer;
(g) give general guidance or, Land Resource use to Engineering
and Technical Services sta-   and
(h) train Nigerian staff in land use and associated techniques.
He wou;! "e directly responsible to the Chief Engineer.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 8
Soil and Water Conservation Engineer
The Soil and Water Conservation Engineer would have had formal
training in engineering, preferably related to dam construction and soil
conservation. He would have had at least 5 years' experience in earth dam
construction and soil conservation in Africa. He would be responsible for
overall supervision of the dam construction and soil conservation component
of the project. His responsibilities would include:
(a) survey and design of water catchment areas and dams;
(b) design and planning, and supervision of construction of
all soil conservation work;
(c) liaison with the Road Engineer and Land Use Planning
Officer in integrating dam construction and soil conser-
vation works into a general land use plan and the road
building program; and
(d) training project staff in dam construction and soil
conservation work.
The Soil and Water Conservation Engineer would be responsible to
the Chief Engineer.
Mechanical Engineer
The Mechanical Engineer would be a professionally qualified engineer
with specific experience in operating and maintaining heavy earth moving
machinery. His principal duties and responsibilities would be supervising
the operation and maintenance of all project plant, equipment, and vehicles.
His duties would include:
(a) establishment of a workshop capable of maintaining
project plan, equipment and vehicles;
(b) ordering and maintaining an approtriate supply of
stores and spare parts:
(c)  ordering and maintaining an acequate and suitable fuel
supply; and
(d)  training of Nigerian mechan_ca' engineers.
He would be directly respc;s-l, e _o tne Chief Engineer.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 9
Road Engiaeer
Successful experience in the design, construction and maintenance
of earth roads in tropical climates would be the main qualification of the
Road Engineer. The Road Engineer would be responsible for implementing the
project road program. his duties would include:
(a) advising the Chief Engineer on road design, drainage and
water disposal, culverts and river crossing designs;
(b) the direction of all machinery and equipment put at his
disposal for construction purposes;
(c) the preparation and maintenance of records adequate to
enable unit costs to be established;
(d) evaluation of road construction program and techniques
used; and
(e) training Nigerian engineers in road design and construction
methods.
He would be directly responsible to the Chief Engineer.
Senior Evaluation Officer
A Senior evaluation officer would be required to direct the field
enumeration units attached to the project. His job would be essentially to
supervise enumerators, implement programs set out by theilead of the Proj,ect
Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU) evaluation section, and feed back the
required information for analysis. His views and suggestions would be taken
into account during the programming of the enumeration process and any subse-
quent amendments to it.  The senior evaluation officer would be technically        >
responsible to the Head of PMEU evaluation section and administratively
responsible to the Project Manager.
The senior evaluation officer would have post graduate training i:
agricultural economics or rural economics and would have controlled and di-
rected field enumeration units.

NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Inputs
1.        The traditional agriculture of the Gusau area uses almost no modern
agricultural inputs. Currently there is very little demand for, and very
little supply of, such inputs as fertilizers, insecticides, sprayers and other
farm machinery. Yet, if the project is to achieve its goals, the project
must make available a supply of these inputs, create a demand for them, de-
velop the means for farmers to acquire them, and educate farmers in their
use - even supervise their use where necessary to assure results.
2.        At present suppliers of farm inputs in the project area are either
absent or inadequate. Therefore, the project would be an importer and re-
tailer of critical farm supplies and equipment. These supplies include 600 m
tons fertilizer in year one, increasing to 11,000 m tons in year five; 1,500
sprayer kits in year one increasing to 14,500 in year five; as well as ox
drawn equipment, seeds, and insecticides. The logistics of acquiring, moving
and distributing these quantities would be substantial and would be the respon-
sibility of the Chief Marketing Officer. The selling price of inputs would
be based on an ex-farm center store cost.
3.        Inputs would be transported to the project center at Gusau where a
4,000-ton storage facility would be constructed at Gusau railway yard and to
40 village stores each with a 500-ton storage capacity. Rail to village store
transportation would be arranged with private truckers, many of whom would be
LBA transport contractors who would welcome back haul loads while moving pro-
duce from village to market.
4.        Seasonal inputs include fertilizers, insecticides, seeds and battery
sets for ULV sprayers. Fertilizers would be purchased in 50 kg bags clearly
marked to distinguish kind. The landed cost per m ton in the village store
(including a 10% handling charge) is estimated at N 200.8 for calcium ammonium
nitrate, N 187.6 for single superphosphate, and N 118.3 for muriate of potash.
Insecticides for cotton and cowpeas would cost farmers an estimated N1.65/
kilogram. Battery sets for sprayers would cost about N2.64 per set. Improved
seeds would be made available at varying costs.
5.        Subsidies.  NWS would continue to subsidize nitrogenous and phos-
phatic fertilizers to an amount of 50% of cif village store cost. At present
farmers pay N40 per m ton for ammonium sulphate and N32 per m ton for single
superphosphate (less than projected cif Nigerian ports price). It has been
Government policy to subsidize cotton insecticides. In the expectations of



ANNEX 7
Page 2
increasing yields by as much as 400% under the project through spraying, and
associated practices, and because a large number of farmers would be involved,
compared to the negligible number at present, it is considered desirable that
cotton insecticides would not be subsidized. The subsidy element for ferti-
lizers is shown in Table 1.
6.        Sales of Seasonal Inputs.  It is expected that 25% of seasonal in-
puts would be sold for cash at farm service centers. Payments would be
made at the service center, recorded by cash register, and simultaneously
entered in the stores register. The remaining 75% of seasonal inputs would
be sold on credit. The credit system is described in more detail in Annex 8.
Sales on credit would be in kind and would be similar to the system for a
cash sale except that they would be registered on the farmer's credit card.
Tables 2, 3, and 4 show the amounts involved.
7.        Medium Term Inputs and Farm Equipment include ULV sprayers, ox
drawn equipment kits and tractor kits. These would be procured by the proj-
ect for sale to farmers. ULV sprayers together with protective gloves and
face masks would be sold for N14.9. An estimated 29,500 sprayer kits would
be made available to farmers over the 5 year development period (Table 5).
Ox drawn kit at N165 each would include an ox cart, plow and ridger. An
estimated 5,000 ox drawn kits are expected to be taken up over 5 years in
the project area (Table 5); mainly to larger farmers owning at least 6-10 ha
of land under cultivation. The project would not supply oxen. Farmers in-
terested in obtaining ox equipment would be expected to acquire oxen with
their own resources. Extreme difficulties are involved in supplying oxen on
credit, given their relatively high value and the opportunity of realizing
this through sale for meat.
8.        Tractor and Equipment.  At present the Government supports a limited
but totally uneconomic tractor hire service within the project area. This
service would be phased out during the project period and would be replaced
by contract tractor operations provided by private entrepreneurs receiving
credit under the project. About 50 such loans would be made initially, and
would be made primarily to existing transport contractors, small businessmen
or larger farmers. The project would provide such contractors with maintenance
services, through its mechanical workshop and field servicing units, short
training courses for tractor drivers, and would direct operators to selected
areas. The tractor kit would include a 65 hp tractor, trailer, chisel plow,
disc harrow and a ridger. The project management would select a standard make
and specifications for the kit, taking into account prices obtained through
domestic competitive bidding, which would require a minimum level of local
maintenance service to be provided by the tractor suppliers. Project manage-
ment would help to establish realistic and fair hire rates for contractors.
By the fifth year of the project, tractor cultivations could be extended some
3,000 to 4,000 ha annually and if successful could be further expanded.

NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Credit
1.        Provision of inputs, and of technical services, is of little avail
unless farmers have the means for acquiring inputs. Thus farm credit plays
a critical role in the package of goods and services needed to make the
agriculture of a region more productive.
2.        Almost no commercial credit is available at present to farmers in
the project area, especially to the large majority who are small farmers.
Although small farmers apparently get some credit from local traders and
other private persons, it is unreliable, costly, and totally inadequate for
generating a more productive agriculture. Commercial banks in the area are
not equipped to extend large numbers of small loans to farmers. The costs
of doing such business are prohibitative. Cooperative societies that have
extended credit to farmer members have failed more often than they have suc-
ceeded, partly because the inputs necessary to make enterprises productive
were not available, partly because no technical service or supervision ac-
companied the loan, and partly because of inadequate management of the co-
operatives themselves. In any case, sources of credit currently are totally
inadequate to serve the needs for an increasingly productive agricultural
economy.
Amounts of Credit Needed
3.        The credit system visualized for the project area would be limited
to providing farmers with specific inputs, namely fertilizers, insecticides,
seeds, sprayers, ox drawn equipment and a small amount of tractor equipment.
The estimated amounts of credit sales for each year of the 5-year project
period are:
Year 1    Year 2    Year 3    Year 4    Year 5
…-----------------  N    000  -…-----------------
Fertilizers           42.6     181.2      299.8     505.1      798.8
Insecticides, seeds,
and battery sets   38.4      100.3     229.7      354.7     570.7
Sprayers, oxdrawn
equirment and
tractor equipment  35.6       96.2      270.9     343.0      564.1
Total          116.6      377.7     800.4   1,202.8   1,933.6



ANNEX 8
Page 2
4.        Operating Procedures for GPMU Revolving Credit Fund.  Several operat-
ing procedures lend themselves to adoption by GPMU in so far as they would as-
sure good service to customers at reasonable cost. The following procedures
would be those which GPMU would employ initially and modify as required in the
course of the project's life. At first, lending would be limited to input
items carried by project farm service center stores; such lending would be in
amounts specified on a simple farm management plan approved by project's
Development Service AA. A simple form of loan application would be employed
which would serve as a farm management plan and promissory note at the same time.
5.        The Credit Assistant would calculate the farmer's credit require-
ments and after clearance on the borrower's credit worthiness from the village
head the borrower would be authorized to present his application to the vil-
lage service center. The items specified would be provided to the farmer and
the sale recorded by a bookkeeping machine. At market time the farm service
center storekeeper would make it as convenient as possible for the borrower
to repay his loan.
6.        Each borrower would have a laminated identity card containing his
picture, serial number (which would identify his village and hamlet) and
signature or mark for convenient identification and record keeping.
7.        Seasonal loans and two-year sprayer loans involving a total out-
standing credit of less than NIOO to borrowers in goodstanding would be
approved by Credit Assistants at the village level; attention being paid to
ensure that the time between loan application and the borrower actually ob-
taining his input items would be minimal. Larger loans and loans for oxdrawn
equipment would need to be approved by the relevant Credit Officer. Loans
of over N500 and all tractor loans would require the Credit Manager's specific
approval.
8.        GPMU would lend up to a maximum of N5,000 to any one borrower.  Larqer
borrowers would be directed to NAB or commercial banks.
9.        Group Obligation.  The procedures would provide that each borrower's
loan application be approved by the hamlet or village head, or a farmers'
association approved by the village authorities. Such approval would certify
to the applicants credit worthiness. Once each year, GPM4U would summarize all
accounts of the hamlet or village. If defaults were found, the headman would
be asked to assist in collections. GPMU and the CA would be empowered to
withbold lending to anyone in that village or hamlet until defaults are removed
unless due cause for default is presented and accepted by GPMU. The group
obligation would be a powerful influence to avoid default under social structure
pertaining to northern Nigeria.
10.       Provision would be made to extend credit to groups, or to village
farmers associations, if farmers wished to pool their needs for inputs and to
assume joint liability for the credit extended.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
Terrzs of Lending
11.       Lending terms to farmers would be established each year prior to the
upcoming season. Preferably these terms should be established well in advance,
and certainly no later than the preceeding November. The lending terms would
be determined by the GPMU management for approval by GPEC after consultation
with rele-yant Federal Authorities and the World Bank. Interest rates would
vary between 5-9-1/2% depending on the viability of the input package provided
to the borrowers. Most appropriately it should be applied as follows:
-    seasonal loans for farm inputs -- repayable within 12 months
at a flat service charge rate of 10%. No downpayments required;
-    sprayer loans repayable within two years in annual equal
installments at a flat service charge rate of 10% per annum.
No downpayment required;
-    oxdrawn equipment loans within three years in three annual
equal installments at a flat service charge rate of 10%
per annum. A 20% downpayment required;
-    tractors and tractor drawn equipment within four years in
four annual equal installments at a flat service charge
rate of 10% per annum. A 30% downpayment required.
12.       The above terms may differ at the time of implementation depending
on the economic situation prevailing at the time. However, on present fore-
casts and estimates, see farm budgets at Annex 1, the proposed crop improve-
ments and input packages could easily carry the terms proposed in para ll.
It is strongly recommended, for ease of administration and appreciation by
the borrowers, that a flat service rate made up of interest and services is
applied to all loans.
13.       During the implementation of the project, management and PKEU would
make a thorough study of various lending systems, such as cooperatives etc.,
that exist in the project area, with a view to making firm recommendations for
the conversion, in the fourth or fifth year of the project, of the revolving
credit fund into a form that can continue lending to the farmers after the
project development period is completed. It is therefore of great importance
that from the start of the project the revolving credit fund is operated on a
practical and commercial basis, and for this reason an illustrative cash flow
for the i n;'1 Js shown at Table 1 of this Annex.
