GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Evaluation
1.        Rural development projects of the size and scope of the Gusau project
can nave far reaching effects on the people in the project area and on the
economy of the State and the nation. The effects cannot be foreseen with
precision. Thus, an ongoing and thorough evaluation must be initiated and
pursued throughout the life of the project:
(a)  to guide project management;
,'
(b) to help establishment of project goals and scheduling of
project activities;
(c) to measure effectiveness and efficiency of project activities;
(d) to inform State and national governments and others of changes
stemming from project activities;
(e) to enhance knowledge of the rural development process for
future development planning; and
(f) to permit the continuous reappraisal of project goals and
assumptions underlying project design, particularly those
aspects affecting farm credit and input supply and to allow
for progressive modification of project objectives and
structures.
2.        Evaluation must include such standard economic criteria as change
in gross and net incomes of participants, changes in volume and value of
production, and measures of efficiency in use of project resources. It mu
include also such direct and indirect benefits as changes in efficiency and
effectiveness of the marketing system, changes in employment, changes in the
volume and type of business in the region, changes in tax revenues and ex-
penditures, and changes in other economic and social indicators that wo-uld
show direct and indirect results of project activities. Equally important
may be changes in patterns of consumption, in participation in education, in
health, and in other measures of well being of people in the project area.
3.        Precise types of measures to be made and specific methods of data
collection and analyses must be developed by the evaluation staff on site.
Some of the data needed would be generated by project records. Other data



ANNEX 9
Page 2
must be collected by surveys and from secondary sources outside the project
area. The proposed investment of substantial expertise and manpower in
developing and conducting surveys and analyses designed to measure meaning-
fully the parameters of rural development, as partially indicated in para-
graph 2, should generate considerable benefits, both for future planning in
Nigeria and for the general knowledge of the rural development process.
4.        If the broader impact of the project is to be assessed, there is no
alternative to undertaking baseline surveys before the effects of the project
begin to manifest themselves, and to continue the assessment of changes
brought about by project actions during and after the implementation period.
iqo organiztion or projRect_caa. prope~ry-evzuate i-If; and therefore evalu-
ation staff must be independent of project management in technical direction
and analysis methods; although of course they should work closely with proj-
ect staff for administrative purposes.
5.        In order to accomplish a meaningful evaluation, the Federal Ministry
of Agriculture and Natural Resources (FnANR) would establish a Project
Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU) at Kaduna.  PMEU would have a dual
function: (a) using existing Federal staff, suitably strengthened, it would
undertake a continuing technical and financial review of project actions so
as to satisfy FMANR that the project accomplishes its general objectives;
(b) by establishing a special evaluation section and employing specialist
consultants, it would undertake an economic and social evaluation as outlined
in paras 1-3.
6.        The evaluation section of PMEU would be set up under the World Bank
loan for the Funtua Agricultural Development Project and would be directed
by a "iH,ead" who would be responsible for technical and budget control of the
unit. Certain key staff would be employed by the unit to be responsible for
developing an evaluation program and for the analysis of the results from
Gusau project and from other projects established in northern Nigeria. Each
project evaluated would have its own field enumeration teams that would remain
ladministratively responsible to the respective projects.
7.        The ilonitoring section would be funded from ongoing FMAUNR budgetary
allocations and initially would Be hardly more than a one-man unit.  The
evaluation section would be funded by FMG from its own resources and a loan 1/
from the IJorld Bank. A special bank account would be maintained for the
evaluation section. The field enumeration units would be funded by the State
Government from its own resources and from a Bank loan.
1/   This loan would be made under the Loan for the Funtua Agricultural
Development Project.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
8.        The Federal Government has indicated that it would contract the
staffing of the evaluation section of PMEU to consultants. The consultants
would probably be a Nigerian university, in all likelihood the favorably
placed Ahmadu Bello University at Zaria. The consultants would be free to
draw upon the services of experienced individuals or possibly an outside
university to fill the posts provided for.
9.        Initially, the evaluation section of PMEU would monitor and evaluate
the Funtua, Gusau and Gombe Agricultural Development Projects, but as time
progresses, the evaluation section would be expanded to take in other projects
that might be developed, particularly as the State Governments would in every
case be responsible for administering the respective field enumeration units.
10.       The Gusau field evaluation unit would be made up of two enumeration
units each consisting of 1 economist, 4 supervisors and 20 enumerators (both
men znd women). Each unit would be responsible for approximately 600 samples,
the actual number of samples depending upon the degree of intensity of
sampling laid down by PMEU.  One senior evaluation officer based at projt
headquarters would be responsible for the project evaluation unit which would
comprise himself, his support staff and the 2 field enumerator units (see
table for costs).
11.       The evaluation section of PMEU would prepare a summary analysis
report at least once each year for each project, and such other special
reports as needed and when available information may suggest. The summary
report would be reviewed with the Project Manager and subsequently made
available to the Federal Coordinating Committee. A final summary appraisal
would be prepared for each project at the end of its first five years of
operation.
12        The project evaluation activities described in this annex would be
in addition to that which would be undertaken by each Project Manager as a
matter of routine to provide himself with performance and cost effectiveness
information necessary for good management and the achievement of project
goals.

ANNEX 12
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
MARKETING AND PRICES
A. Background Summary
1.        To date officially organized marketing in the northern States has
been confined to the so called cash crops, primarily cotton and groundnuts.
Arrangements for food crops have evolved through the private sector on a
laissez-faire basis with minimal assistance, or interference, from Federal
or State Governments. Producer prices for cash crops have been determined
by world levels (over which Nigeria has little influence) and the need to
generate Government revenue. Food prices have been determined by domestic
supply demand relationships in an essentially self sufficient environment
largely uninfluenced by wqorld levels. The basic conflict between the systems
has only recently become apparent as the price effect of increased food grain
demand has impacted on overall cropping patterns.
2.        The geographical extent of the country, difficulty of communication
and the widespread nature of basically subsistence oriented food crop produc-
tion has prevented the development of an integrated national grain-market.
Consequently there has grown up a multiplicity of small, unrelated markets;
these are probably rather efficient in themselves but they nevertheless give
rise to marked spatial and temporal price irregularities. In such an imper-
fect situation, inelasticity of market demand has a magnifying effect on price
movement and emphasises inadequacies in both structure and system. Prices
for food grains are now, however, sufficiently high to compete with tradi-
tional cash crops where labor constraints permit. Nevertheless there is
continued heavy private and state investment in agro-industry, particularly
seed crushing, and textile mills much of which will remain underutilized un-
less significant increases in marketed output of the traditional cash crops
are achieved.
3.        In order to improve the situation, increase relative cash crop
returns and overcome imperfections and inefficiencies in statutory marketing
systems, FMG has initiated a series of policy and organizational changes.
Producer prices are now Federal rather than State responsibility, taxes have
been reduced or abolished, and some means of fixing marketing overheads are
being introduced. To stimulate food grain production a form of price support
is planned operating through a national storage and marketing program. A
series of national grain production units (State owner and operated commercial
farms) is also contemplated. Detailed plans for the implementation of these



ANNEX 12
Page 2
policies have still to be announced. There is clearly considerably room,
and need, for improvements in marketing if proper benefits of better produc-
tion technology are to accrue to both producer and consumer; however recent
and current intercrop price relationships show the importance of an overall,
balanced approach if production of one set of crops is not to be unduly en-
hanced at the expense of others. Certainly the development of a medium term
minimum price policy, coupled with timely preplanting announcement of producer
prices for gazetted crops is badly needed.
B. Statutory Marketing Organizations
4.        Scheduled crops 1/ are marketed through statutory organizations con-
trolling a series of franchise operations - each of which may have opportuni-
ties to exploit a monopolistic position. Until recently the primary marketing
organization (NSNB) enjoyed the dominant role, coordinating the functions of
the various participants in the system (LBA, CAP, NPMC). FMG has now taken
over NPMC as part of the program to reorganize and improve marketing; 2/ NSMB
will now act only as purchasing agent for its erstwhile subsidiary, which will
continue to carry out the export marketing function.
5.        Producer prices for statutory crops have become a Federal responsi-
bility, recommendations being made to the Head of State by the Technical Com-
mittee on Produce Prices. NSNB will pass on produce to NPMC at a fixed price,
intended to take into account NSMB marketing costs and administrative over-
heads. These have still to be determined and agreed. NSMB operations are
extensive and the difficulties exacerbated by lack of communication facilities,
particularly feeder roads. Apart from obvious conseluent problems of crop
movement, this limitation also results in considerably hindrance to the exer-
cise of proper financial and operational control over all those involved in
produce marketing.
6.        Rural buying and first assembly are performed through a system of
licensed buying agents (LBA) and subagents. Quality control is the responsi-
bility of the Produce Inspection Department (PID) of the Ministry of Agri-
culture. Cotton is officially graded by Produce Examiners (PE) prior to
purchase at gazetted markets. Groundnuts are officially graded by Produce
Inspectors (PI) after purchase and on presentation at designated inspection
points. Check grading takes place when goods are moved into or from NSMB
storage points. LBA are required to arrange their own finance for purchases
and are paid (through discountable produce receipts) upon delivery to NSMB
depot. Despite this payment LBA remain responsible for "their" produce while
it is stored and until it is despatched to the consumer.
1/   In the project area cotton and groundnuts are the most important.
2/   The new policy aims at reducing the burden of taxation; reducing statutory
marketing overheads, transferring wqorld price developments more accurately
to producers.

8.        Export duty and produce sales tax have been abolished and replaced
by a single produce tax. This is set at 10% of producer price compared with
about 25% before the reform.
9.        World prices currently at a very high level are expected to decline
over the course of the project; the fixed nature of most marketing costs
that such a decline would be disproportionately reflected in resulting pr
ducer prices. It is therefore important that new policies and practices
result in the best possible relative return to farmers thereby increasing
production incentives at given crop values. To attempt to overcome the pro-
blem by arbitrary reduction of agency allowances, in reflection of individual
crop prosperity, would only result in the 'saving' being translated into a
direct or indirect reduction of the price actually paid to producers.
10.       Since the project would operate within the statutory marketing
system it is necessary to identify the deficiencies and outline ways in which
improvements may be made. This may be doubly important in view of the possi-
ble parallel development of a price support program for food grains.



ANNEX 12
Page 4
C. Marketing Policy and Organization
Fragmentation of Responsibility
11.       Absence of total marketing responsibility, particularly at primary
level, frustrates the emergence of corporate pride and wiholehearted effort
so important in an operation of this nature. The present system of split
responsibilities under wfhich PID and not NSMB are responsible for the conduct
of markets operated by NSMB agents, as well as aspects of produce quality,
if more divisive than cohesive. It appears to provide more opportunities for
abuse than for the imposition of independent checks and balances which it is
intended to do. Clearly, independent inspection is essential at a point or
points in the marketing chain, however the organization responsible for market-
ing should not only control its agents but should also have power to decide
whether or not produce is of sound marketable quality. It should then be in
a position to adequately control storage and accept the consequent risks.
Presently NSMB bears a number of risks without compensatory control. Possible
abuse of quality (and thus price) at primary level may be overcome partially
by inspection but mainly by proper competitive buying.
Late Announcement of Seasonal Guaranteed Prices
12.       In the past prices have seldom been announced prior to planting.
This has prevented firm price considerations from affecting planting decisions.
Furthermore, inter-seasonal world price variations reflect conditions over
*which Nigeria has little or no control or influence. Consequently the linking
of annual producer prices to projected xworld levels is likely to result in
magnified production fluctuations compared with a rather stable domestic
demand. A 'rolling' three to five year minimum price structure, related to
projected world prices, should be developed. This would encourage better
planning at farm level and assist achievement of greater inter-seasonal pro-
duction stability. Within this structure, minimum seasonal prices would be
announced before planting, and confirmed (or increased if justified) prior
to the opening of markets.
Lack of an Effective Crop Reporting and Market Intelligence System
13.       This prevents early assessment of crop sizes and dispositions and
eitiher prevents timely marketing decisions from being made, or causes them
to be inadequately based. The establishment of an intelligence service is
a long term undertaking, however, early and significant improvements could
be achieved by instituting a more widespread comparative two-way reporting
system among members of MA,Local Authorities, and NSMB staff, coordinated
at State and Federal level. It must be emphasized that communication needs
to be txwo way:  a field reporter should be kept informed of conditions out-
side his immediate area, if only to maintain his interest. The present
practice of keeping crop and wqeather reports under 'secret' cover is self
defeating. The development of an intelligence service is essential to im-
proving food grain marketing.



ANNEX 12
Page 5
D. Marketing Practices
The Licensed Buying Agent (LBA)
14.       The number of LBA is relatively small compared to the number of
agents and sub-agents directly or indirectly employed. Although open compe-
tition is officially encouraged it hardly exists except in areas directly
tributary to major highways. Transportation difficulties are very real and
noncompetitive practices result, with little encroachment by one LBA on the
'territory' of another. In general, LBA perform reasonably well, but the un-
scrupulous have not been adequately disciplined in the past; the monopolistic
positions achieved for individual LBA by a network of subagents and buyers
means that suspension or dismissal of a LBA, mid season, could result in farm-
ers being worse off through denial of outlets for their produce. Most of the
'normal' methods of cheating in produce marketing are employed at various
degrees at all levels of trade. These include underweighing and undergrading,
imposition of 'dash' payments for inspection services, degradation of gazetted
market services (thereby forcing sales elsewhere) etc. LBA and their agents
naturally do not confine their activities solely to produce, however the
advance of consumer credit at other times, for ultimate repayment with produce,
provides another means of 'buying' at less than gazetted levels. The fact
that produce payments by NSMB are not always prompt or assured, provides
constant encouragement for cheating to continue.
15.       Buying Seasons are over extended relative to volume purchased.  This
increases costs and further degrades competition through frequent absences of
buyers and PID staff.
16.       Movement and Storage particularly of groundnuts, is poorly planned
and executed. This is partially due to lack of crop information but also due
to system rigidities which prevent optimum use being made of the resources
and facilities of the local agro-industry. The continuation of LBA responsi-
bility for produce w.hile it is in NSMB depots or dumps adds unnecessarily to
storage costs.
E. Individual Crop Marketing
Cotton Marketing
17.       Cotton marketing is probably the most highly technical of all annual
crops and maximum cooperation among all involved in the industry is essential
if producer and consumer requirements are to be satisfactorily blended and
returns assured.



ANNEX 12
Page 6
18.       Recent experience shows that production is price sensitive; thus
the projected decline in world values makes it even more important that post
harvest and preplanting costs are reduced as much as possible. Ways of effec-
ting further savings need to be explored as well, and in this respect the
project should be able to make a significant contribution. The main defects
include:
(i) high seed waste and loss;
(ii) poor handling and storage techniques up to point of
ginning; and
(iii) absence of independent quality assessment of cotton lint.
Seed Wastage
19.       Planting seed is dressed and distributed to strategically sited
stores, where it is held before being issued free of charge. Apparent planting
rate is three to six times that recommended but seed is also used for other
purposes, such as fertilizer, and as an effective "smudge" fire against mos-
quitoes. Althiough theoretically free, cotton seed is traded in village markets,
sellers usually being those who live near a cottonseed store and have managed
to work a corner on local supplies. Cottonseed accounts for about 65% of the
weight and 35% of the total value of seed cotton; it costs about N 15.00 a
ton to dress and deliver to store, thus every ton of seed unnecessarily dressed
and distributed results in a marketing loss of about N 65.00. For 1973/74 the
total cost is probably in excess of N 1.3 million, or, in terms of producer
returns, rather more than 1 kobo/kg seed cotton, equivalent to 8% of the pro-
ducer price in the 1973/74 season. Part of the cause of over supply of plant-
ing seed (for which allowances have been made in arriving at the above figure)
is the relatively poor performance of fuzzy, i.e., undelinted seed. The
solution would seem to lie in progressively moving toward a situation where
only "improved" (i.e., delinted) seed is available and for which at least a
nominal price has to be paid by growers.
20.       By using acid delinting all costs of dressing would be saved (and
the mixers released for other seed treatment), empty seeds would be floated
off and high germination reasonably assured. After allowing a 30% weight loss
for delinting and seed selection, at least half of the customarily required
quantity of seed would thus become available for sale. Additional expense
would be incurred in packaging seed for sale in 10 kg bags, but this cost as
well as that of actual delinting would be more than compensated by the savings
in both seed and distribution costs. The project would be an excellent vehi-
cle for developing this approach, all seed being sold through service centers.
Estimated financial benefits would be about N 5.8 per ton of seed cotton.
The estimated cost of delinting, distributing and packaging is about 40 kobo
per 10 kg pack (enough for 0.5 ha) so the sale price could be relatively
nominal, however some charge must be made, and better seed performance demon-
strated, if the present widespread abuses are to be curbed and a greater pro-
portion of the real value of seed cotton realized. In discussions with CAP



ANNEX 12
Page 7
and its parent company, the principle of their installing acid delinting
plants during the project has been tentatively agreed, subject to suitable
arrangements being made concerning fees.
21.       Handling and storage of seed cotton:  After purchase, seed cotton
is packed in 100 lb bags and transported to dumps near the ginneries. From
there it is ultimately moved to the gin, being reweighed, unbagged and check
graded into the suction room. LBA remain responsible for the cotton they
have purchased until after it has been weighed into the gin, although provi-
sional payment will already have been made to them on purchased weights and
grades. There is little enthusiasm or incentive among LBA to ensure proper
storage in the dumps: spoilage of cotton through failure to provide stillage
is usual. PID control is sketchy and it is by no means unusual for LBA to
wet seed cotton before it is weighed into the gin in an attempt to increase
weight and/or overcome losses caused by natural shrinkage or other less
easily explained causes.
22.       Efficiency would be improved and losses reduced by providing fixed
(plinth) storage at the dumps and limiting responsibility of LBA to delivering
cotton to point of storage, from which time it would come under the charge of
ginners (CAP), until completion of the ginning process. Although no proce-
dural changes in marketing have yet been agreed, the project does provide for
the construction of proper seed cotton reception and storage facilities ad-
jacent to ginneries. Total cost of each storage depot (there would be three
constructed under the project) is estimated at N 94,000 including cost of a
weighbridge. The possibility of opening ginnery markets, for the purchase of
large lots, i.e., group marketed produce, needs to be investigated, as does
the feasibility of reducing handling by bulk movement of cotton from village
markets.
23.       Absence of independent lint quality assessment:  seed cotton is
graded on purchase and prior to ginning, and the lint is subsequently classi-
fied by CAP, for which service they receive a flat fee payment from NSNB.
The crop is then allocated to spinners taking account of their stated require-
ments, (prepared 12 months in advance) and classes actually available. No
selling samples are furnished nor in cases of dispute is there any provision
for independent quality determination, although these are customary trade
practices, and are performed on all export contracts. A partial consequence
is an almost total lack of cooperation within the industry, and while neither
side is blameless, the take it or leave it attitude of sellers does not con-
tribute to a greater degree of understanding. The manufacturers could con-
tribute to a greater degree of understanding, and could contribute considerably
to the well-being of the cotton industry through their technical and research
expertise and experience elsewhere, and it is important that a more coordi-
nated approach is adopted.
24.       The whole approach to cotton marketing is lack-lustre and far greater
enthusiasm needs to be generated if anticipated falls in world values are not
to make the crop unremunerative. Ginneries are well sited and could become



ANNEX 12
Page 8
catalysts for production instead of just processing agencies; the possible
development of much closer links between the State Government and CAP might
assist this. Greater participation by spinners in development could result
in competitive contract ginning: whatever does happen, marketing costs must
be brought into more reasonable relationship with the value of the crop.
25.       Cotton Ginning:  The ginning season extends for about 24 weeks from
December to June and ginning capacities are calculated on this basis. (Table
3.) These levels have been exceeded by up to 20% in exceptional years but
absence of covered storage for seed cotton makes extension of the season im-
practical. Additional facilities would be needed towards the end of the
project development period and management of CAP is willing to increase effec-
tive capacity as justified by sustained growth of production. Detailed ar-
rangement would be concluded by project management, MA, NSMB and CAP during
the development period. All ginning is done under contract with NSMB, the
present agreement expiring in 1976. Estimated break even is about 200,000
bales, after which fees are reduced; mean output over the last 13 years was
262,000 bales (249,000 omitting highest and lowest years).
Groundnut Marketing
26.       Groundnuts are purchased from farmers at buying points licensed by
the Local Authority, although considerable 'assembly buying' by subagents also
occurs outside licensed markets. Operations are not supervised and farmers,
particularly in areas remote from roads, are frequently cheated. Conversely,
the way in which LBA allowances are calculated results in strong competition
in areas near inspection stations and inducements in cash or kind may be
offered in order to attract offerings from farmers. All purchases are inspec-
ted and graded at PID supervised inspection stations before being moved into
NSMB depots where they are checked and stacked in pyramids. LBA remain res-
ponsible for stored nuts until they are despatched for export or to local
mills.
27.       Groundnuts should be moved as little as possible in order to minimize
percentage of "splits and brokens" which are more liable to aflatoxin infec-
tion and reduce the quality of a sample. Current practices could be Improved
and handling reduced, particularly now that local crushing capacity is begin-
ning to outstrip groundnut production. Much of the present overhandling is,
however due to lack of adequate early information concerning the size and
geographical disposition of the crop; until this situation is remedied un-
necessary marketing costs will continue to be incurred. Nevertheless the
establishment of depots adjacent to mills would cut out quite a large amount
of present handling and double transport. Consideration should also be given
to the establishment of depot markets to which Farmer Associations or other
similar marketing groups could deliver directly. This type of operation would
be well suited for development within the project since nuts could be assembled
and graded at a service center, and then either held to the order of NSMB
(storage charges being payable) or despatched to mill or exported.



ANNEX 12
Page 9
28.       Export marketing is carried out by NPMC, with local crushes being
allowed to buy at a level equivalent to world price. This price is determined
and fixed daily by NPMC and representatives of the oil millers, who are free
to make their own arrangements for the sale of oil and cake. Raw material
price movements are not usually immediately reflected in those of the products,
and vice versa; it can be argued that local millers have the put and take on
the market. This may be true to some extent, certainly a better overall price
could be realized if the marketing agency had selling options (i.e., nuts, oil
or cake or combinations thereof) always open to it. The possibilities and
potential ramifications of this should be investigated but, on the face of it,
benefits are likely to be small relative to the risks involved and the sophis-
tication of information and action required. Nevertheless, the projected
declines in world value through increasing competition from other sources of
oil and protein make it essential that marketing practices, and costs are
carefully examined and improvements made, wherever possible.
Mlarketing and the Project
29.       Trends of marketed production over the last decade combined with
recent food price developments properly reflect the failure of the marketing
systems, statutory and otherwise, to adequately transmit consumer needs to
producers. Relatively poor production performance is not solely the fault
of marketing - lack of education and associated physical inputs such as ferti-
lizer is also to blame - but the inescapable fact remains that for statutory
crops at least, farmers have not been offered enough incentive in terms of
price and/or service to encourage increased output. This failure of the
system has been heightened by shortcomings within the system which have led
to apparently increasing abuses and consequent accelerated producer disenchant-
ment. Under the project, steps would be taken to rectify this position; in-
deed, faith in the systems must be restored if projected benefits are to be
generated. To facilitate this transition maximum use would be made of the
physical facilities within the project together with some organizational anQ.
operational changes.
30.       Abuse and evasion of statutory marketing requirements by LBA ar
PID staff has been made easier by the small farmers basic lack of bargaii
power. The cooperative movement has been neither widespread nor effecti-
enough to alter this situation. Project staffed service centers would not
purchase products but would provide a focal point for farmers from which
they would draw advice and assistance and at which they would collectively
increase their bargaining power. The service center would become a place of
first assembly and storage from which, eventually, deliveries would be made
directly to domestic buyers or point of export. On such a basis the project
would make the following marketing improvements:



ANNEX 12
Page 10
Mi) Restoring or establishing producers faith in the system through:
(a) early announcement of producer prices;
(b) adequate control and discipline of marketing agents;
(c) enhanced competition at gazetted markets;
(d) provision of timely and accurate market intelligence;
(e) facilities for increasing individual farmers bargaining
power; and
(f) assistance in securing profitable outlets for new cash
crops, such as maize.
(ii) Developing a more disciplined approach to statutory marketing
by producers, thereby cutting costs; and
(iii) Investigating and developing new approaches in order to ensure
the producers as large a share as possible of ultimate product
value.
31.       Statutory Crop Purchasing.  LBA and their subagents in the project
area would be subject to approval by project management. Since project super-
visorv coverage of all sorts would be fairly intensive, abuses by LBA or their
agents should be quickly discovered and similarly dealt with.
32.       In order to ensure that crop purchases are not impeded through lack
of finance, arrangements would be made by NSMB (or NPMC) for working capital
requirements, as estimated by project management, to be advanced to LBA in
the project area.
33.       LBA should quickly realize that there is more to be lost than gained
by attempting to cheat farmers. Apart from much easier transport as a result
of the improved roads, as well as more easily obtained crop finance; LBA opera-
ting in the project area would be best placed to benefit from the immediate
economic consequences of increased production, not least would be back hauls
with farm inputs as other generally more attractive consumer goods.
34.       Project management would be given LBA status.  While it is not
intended that the project should carry out any crop purchasing, such an
appointment would enable sub-licensing, and therefore full audit control, of
emergent farmer or other associations wishing and competent to engage in group
marketing. Such a system would greatly ease the potential problems of train-
ing emergent marketers.



ANNEX 12
Page 11
35.       Fair buying practices would be encouraged through the careful siting
of markets. Initially these would be situated as jointly agreed between proj-
ect management, PID, NSMB, and appropriate local authority. Over the course
of the project gazetted markets would come to be sited only directly adjacent
to a servicing center. Actual purchasing would be carried out under the direct
supervision of the project employed PE or PI. Farmers would be encouraged to
weigh their produce on the dial or self recording scale prior to sale. The
principle of competitive servicing of producers would be reasserted and every
market would have at least two authorized buyers. Table 5 shows schematic
layout of a proposed center.
36.       Extended seasons add unnecessarily to marketing costs, reduced
supervision and competition, and make early assessment of marketable produc-
tion more difficult. After peak marketing periods, purchases would only be
made on set market days; in addition the possibility of shortening the market-
ing season would be investigated. Although this would result in greater flows
and increased peak cash requirements during certain months, the benefits of
having earlier control of the crop would outweigh these considerations.
37.      W4hile these steps should result in a considerable improvement in
farmers gross receipts for a given weight of produce, some of their possible
impact would be lost if they are not matched by an effective pricing policy.
It is essential that firm preplanting prices are widely announced.
38.       Food grains and other crops.  The facilities of the service center
would be available to Farmer Associations for assembly and short term storage;
difficulties over identity preservation would make it impractical to allow
similar use by individuals. Lack of accurate information is one of the major
constraints to improving food grain marketing. Under the project a market
intelligence system would be established to collect, disseminate and analyze
news about supply, demand and prices in various market centers. Services
would be centered on each Development Centre UJnit and coordinated by the
marketing section in Project Headquarters. Collection and dissemination in
the project area would be achieved through the Service Centers, while a
regular reporting system from major markets which could be supplied by project
production would be established in conjunction with MA.
39.       In addition to supervising the information systems, the marketing
section at project headquarters would also have prime responsibility for
market and marketing development. Market development would include, for ex-
ample, exploring and expanding the possibilities of securing profitable
outlets through some form of contract growing for, inter alia, the feed in-
dustry. It should also cover the identification of potentially profitable
'new' crops, such as confectionary groundnuts, and provide commercial guide-
lines for the research effort. Marketing development would be specifically
aimed at cutting costs or increasing values through such things as better crop
handling and storage techniques, contract processing, and the employment of a
generally more imaginative approach to marketing problems.



ANNEX 12
Page 12
G. Prices and Marketing of Maize, Sorghum and Cowpeas
Present Situation
40.       Traditionally Nigeria has been self sufficient in cereals other than
wheat. Sorghum and millet are the most important staple grains; consumption
of maize as grain occurs mainly in the southern and western states, although
this crop is now beginning to receive wider attention, particularly in the
North. Data on food production and consumption are inadequate and at best
provide only general indications of trends. Recent food crop production
figures underline however the significant role of the northern states as
cereal producers. Examination of other information 1/ indicates an insignifi-
cant trade in food grains, other than wheat.
41.       The situation of apparent self sufficiency has recently changed
into one where shortages persist and are rather marked in some areas. Re-
peated droughts and the effects of normal population changes have been com-
pounded by burgeoning urbanisation and income growth associated with economic
recovery, reconstruction and increased oil earnings. Consequently, food
prices have tended to increase steadily and in urban areas rose by 24% in
1970 and 21% in 1971. Some decline occurred in 1972 but the upward tendency
returned in 1973. Prior to recent sharp gains in world prices those in
Nigeria were double US farm prices for coarse grains, and three times world
level for rice.
42.       The effect of long distances on distribution costs is increased by
bottlenecks in the transportation system; as a result the spread between im-
port and export parities (already exaggerated by a 40% import duty) is too
big to enable world conditions to have any significant effect on domestic
price formation. Furthermore, the fragmented nature of production and, to
a certain extent, demand, difficulties of communication combined with lack
of market intelligence, make it impossible to speak of a Nigerian market per
se. Instead there exists a mass of relatively small market areas which appear
to exert little or no influence upon those adjacent.
43.       Staple food marlketing is almost exclusively confined to the private
sector although some official purchase, storage and distribution schemes are
run in efforts to relieve local shortages. A number of studies have con-
cluded that performance within the marketing system is rather efficient. This
may tend to mislead; while reported functional margins within study areas are
low, this cannot be taken as an indication of the efficiency of the system
itself in interpreting the needs of producers and consumers. Lack of cohesion
between market centers shows that it is the system which may be inadequate
regardless of any efficiency within it. While exploitive practices are prob-
ably being curbed by competition in individual markets, there is no reason to
suggest that a similar conclusion may be drawn in any broader context. In
fact the reverse is likely to apply.
1/   FAO Commodity Balance work sheets and Food Balance Sheet for 1968/69
computed for NADC.



ANNEX 12
Page 13
44.       Lack of communication between markets may be illustrated by com-
parison of officially reported monthly prices for sorghum at Daudawa and Zaria
in the North Central State. These markets are about 80 kilometers apart and
connected by a full width tarmac road and a railway, yet over a period of 42
months (up to July 1973), the price between them varied by as much as N 54.00
per ton. During the period, Zaria prices were higher by an average of N 26.47
per ton in 32 months and Daudawa prices higher by an average of N 30.00 in
three months (no prices were reported in one or other market for the remain-
ing seven months). Moreover, monthly price movements in the two markets were
in opposite directions in sixteen months. Prices within the markets varied
substantially from month to month with lowest prices not necessarily occurring
around harvest. Standard deviation from monthly mean prices was 24.03 in
Daudawa and 19.76 in Zaria. A similar situation was obtained for millet
(Table 7).
45.       This erratic price behavior is indicative of the very thin markets
that are to be expected under present circumstances. Food grains have not
yet become an accepted cash crop, as such, and most grain storage takes place
at farm level. Sales are made to meet requirements not provided for by 'con-
ventional' cash crops. Worthwhile market intelligence is unavailable and
price is extremely sensitive to slight changes in daily supply or demand. It
is not unknown for merchants to capitalise on this situation by attracting
supplies through paying high prices when offerings are limited, reducing them
sharply when the consequent increased supplies eventually reach the market.
This type of operation is assisted by the absence of adequate feeder roads
which effectively discourage most farmers from refusing a low price and taking
their grain back home. The overall picture is thus one of a rather passive,
inelastic and poorly informed system, in which market risks are taken more
by the farmer and consumer than by the merchant.
H. Future Developments, Markets and Prices
46.       As part of the policy of improving production and marketing and to
prevent a chronic supply deficiency situation from arising, FMG is developing
a scheme for the establishment of strategic grain storage and is encouragi
State food production farms; some form of price support program is also cc
templated. Details have not yet been announced (January 1974) but clearl:
this could usher in a completely new phase in Nigerian agriculture. Overall
pricing policies would have to be most carefully developed to match new pro-
duction techniques and ensure that capacity is most beneficially utilized.
Although the introduction of price support could go some way to overcoming
the inherent inelasticity of the food grain market, massive 'official' pro-
duction would at the same time inhibit or retard smallholder development.
In this context, and particularly in the light of expected incremental maize
production from the Funtua, Gombe and Gusau projects (117,000 tons), the
evolution of the feed and starch industries will provide an increasing 'spill-
way' for surplus food grain output.



ANNEX 12
Page 14
47.       There are a number of projections for food demand and supply for
Nigeria of which "A Quantitive Analysis of Food Requirements, Supplies and
Demand in Nigeria 1968-1985" is the most recent. The study is among the
most comprehensive, but the quality of the basic data is such that quoted
figures should be regarded as providing only indicative value. Demand pro-
jections have been based on the 1968/69 food balance sheet which in itself has
a number of shortcomings. Growth of population is estimated at 3% annua'lly
and that of per capita income 4%. Income elasticity of demand for maize,
sorghum and millet is estimated at 0.4, with cowpeas at 0.3. Food supply has
been projected on the assumption that past (estimated) rates of growth will
be maintained. These rates, and estimates derived from them, carry the same
degree of reliance as the relative estimates in the sample survey; projected
surplus/deficits should be regarded as broad orders of magnitude only (Table 8).
48.       Demand and supply for livestock feed have not been included in these
figures, nor has any provision been made for possible changes in diet (e.g.,
a move away from sorghum to maize and/or wheat based products). About 45,000
tons of maize was fed commercially to livestock (mainly poultry) in 1971 and
the study aspects consumption increase fairly dramatically, re-aching 106,000
tons in 1974, and over 375,000 tons in 1980. Demand has, however proved to be
rather more price elastic than anticipated and total production has remained
rather static in the face of sharp increases in raw materials costs. Unless
there is a significant and sustained reduction of prices relative to incomes
it seems unlikely that total 1980 demand for grain for (commercial) feed would
reach 65% of previously anticipated requirements.
49.       Comparison between demand and supply projections nevertheless
reveals a deficit for all crops except cowpeas. The surplus shown for this
crop is somewhat questionable: data are extremely fragmentary but the per-
sistent price trend indicates a shortage situation for the preferred varieties.
Demand also appears more elastic than for the staple grains which should
encourage steady increase in production. Although the projected deficiencies
are substantial it is important to differentiate between 'total demand' and
'effective market demand' when considering likely price developments. Allow-
ance must be made for the fact that despite current high prices, marketable
production and effective market demand are probably only very slightly out
of balance, when expressed in percentage terms. Any rapid increase in marketed
production without the benefit of a support program, could have a dramatic
effect on prices.
Maize and Sorghum
50.       In the north, maize is not yet an entirely acceptable dietary substi-
tute for either sorghum or millet. Research on this subject has been markedly
lacking, a most serious omission when the amount of production research and
development is considered. Limited subjective investigation revealed marked



ANNEX 12
Page 15
differences of opinion and preference both as to the possible part of maize
in the diet and the most desirable color (White maize is preferred in the
south). Being harder than sorghum maize is unpopular to process, both by
hand and by machine. The customary diesel powered light plate type provender
mill is not very suitable for maize and typical milling charges show a 30%
premium is required. Conversely, development of surplus production will be
relatively gradual, manufacturers are likely to continue keen, if not always
easily accessible buyers, and any periods of general food grain shortage
should tend to hasten the acceptance of 'new' tastes, particularly in urban
areas.
51.       Although import substitution is a convenient peg from which to hang
price formulation for food grains produced as cash crops, such a calculation
can only provide a broad indication. In fact, import parity may reasonably
be taken as a long term ceiling price for any significant quantities; however,
in the event of serious shortages it is unlikely that the consumer would be
penalized to the extent of the 40% import duty now imposed.
52.       Consideration has been given to the following factors in estimating
maize and sorghum prices:
(i) projected cost of imports;
(ii) relative production costs (4:5 maize:sorghum);
(iii)  the maize production encouragement that should be
generated by the starch and feed industries;
(iv) the established place of sorghum in the diet;
(v) relative inelasticity of market demand for food grains;
(vi) probable field and storage losses that farmers would
suffer through dealing with grains on a far larger scale
than previously. These are expected to be greater in the
case of maize, because of relatively larger production and
longer storage periods.
53.       The low value volume ratio of maize and sorghum compared to cotton
or groundnuts will tend to limit the radius within which profitable marketing
may occur, this could contribute to possible price instability. Because so
little information exists from which to determine probably ultimate centers
of market demand, an arbitrary 400 mile 'import' trunk haul to wholesale market
has been assumed. It has also been assumed that this market would be served
by local producers within a radius of 150 miles, first assembly being carried
out at a service center or equivalent. Prices are estimated as follows (see
Table 9):

Cowpeas
54.       Owing to lack of data an arbitrary price has been taken in a known
major market center (Ibadan).  This corresponds to the lowest post harvest
price for white cowpeas in recent years. Cowpeas are very susceptible to
pre and post harvest infestation, storage and fumigation procedures are in-
adequate at all levels so a 20% weight loss has been calculated. Prices are
estimated as follows (see Table 10):
N per metric ton
Economic                        99.00
Financial                        91.00
55.       In all calculations inland transport has been assumed to be by road
at rates quoted by local firms. On trunk routes rates for big articulated
vehicles or truck/trailer combines are fairly competitive with the railway
which is still heavily congested.
Cotton
56.       The projected incremental production of 70,000 tons seed cotton
(130,000 bales lint) from the Gusau (27,000 bales), Gombe and Funtua projects
represents only 0.2 percent of expected world consumption in 1980 and, even
if all exported, would have practically no impact on likely world trade. A
comprehensive survey of the local textile industry is presently being carried
out; however, it is provisionally estimated that 1980 requirements will reach
400,000 bales lint or 210,000 tons seed cotton. Earlier demand projections,
as made by CSNRD, FAO and others over 1966-68 put probable 1980 demand at
680,000 bales.
57.       The local industry is emerging from a substantial shake out which
arose as a result of liberalization of imports in 1971, duties having now
been reimposed. 1/ Nevertheless, a number of factors adversely affecting the
originally anticipated rate of expansion still apply; these include:
1/   Duties were removed in the 1971 budget in a successful attempt to curtail
profiteering by certain sections of the local industry. Official statis-
tics show that as a result, recorded 1971 imports reached the equivalent
of 9 months normal total supply.



ANNEX 12
Page 17
(i) real income development combined with inflationary rise in
living costs has reduced the scope of textile demand. This
has been accentuated by the recent price rises in cotton
(and synthetic) products in line with world market changes;
(ii) the changing pattern of demand towards suiting materials,
generally synthetic based, in the emerging consumer generation;
(iii) the recent inability of the industry to satisfy the demand
for better cloths and textures; and
(iv) competition from legal and illegal imports.
58.       Existing techniques do not permit the spinning of high counts from
present cotton varieties and this section of total demand would continue to
have to be met by imports for the foreseeable future. The projected decline
in world cotton prices will also increase the pressure of imports on local
industry. However, given recent performance in the face of factors already
mentioned, the provisional total demand of 400,000 bales in 1980 maybe some-
what conservative.

59.       With incremental production from the three projects totalling 70,000
tons, maintenance of recent production growth rates elsewhere would result in
a total crop of about 190,000 tons seed cotton in 1980. This is equivalent
to 370,000 bales lint, about 30,000 bales below anticipated demand. All in-
cremental production from the project area would thus be effective import
substitution.
60.       Investment in the seed crushing industry 1/ demands a seed supply of
around 120,000 tons, or the equivalent of 200,000 tons seed cotton after
allowing for provision of planting seed.  Most of the output of this industr,.
1/   A total of 370,000 tons dual purpose (groundnut and cottonseed) crushir;><
capacity exists or is in course of construction in 4 northern states.
Total seed crushing capacity is estimated at 950,000 tons.



ANNEX 12
Page 18
however, would be confined to the export-market; although there would be some
freight savings, and possibly slightly better oil recovery, the cotton-seed
price as a factor in producer price calculations is unlikely to improve above
levels for direct exports.
61.       The local textile industry needs to be oriented towards world
market levels if substantial subsidy and, or, penal import levels are to be
avoided. Conversely, during the past two years when higher producer taxes
were imposed, 'export parity' did not appear to provide sufficient inducement
to farmers who had a choice of cash crops. Consequently an extra charge of
2.5 K/Kg lint had to be made to spinners in order to permit producer prices
to be raised to incentive levels. This charge has now fallen away as a
consequence of the present very high world prices. (1973/74 season producer
prices are 13.2 K/Kg and 11.0 K/Kg for Grade I and II seed cotton respectively).
62.       It is not envisaged that competition from other crops would lessen
over the project period, indeed, such competition may well increase parti-
cularly from cereals. Estimated farmgate prices for seed cotton are as
follows (see Table 11):

Groundnuts
63.       Projected incremental production in the project areas (about 27,000
tons) would be less than the anticipated decline elsewhere if the trend of the
last decade is maintained. Indeed, if production is to meet the capacity of
local crushing facilities some 300,000 tons of increased marketed output would
be required. This would necessitate an overall production growth rate in ex-
cess of 8% per annum. 1/
64.       Nigeria is the largest exporter of nuts and the third largest pro-
ducer (after India and China). Servicing the needs of the local crushing
industry would have a dramatic effect on world export availabilities of whole
nuts. This is not however expected to have a similar effect on prices; pro-
duct values are far more influenced by availability and prices of competing
sources of protein and oils, such as soya beans, sunflowerseed etc. Although
the trend for protein feed prices is slightly upward, that for oil is static
to declining. As a result, world prices for groundnuts are projected to de-
cline from recent levels. Since by far the greater part of the output of
the crushing industry would be exported, farmgate prices are expected to
follow the world trend. Estimated farmgate prices are as follows (see Table
12).

ANNEX 13
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Benefits
1.        The annual costs and returns used in calculating the economic rate
of return are shown in Table 1.
2.        The following assumptions and methods have been made:
(a) Project life is assumed as 25 years. Most project
development activities will be completed by project
year 5 (1980), so a salvage value is added to benefits
in project year 6 to account for those houses and
buildings no longer required by the project and are
transferred to other Government agencies.
(b) Incremental production of crops and values of production
are shown in Table 2. The Bank Economic Analysis and
Projections Division's projections for 1980 have been used
as the basis for estimating import substitution and export
prices. Details of estimates are shown in Annex 12.
(c) Incremental benefits accruing to investments from oxdrawn
equipment and tractors have been calculated (Schedules 1
and 2). The total benefits from these investments are
offset against incremental labor costs, as shown in Table 3.
(d) Economic costs include adjustments for identifiable taxes,
and appropriate amendments to offshore costs to reflect a
shadow foreign exchange rate of US$1.27 per N1.00. Incremental
farm labor (Table 3) due to improved cropping practices has
been estimated at the shadow price of NO.40 per six-hour day
compared to the official minimum wage rate of NO.6 for the same
working period. Economic costs do not include project costs for
water development, soil conservation and project evaluation, as
benefits from them are intangible and cannot be calculated with
any accuracy.
3.        Using the above assumptions the economic rate of return is 16%.  A
sensitivity analysis has been carried out on a number of variables, the main
comparison being between shadow rated and non-shadow rated foreign exchange.
In each instance two cost streams have been used, one stream comprising of

project overhead costs, and the other farm inputs. The results of this
analysis is shown below:

ANNEX 13
Schedule 1
NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Returns to Farmers using Work Oxen
1.        Work oxen are traditionally used in land preparation work in
northern Nigeria. At present insufficient and poor quality equipment limits
the amount of land preparation done by oxen.
2.        Farmers owning work oxen would be able, through the project to
obtain credit for the purchase of ox-drawn equipment, including a plow, a
ridger, and a cart.
3.        The one-ha budgets for all the major crops show a heavy utilization
of labor for land preparation. In the one-ha maize budget, cultivation costs
using hand-labor are estimated to be between N30.40 and N34.80 dependent on
crop yields. This cost represents between 456 and 522 hours per ha assuming
a labor cost of NO.066 per man-hour.
Benefits
4.        The use of oxen would result in considerable savings in labor as
demonstrated below:
A.   Ox Time per Operating using 2 ox-teams
Plowing                 30 hours/ha
Cultivating             10 hours/ha
Ridging                 15 hours/ha
Total              55 hours/ha
B.   Area of Cultivation achievable per ox-team per annum: 4 ha
C.   Costs per ox-team
Oxen upkeep             N14.0 p.a.
Ox depreciation          20.0 p.a.
Subtotal           N34.0
Cost per ha             N 8.5
Labor cost per ha:
- using 1 driver
plus 1 laborer        N11.0
Total cost per ha       N19.5
Total cost per ha
using hand-labor      N33.0

Schedule 2
NIGERIA
NORTH WESTERN STATE
GUSAU AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROECT
Returns to Tractor Users
Tractors
1.        The Goverment operates a very uneconomic tractor hire unit within
the project area.
2.        Loan would be made available under the project to larger farmers
for tractors and equipment for use on their own farms and to individuals who
would provide contract tractor services.
3.        A farmer or contract tractor operator would be expected to provide
30Z of the total cost of a tractor unit.
4.        A tractor unit would include: a 65 hp tractor, a chisel plow, a
ridger, and a trailer. The total cost is estimated at N6,000. The farmer
or contract tractor operator would provide N1,800 In cash and a loan of
N4,200 would be rade available.
Tractor Bnef its
5.        Savings in labor can be calculated as follows:  Estimated hours of
hand labor cultivation required on one ha of maize to achieve a yield of
3,000 kg - 500 hours. Estimated tractor hours required to prepare 1 ha land
for maize: Plowing 2.5 hours; Cultivating 2.75 hours; Rldging 1.25 hours;
Weeding 3.50 hours; total 10.00 hours.
Tractor costs are estimated as follows:
Actual Cost         Economic Cost
Fuel                           NO.30/hour           N0.10/hour
Driver                          0.20/hour            0.20/hour
Repairs                         1 .00/hor            1 00/hour
MI1.50              11 .30
Add: Depreciation        N1.00                   -
Total hourly cost        N2.50/hour           N1.30
Total economic cost for land preparation for maize per ha - 10 x 1.3
Saving sbown by tractor per ha - N20.0
Minimum ha required to justify tractor - 70 ha
Annual minimum savings per tractor - N1,400
